Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
6 min
Definition and Nature of Qawaid Kulliyya (General Legal Maxims)
Defining qawaid fiqhiyya linguistically, terminologically, and distinguishing them from theories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qawaid fiqhiyya as predominant rulings, distinguish them from universal grammatical rules, and explain why they admit exceptions due to istihsan.

Core Idea
Qawaid fiqhiyya are hukm aghlabi: they apply to most cases but not all, with istihsan creating necessary exceptions.

In this lesson, we explore the definition and nature of general legal maxims (qawaid fiqhiyya). Starting from the linguistic meaning of 'foundation' and moving to the legal concept, you will see that a qa'ida is a concise, general principle that covers most particular cases. However, unlike grammatical rules, legal maxims are not universal; they are predominant (aghlabi) and often have exceptions due to the principle of istihsan (juristic preference). This understanding is crucial for applying maxims correctly in deriving rulings.

Guided Path
1
Define qa'ida linguistically and terminologically
Identify the root meaning of qa'ida as 'foundation' (e.g., the foundations of the Ka'ba). Then note the grammatical definition: a rule applying to all its particulars (e.g., 'the subject is raised').
Linguistic and grammatical definitions are foundational but differ from the legal definition.
2
State the legal definition
Restate the Hanafi definition: a qa'ida fiqhiyya is a predominant ruling (hukm aghlabi) that applies to most, not all, of its particulars.
The term 'aghlabi' is key; it is not muttariḍ (universal).
3
Examine the examples given
Consider the examples: 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are according to intentions) and 'al-asl baqa' ma kana' (the original state remains). Note how these are concise and cover many cases.
These examples illustrate the concise and general nature of qawaid.
4
Distinguish qawaid from nazariyyat (theories)
Explain that qawaid are concise principles (mabadi'/ضوابط), while nazariyyat form systematic subjects. A qa'ida may be a specific rule within a theory.
This distinction helps in understanding the scope and function of each.
5
Identify the aghlabi nature and the role of istihsan
Recognize that qawaid are not universal because they are based on qiyas (analogy), which is often overridden by istihsan in specific cases. This creates exceptions (istithna'at) to most maxims.
The aghlabi nature is a distinguishing feature of Hanafi qawaid.

١/٧٩ - القاعدة في اللغة هي أساس البيت ونحوه. وعليه قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذْ يَرْفَعُ إِبْرَاهِمُ الْقَوَاعِدَ مِنَ الْبَيْتِ وَإِسْمَعِيلُ ﴾ [سورة البقرة ٢/١٢٧]. (ر المصباح وغريب القرآن للسجستاني).
وفي اصطلاح النحاة هي: الضابط بمعنى الحكم المنطبق على جميع جزئياته، كقولهم : الفاعل مرفوع، والمفعول به منصوب.
أما في اصطلاح الفقهاء فالقاعدة هي: حكم أغلبي ينطبق على معظم جزئیاته (ر) حاشية الحموي على الأشباه الفن الأول تحت القاعدة الأولى) وذلك كقولهم: «الأمور بمقاصدها وقولهم: «الأصل بقاء ما كان على ما كان حتى يقوم الدليل على خلافه ويسمى أمثالها اليوم في الاصطلاح القانوني : «مبادىء» مبدأ principe.

الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية
٢/٧٩ - وقد سلفت الإشارة أول البحث في قسم النظريات الأساسية الكبرى إلى الفرق بين تلك النظريات والقواعد الكلية. فقد بينا هناك أنَّ هذه القواعد إنما هي مبادىء وضوابط فقهية يتضمن كل منها حكماً عاماً أما النظريات الأساسية التي عرضناها في ذلك القسم الثاني فتؤلفُ كل منها نظاماً موضوعياً في الفقه والتشريع. وقد تأتي القاعدة الكلية ضابطاً خاصاً بناحية من نواحي إحدى تلك النظريات، كما تقدم.
٣/٧٩ - وهذه القواعد الفقهية هي كما سبق أن قلنا : أحكام أغلبية غير مطّردة لأنها إنما تصوّر الفكرة الفقهية المبدئية التي تعبر عن المنهاج القياسي العام في حلول القضايا وترتيب أحكامها والقياس كثيراً ما ينخرم ويعدل عنه في بعض المسائل إلى حلول استحسانية استثنائية لمقتضيات خاصة بتلك المسائل، تجعل الحكم الاستثنائي فيها أحسن وأقرب إلى مقاصد الشريعة في تحقيق العدالة وجلب المصالح ودرء المفاسد، ودفع الحرج، كما تقدم إيضاحه في بحث الاستحسان.
ولذلك كانت تلك القواعد الفقهية قلما تخلو إحداها من مستثنيات في فروع الأحكام التطبيقية خارجة عنها، إذ يرى الفقهاء أن تلك الفروع المستثناة من القاعدة هي أحكاماً استحسانية خاصة .
قال مهذب فروق القرافي نقلاً عن العلامة الأمير: «من المعلوم أن أليق بالتخريج على قاعدة أخرى، أو أنها تستدعي
(۱) ألفاظ العموم في اصطلاح علم أصول الفقه هي الألفاظ الموضوعة لغة للدلالة بصيغتها أو بمعناها على أفراد كثيرة غير محصورة على سبيل الاستغراق كلفظة (المؤمنون) في قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّمَا الْمُؤْمِنُونَ إِخْوَةٌ ﴾ لأن صيغة الجمع المعرف تعم، وكلفظ «القوم والرهط، ومن، وما فإن معناها يقع على الجمع وإن كان لفظها مفرداً. وأنواع ألفاظ العموم مبينة في بحث العام والخاص من كتب أصول الفقه.
Chapter Seventy-Nine: Meaning of the Universal Maxims and Their Jurisprudential Status. First Section: Meaning of the Universal Maxims. 1/79 - The linguistic meaning of al-qa'ida is the foundation of a house and the like. Based on this is the saying of the Exalted: 'And [mention] when Ibrahim was raising the foundations of the House, and Isma'il' [Surah al-Baqarah 2:127]. (See al-Misbah and Gharib al-Qur'an by al-Sijistani). In grammatical terminology, it is: the rule, meaning the ruling that applies to all its particulars, such as their saying: 'The subject is raised, and the object is accusative.' As for the legal terminology, al-qa'ida is: a predominant ruling that applies to most of its particulars (see Hashiyat al-Hamawi 'ala al-Ashbah, first section under the first maxim). For example, their saying: 'Matters are according to their intentions,' and their saying: 'The original state remains as it was until evidence establishes otherwise.' These are called today in legal terminology 'principles' (principe). We can define it more clearly and precisely as: Legal maxims are general jurisprudential fundamentals in concise constitutional texts containing legislative rulings for events that fall under their subject. They are characterized by extreme conciseness in their formulation while being general in meaning and wide in coverage of particular branches. The maxim is usually formulated in two or a few words. 2/79 - Reference was made earlier at the beginning of the discussion in the section on major fundamental theories to the difference between those theories and universal maxims. We explained there that these maxims are jurisprudential principles and rules, each containing a general ruling. As for the fundamental theories presented in that second section, each forms a systematic subject in jurisprudence and legislation. A universal maxim may serve as a specific rule for an aspect of one of those theories, as previously mentioned. 3/79 - These jurisprudential maxims are, as we said before: predominant rulings that are not universal, because they represent the basic jurisprudential idea that expresses the general analogical methodology in solving issues and organizing their rulings. Analogy is often broken and deviated from in some issues to exceptional istihsani (juristic preference) solutions due to particular requirements of those issues, making the exceptional ruling better and closer to the objectives of Sharia in achieving justice, bringing benefits, repelling harms, and removing hardship, as was explained earlier in the discussion of istihsan. Therefore, these jurisprudential maxims rarely lack exceptions in the applied rulings of branches that fall outside them, as the jurists consider those branches excepted from the maxim to be specific istihsani rulings. Al-Muhadhdhab al-Furuq by al-Qarafi, quoting al-'Allama al-Amir: 'It is known that...' (Footnote: Alfaz al-'umum in usul al-fiqh terminology are words that are linguistically designated to indicate by their form or meaning a large number of individuals without limitation in an inclusive manner, such as the word 'al-mu'minun' in His saying: 'Indeed, the believers are brothers' because the definite plural form indicates generality, and such as the words 'al-qawm', 'al-rahṭ', 'man', and 'ma' because their meaning applies to a group even if their form is singular. The types of general words are explained in the discussion of 'amm and khass in usul al-fiqh books.)
Source pages p. 900

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the nature of qawaid kulliyya (general legal maxims) in Hanafi jurisprudence. The author begins by giving the linguistic meaning of qa'ida: the foundation of a building, citing the Quranic verse about Ibrahim raising the foundations of the Ka'ba. Then he mentions the grammatical usage: a rule that applies to all its particulars (e.g., 'the subject is raised'). The legal definition differs: a qa'ida fiqhiyya is a hukm aghlabi (predominant ruling) that applies to most, not all, of its particulars. Examples include 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are according to intentions) and 'al-asl baqa' ma kana 'ala ma kana' (the original state remains as it was). The author distinguishes qawaid from fundamental theories (nazariyyat): qawaid are concise principles, while theories form systematic subjects. He emphasizes that qawaid are aghlabiyya (predominant) not muttarida (universal), because they are based on qiyas (analogy), which is often set aside in favor of istihsan (juristic preference) for special cases. This creates exceptions (istithna'at) to every qa'ida. The footnote explains 'alfaz al-'umum' (words of generality) in usul al-fiqh, which is relevant to understanding the scope of maxims.

Key Terms
القاعدة (al-qa'ida): حكم أغلبي ينطبق على معظم جزئياته (a predominant ruling applying to most of its particulars)
الحكم الأغلبي (al-hukm al-aghlabi): الحكم الذي ينطبق على الأكثر لا الكل (the ruling that applies to the majority, not all)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the nature of qawaid fiqhiyya in terms of their applicability?
A) They apply to all particular cases without exception.
B) They apply to most (aghlab) but not all cases, and often have exceptions.
C) They are specific rulings for individual events.
D) They are only used in ibadat (worship) and not in mu'amalat (transactions).

Key Takeaway
Qawaid fiqhiyya are aghlabi (predominant) rulings that cover most cases but admit exceptions through istihsan, making them flexible legal tools.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

The Nature and Status of General Legal Maxims
Definition and explanation of the nature and role of legal maxims in fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi legal maxims are described as 'majority-based' and state the ruling of the Majalla regarding their use in adjudication.

Core Idea
General legal maxims are majority-based guidelines with many exceptions; they illuminate fiqh but cannot by themselves justify a judge's verdict.

General legal maxims (al-qawaid al-kulliyyah) are foundational principles in Hanafi fiqh that encapsulate broad rulings applicable to numerous branches. However, as this passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa clarifies, they are not absolute—they are majority-based, with frequent exceptions arising from istihsan (juristic preference). This lesson unpacks their nature, their value in unifying fiqh, and why they cannot serve as standalone texts for judicial decisions.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish general maxims from basic theories
Identify the difference: maxims are individual principles containing a general ruling, whereas theories are comprehensive subject systems. Maxims often act as specific controls within one theory.
This sets the stage for understanding the specific role of maxims.
2
Understand the majority-based nature
Grasp that maxims represent the general analogical method, but istihsan (juristic preference) creates exceptions. Hence, maxims are aghlaabiyyah (majority-based), not muttaridah (universal).
The text quotes al-Qarafi affirming this.
3
Recognize the consequence for adjudication
The Majalla prohibited judges from relying solely on maxims; they must also cite a specific or general text covering the case. Maxims are for tafqih (understanding) not for qada (judgment) alone.
Maxims have many exceptions, so they are not decisive.
4
Appreciate the value despite exceptions
Despite being non-absolute, maxims brilliantly depict general principles, reveal theoretical paths, and unify scattered branches by showing common ratios (manaah). Without them, rulings would be chaotic.
They are indispensable for systematic fiqh thought.
5
Connect to Usul al-Fiqh
Notice the final quote: Shari'ah includes usul (principles)—one type is Usul al-Fiqh (rules of words). Maxims complement these by providing substantive guidelines for derivation.
Maxims are not a separate source but a tool for jurists.

قال مهذب فروق القرافي نقلاً عن العلامة الأمير: «من المعلوم أن أكثر قواعد الفقه أغلبية (ر ) تهذيب الفروق تحت الفرق الثاني جا ص ٣٦).

ومن ثم لم تسوّغ المجلة أن يقتصر القضاة في أحكامهم على الاستناد إلى شيء من هذه القواعد الكلية فقط دون نص آخر خاص أو عام يشمل الحادثة المقضي فيها، لأنَّ تلك القواعد الكلية، على مالها من قيمة بعمومه واعتبار هي كثيرة المستثنيات فهي دساتير للتفقيه لا نصوص للقضاء. ر : المقالة الأولى من مقدمة المجلة).

المطلب الثاني :
مكانة هذه القواعد وموقعها من أصول الشريعة

٤/٧٩ - وكون هذه القواعد أغلبية لا يغضُ من قيمتها العلمية وعظيم موقعها في الفقه وقوة أثرها في التفقيه، فإن في هذه القواعد تصويراً بارعاً وتنويراً رائعاً للمبادىء والمقررات الفقهية العامة، وكشفاً لآفاقها ومسالكها النظرية وضبطاً لفروع الأحكام العملية بضوابط تبين في كل زمرة من هذه الفروع وحدة المناط، ووجهة الارتباط برابطة تجمعها وإن اختلفت موضوعاتها وأبوابها .

ولولا هذه القواعد لبقيت الأحكام الفقهية فروعاً مُشَيَّتةً قد تتعارض ظواهرها دون أصول تمسك بها في الأفكار وتبرز فيها العلل الجامعة، وتعين اتجاهاتها التشريعية وتمهد بينها طريق المقايسة والمجانسة .

الناشئة
يقول العلامة الشهاب القرافي في مقدمة كتاب «الفروق»:
إن الشريعة المحمدية اشتملت على أصول وفروع. وأصولها قسمان:
- أحدهما المسمى «أصول الفقه»، وأغلب مباحثه في قواعد الأحكام الألفاظ، كدلالة الأمر على الوجوب، ودلالة النهي على التحريم، وصيغ الخصوص والعموم وما يتصل بذلك كالنسخ والترجيح.
عن
٩٦٧
Chapter Ten: General Legal Maxims

(1) From the general words in fiqh

2/79 - Reference has already been made at the beginning of the section on major basic theories to the difference between those theories and general maxims. We explained there that these maxims are only principles and fiqh controls, each containing a general rule, whereas the basic theories presented in that second section each form a subject system in fiqh and legislation. And a general maxim may come as a specific control for one aspect of one of those theories, as previously mentioned.

3/79 - These fiqh maxims are, as we have said, majority-based rulings that are not universal because they only represent the initial fiqh idea that expresses the general analogical method in solving issues and arranging their rulings, and analogy is often breached and deviated from in some issues towards exceptional istihsani solutions due to special considerations in those issues, making the exceptional ruling therein better and closer to the objectives of Shari'ah in achieving justice, bringing benefits, repelling harms, and removing hardship, as previously explained in the discussion of istihsan.

Therefore, these fiqh maxims rarely lack exceptions in the applied branches of rulings outside them, since the jurists consider that those branches excluded from the maxim are specific istihsani rulings that are more suitable to be derived from another maxim, or they require [special consideration].

Al-Muhadhdhab of Furq al-Qarafi said, quoting from al-Allamah al-Amir: “It is known that most fiqh maxims are majority-based.” (Tahdhib al-Furuq under the second difference, vol. 1, p. 36)

Hence, the Majalla did not permit judges to rely solely on any of these general maxims in their judgments without another specific or general text covering the incident being judged, because these general maxims, despite their value in their generality and consideration, have many exceptions; they are constitutions for fiqh understanding, not texts for adjudication. (See: First article of the introduction of Majalla).

Second Section:
The Status of These Maxim and Their Position vis-à-vis the Usul of Shari'ah

4/79 - And the fact that these maxims are majority-based does not diminish their scientific value, their great status in fiqh, and the strong impact in fiqh understanding, for in these maxims there is a brilliant depiction and a wonderful illumination of general fiqh principles and determinations, and a revealing of their horizons and theoretical paths, and a control of the branches of practical rulings by controls that show in each group of these branches the unity of the ratio, and the point of connection with a bond that combines them even if their topics and chapters differ.

Were it not for these maxims, the fiqh rulings would remain scattered branches whose apparent aspects might conflict without principles to hold onto in ideas, and without revealing the unifying causes, determining their legislative directions, and paving the way for comparison and harmonization between them.

Al-Allamah al-Shihab al-Qarafi said in the introduction of his book “al-Furuq”:
“Indeed, the Muhammadan Shari'ah includes usul and furu. Its usul are of two types:
- One called ‘Usul al-Fiqh’, most of its discussions are about the rules of words, such as the indication of a command for obligation, and the indication of a prohibition for prohibition, and the forms of particularity and generality and what is connected to that like abrogation and preference."
from
967
Source pages p. 901

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the nature and status of general legal maxims (al-qawaid al-kulliyyah) from Mustafa al-Zarqa's work. The author begins by distinguishing general maxims from larger basic theories: maxims are individual principles containing a general ruling, whereas theories form subject systems. He then emphasizes that these maxims are 'majority-based' (aghlaabiyyah) and not absolute (mutarridah), meaning they apply to most cases but have exceptions. These exceptions arise due to istihsan (juristic preference), which allows deviation from qiyas (analogy) when necessary to fulfill Shari'ah objectives like justice and harm prevention. The author quotes al-Qarafi to affirm that most fiqh maxims are majority-based. Consequently, the Ottoman Majalla (civil code) prohibited judges from relying solely on these maxims; they must also cite a specific or general text covering the case, because maxims are constitutions for ijtihad, not texts for adjudication. In the second section, al-Zarqa defends the value of these maxims despite their non-absolute nature: they brilliantly illuminate general principles, reveal theoretical paths, and control the branches of rulings by showing the common ratio (manaah) among diverse issues. Without them, rulings would remain scattered and contradictory. Finally, he quotes al-Qarafi's classification of Shari'ah sources into Usul al-Fiqh (rules of words) and other principles, setting the stage for the study of maxims as a tool for jurists.

Key Terms
القواعد الكلية (al-qawaid al-kulliyyah): General legal maxims that contain a broad ruling applicable to many branches of fiqh, but are not absolute due to exceptions.
أغلبية (aghlaabiyyah): Describes rulings that apply to the majority of cases but have exceptions, as opposed to universal rulings.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference to deviate from qiyas for a specific case due to a stronger evidence or maslahah.
ألفاظ العموم (alfaz al-umum): Words that indicate generality (e.g., collective nouns, all-inclusive terms) in Usul al-Fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the Majalla prohibit judges from relying solely on general legal maxims (al-qawaid al-kulliyyah) in their judgments?
A) Because maxims are too general to be useful.
B) Because maxims are majority-based and have numerous exceptions.
C) Because the Majalla did not recognize maxims at all.
D) Because maxims are derived from analogy which is unreliable.

Key Takeaway
General legal maxims are fundamental but non-absolute; they require contextualization and exceptions, making them tools for ijtihad not direct texts for judgment.

Meaning and Status of Comprehensive Legal Maxims
Explaining the nature and importance of qawa'id kulliyya in fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qawa'id kulliyya, explain their aghlabiyya nature and limitations, and apply the guideline that they require textual support for judicial verdicts.

Core Idea
Qawa'id kulliyya are majority-based maxims that unify fiqh, but they require textual support for adjudication.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa defines the nature of comprehensive legal maxims (qawa'id kulliyya) and clarifies their role in fiqh. You will learn that they are powerful tools for understanding fiqh (tafqih) but are not absolute—they admit many exceptions. The Ottoman Majalla therefore restricted their use in court, demanding a textual basis. This lesson helps you use maxims correctly in fatwa and ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the aghlabiyya nature
Identify that most qawa'id are aghlabiyya (majority-based), meaning they apply to most cases but not all. They have numerous exceptions.
This is an epistemological distinction: maxims are probabilistic, not certain.
2
Understand the Majalla's limitation
Note that the Majalla forbade judges from relying solely on qawa'id without a specific or general text covering the case. Reason: maxims have many exceptions and are not legislative texts.
This protects against arbitrary application.
3
Identify the benefits of qawa'id
List benefits: they unify scattered rulings, reveal common rationales (manat), reduce memorization, and clarify legislative directions.
These benefits make qawa'id essential for tafqih.
4
Distinguish from Usul al-Fiqh
Compare qawa'id kulliyya with Usul al-Fiqh as per al-Qarafi: Usul deals with linguistic rules (commands, prohibitions, generality), while qawa'id are substantive maxims covering many branches.
They complement each other.
5
Apply the takeaway to fatwa practice
As a mufti, use maxims as guiding principles but always verify textual sources and check for exceptions before issuing a fatwa.
This prevents error.

الفصل (۷۹) معنى القواعد الكلية ومكانتها الفقهية
أكثر قواعد الفقه أغلبية (ر ) تهذيب الفروق تحت الفرق الثاني جا ص ٣٦).
ومن ثم لم تسوّغ المجلة أن يقتصر القضاة في أحكامهم على الاستناد إلى شيء من هذه القواعد الكلية فقط دون نص آخر خاص أو عام يشمل الحادثة المقضي فيها، لأنَّ تلك القواعد الكلية، على مالها من قيمة بعمومه واعتبار هي كثيرة المستثنيات فهي دساتير للتفقيه لا نصوص للقضاء. ر : المقالة الأولى من مقدمة المجلة).
المطلب الثاني :
مكانة هذه القواعد وموقعها من أصول الشريعة
٤/٧٩ - وكون هذه القواعد أغلبية لا يغضُ من قيمتها العلمية وعظيم موقعها في الفقه وقوة أثرها في التفقيه، فإن في هذه القواعد تصويراً بارعاً وتنويراً رائعاً للمبادىء والمقررات الفقهية العامة، وكشفاً لآفاقها ومسالكها النظرية وضبطاً لفروع الأحكام العملية بضوابط تبين في كل زمرة من هذه الفروع وحدة المناط، ووجهة الارتباط برابطة تجمعها وإن اختلفت موضوعاتها وأبوابها .
ولولا هذه القواعد لبقيت الأحكام الفقهية فروعاً مُشَيَّتةً قد تتعارض ظواهرها دون أصول تمسك بها في الأفكار وتبرز فيها العلل الجامعة، وتعين اتجاهاتها التشريعية وتمهد بينها طريق المقايسة والمجانسة .
الناشئة
يقول العلامة الشهاب القرافي في مقدمة كتاب «الفروق»:
إن الشريعة المحمدية اشتملت على أصول وفروع. وأصولها قسمان:
- أحدهما المسمى «أصول الفقه»، وأغلب مباحثه في قواعد الأحكام الألفاظ، كدلالة الأمر على الوجوب، ودلالة النهي على التحريم، وصيغ الخصوص والعموم وما يتصل بذلك كالنسخ والترجيح.
عن

الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية في الفقه
- والثاني هو القواعد الكلية الفقهية وهي جليلة كثيرة لها من فروع الأحكام ما لا يحصى وهذه القواعد لم يذكر منها شيء في أصول الفقه،
وقد يُشار إليها هناك على سبيل الإجمال.
وهذه القواعد مهمة في الفقه عظيمة النفع، وبقدر الإحاطة بها يعظم قدر الفقيه، وتَتَّضح له مناهج الفتوى ومن أخذ بالفروع الجزئية دون القواعد الكلية تناقضت عليه تلك الفروع واضطربت واحتاج إلى حفظ جزئيات لا تتناهي.
ومن ضَبَطَ الفقة بقواعده استغنى عن حفظ أكثر الجزئيات لاندراجها
في الكليات، وتناسب عنده ما تضارب عند غيره اهـ ملخصاً.
٩٦٨
Chapter (79): Meaning of Comprehensive Maxims and Their Jurisprudential Status.
Most of the jurisprudential maxims are majority-based (see Tahdhib al-Furuq under al-Farq al-Thani, p. 36). Hence, the Majalla did not permit judges in their rulings to rely solely on any of these comprehensive maxims without another specific or general text that covers the incident being adjudicated, because these comprehensive maxims, despite their value in generality and significance, have many exceptions; they are constitutions for fiqh comprehension, not texts for adjudication. (See the first article of the Introduction to the Majalla).
Second Subsection: Status of these Maxims and Their Position Relative to the Principles of Sharia.
4/79 - And the fact that these maxims are majority-based does not detract from their scientific value, their great position in fiqh, and the strength of their impact on figh comprehension. For in these maxims there is a brilliant portrayal and a wonderful illumination of general fiqh principles and determinations, a disclosure of their horizons and theoretical paths, and a regulation of the branches of practical rulings with controls that show in each group of these branches the unity of the rationale (manat) and the point of connection with a unifying bond, even if their subjects and chapters differ. Without these maxims, the fiqh rulings would remain scattered branches whose apparent aspects might conflict, lacking principles to grasp in thought, to highlight the unifying causes, to determine their legislative directions, and to pave the way between them for analogy and comparison.
The eminent scholar al-Shihab al-Qarafi says in the introduction to his book 'al-Furuq': Indeed, the Muhammadan Sharia includes principles and branches. Its principles are of two types: One is called 'Usul al-Fiqh', and most of its discussions are about the rules of the meanings of words, such as the indication of a command for obligation, the indication of a prohibition for prohibition, and the forms of specificity and generality and what connects to that like abrogation and preference.
Chapter Ten: The Comprehensive Maxims in Fiqh
The second is the comprehensive jurisprudential maxims; they are magnificent and numerous, with countless branches of rulings. These maxims are not mentioned at all in Usul al-Fiqh, though they may be alluded to there briefly. These maxims are important in fiqh, of great benefit. The more one is versed in them, the greater the faqih's stature becomes, and the paths of fatwa become clear to him. Whoever takes only individual branches without the comprehensive maxims will find those branches contradictory and confused, and will need to memorize endless particulars. Whoever masters fiqh through its maxims becomes independent of memorizing most particulars because they fall under the universals, and what seemed contradictory to others becomes harmonious for him. End, summarized.
Source pages p. 902

Commentary
This passage defines the nature and role of al-qawa'id al-kulliyya (comprehensive legal maxims) in Hanafi fiqh and Islamic jurisprudence generally. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by noting that most such maxims are 'aghlabiyya' (majority-based, not absolute). This means they admit exceptions. Consequently, the Ottoman Majalla (the Hanafi civil code) did not allow judges to base their verdicts solely on these maxims without reference to a specific textual source – because maxims are broad guidelines for interpretation (dasaatir lil-tafqih), not legislative texts for adjudication (nusus lil-qada'). This is a critical practical limitation: a mufti or judge must always check for relevant texts before applying a maxim.

Next, the author emphasises that this majoritarian nature does not diminish the maxims' value. On the contrary, they serve vital functions: they brilliantly portray general principles, illuminate theoretical aspects, and regulate the vast corpus of fiqh rulings by identifying common rationales (manat) linking diverse cases across different chapters. Without them, fiqh would be a chaotic collection of contradictory cases. The passage then quotes Imam al-Qarafi (a Maliki scholar, but his point is universally applicable) who distinguishes two types of principles in Sharia: (1) Usul al-Fiqh, dealing with linguistic rules (e.g., commands indicate obligation), and (2) the qawa'id kulliyya fiqhiyya, which are substantive legal maxims (like 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha,' etc.) that cover countless branches. These maxims are not systematically covered in Usul al-Fiqh works.

The passage concludes by extolling the benefit of mastering these maxims: they allow a faqih to transcend contradictions, reduce the need to memorize endless particulars (since particulars fall under universals), and bring clarity to fatwa methodology. This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on systematic reasoning and the use of qawa'id as an essential tool for ijtihad and fatwa.

Key Terms
القواعد الكلية (al-qawa'id al-kulliyya): Comprehensive legal maxims that cover many branches of fiqh under a unifying principle.
التفقيه (al-tafqih): The process of understanding and teaching fiqh in a principled manner, as opposed to mere memorization.
المجلة (al-Majalla): The Ottoman Majalla, a codification of Hanafi fiqh in civil law (1876).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why did the Majalla not permit judges to rely solely on qawa'id kulliyya in their verdicts?
A) Because qawa'id kulliyya are not derived from the Quran and Sunnah.
B) Because qawa'id kulliyya are aghlabiyya and have many exceptions, so a specific textual basis is needed.
C) Because qawa'id kulliyya were developed by later scholars and lack authority.
D) Because judges are not qualified to interpret qawa'id kulliyya.

Key Takeaway
Qawa'id kulliyya are indispensable for tafqih but must be applied alongside textual sources due to their exception-ridden nature.

The Role of General Fiqh Maxims in Ifta
Introduction to general fiqh maxims and their importance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the importance of general legal maxims (al-qawa'id al-kulliyya al-fiqhiyya) for a mufti and distinguish them from usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Mastering the general maxims makes one a true faqih, while memorizing only particulars leads to confusion.

This passage introduces the second category of fiqh rules: the general legal maxims. The author emphasizes their immense value for the jurist and warns against relying solely on specific branches. Understanding these maxims is essential for issuing consistent and sound fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of rules
The passage distinguishes between usul al-fiqh and general fiqh maxims. Note that the maxims are not systematically covered in usul al-fiqh.
Usul al-fiqh deals with sources and methods, while qawa'id are substantive principles that cluster rulings.
2
Recognize the benefit of maxims
Mastery of maxims elevates the jurist's rank and clarifies the methodology of issuing fatwas.
Maxims provide a framework for deductive reasoning in fatwa.
3
Understand the warning
A jurist who relies only on specific cases will face contradictions and need to memorize endless details.
This highlights the practical necessity of maxims for consistency.
4
Appreciate the summary
Whoever masters fiqh through its maxims is freed from memorizing most particulars, as they are subsumed under general rules.
Maxims create coherence and reduce memory burden.

Chapter Ten: General Rules in Fiqh - And the second type is the general fiqh maxims, and they are noble and numerous, having countless branches of rulings. Nothing of these maxims is mentioned in Usul al-Fiqh, though they may be alluded to there in summary. These maxims are important in fiqh, of great benefit; to the extent of mastery over them, the jurist's rank becomes great, and the methods of issuing fatwas become clear to him. Whoever relies on partial branches without the general maxims, those branches become contradictory and confused for him, and he needs to memorize endless particulars. Whoever masters fiqh through its maxims is freed from memorizing most particulars because they are subsumed under the general rules, and what appears contradictory to others becomes harmonious for him. End, summarized.
Source pages p. 903

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the second category of rules in fiqh: the general legal maxims (al-qawa'id al-kulliyya al-fiqhiyya). The author distinguishes them from usul al-fiqh rules, noting that these maxims are not systematically discussed in usul al-fiqh. He then highlights their immense benefit: they elevate the jurist's rank, clarify the methodology of fatwa, and prevent contradictions that arise from relying solely on specific cases. A jurist who grasps these maxims can deduce rulings for countless particulars without memorizing them individually, because the particulars fall under the general rules. This passage encourages the student to study and internalize these maxims as a foundation for ijtihad and iftaa.

Key Terms
القواعد الكلية الفقهية (al-qawa'id al-kulliyya al-fiqhiyya): General legal maxims that encompass many specific fiqh rulings under a single principle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
beginner
10 min
Historical Origins of the Five Cardinal Legal Maxims
Historical survey of qawa'id codification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five major legal maxims and trace their earliest Hanafi compilations from al-Dabbas to al-Karkhi to Ibn Nujaym.

Core Idea
The five cardinal maxims were codified by early Hanafi masters as foundational principles for fatwa.

This lesson situates the five major legal maxims historically. You will learn how these maxims, which govern vast areas of fiqh, were first collected and refined by Hanafi jurists from the 3rd to 10th centuries AH. Understanding this history gives you the pedigree of each maxim and its authority in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five maxims
List the five maxims in Arabic and English: al-umur bi-maqasidiha (matters are determined by intentions), al-darar yuzal (harm must be removed), al-'adah muhakkamah (custom is authoritative), al-yaqin la yazul bi al-shakk (certainty is not overruled by doubt), al-mashaqqah tajlib al-taysir (hardship begets facility).
These are known as al-qawa'id al-kulliyyah al-khams.
2
Trace the chain of transmission
The oldest surviving collection is al-Karkhi's (37 maxims), which built upon the earlier collection of al-Dabbas (17 maxims). This shows a cumulative process.
Al-Karkhi and al-Dabbas were contemporaries in the late 3rd/early 4th century AH.
3
Distinguish maxims from usul al-fiqh
The passage emphasizes that qawa'id are substantive rules governing fiqh branches, whereas usul al-fiqh is the methodology for deriving rulings from texts.
This distinction is critical for a mufti's approach to novel cases.
4
Note subsequent contributions
Al-Dabusi (Ta'sis al-Nazar) expanded the genre with comparative fiqh controls, and Ibn Nujaym (al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir) compiled 25 maxims classified into two types.
Ibn Nujaym's work is the standard Hanafi reference for qawa'id.
5
Evaluate cross-madhhab recognition
A Shafi'i poem versifies the same five maxims, indicating their acceptance beyond the Hanafi school.
This universality reinforces their authority in fatwa.

الفصل (۸۰) لمحة تاريخية عن صياغة القواعد الكلية
بعض هذه القواعد (۱) ، ومن جملتها القواعد الخمس التي تعتبر أمهات القواعد ومباني الأحكام الشرعية من نصية واجتهادية، وهي:
١ - الأمور بمقاصدها ۲ - الضّررُ يُزال ۳ - العادةُ مُحَكَمةٌ ٤ - اليقين يزول بالشك ٥ - المشقة تجلب التيسير
خاصة
(۲)
٤/٨٠ ـ وأقدم مجموعة من مجموعة من هذه القواعد الكلية وصلت إلينا في شكل رسالة هي قواعد الإمام أبي الحسن الكرخي، وقد شرحها وأوضحها بالأمثلة الإمام نجم الدين أبو حفص عمر النَّسَفي الحنفي المتوفى سنة ٥٣٧ للهجرة (۳) والظاهر أن الكرخي قد أخذ القواعد التي جمعها أبو طاهر الدباس وأضاف إليها، فقد جاءت مجموعة الكرخي بسبع وثلاثين قاعدة، بينما رأينا آنفاً فيما رواه ابن نجيم أن القواعد التي جمعها الإمام الدباس كانت سبع عشرة .
(۱) يروي ابن نجيم قصة هذه الرحلة والنقل بصورة مستبعدة لا تصح تفاصيلها. ولكن الظاهر أن لها أصلاً ثابتاً، إذ رواها الجلال السيوطي الشافعي في كتابه «الأشباه والنظائر (ص/ ٤ - ٥ ) ، والكمال ابن الهمام الحنفي في فتح القدير».
ر: مقدمة الأشباه لابن نجيم وحاشية الحموي عليها).
(۲) وقد نظمها بعض الشافعية مشيراً إلى أساسيتها في مذهبهم أيضاً بقوله : خمس مقررة قواعد مذهب للشافعي فكن بهن خبيرا ضرر يزال، وعادة قد حكمت وكذا المشقة تجلب التيسيرا والشك لا ترفع به متیقناً والقصد أخلص إن أردت أجورا
(۳) الإمامان الكرخي والدباس كانا متعاصرين، وكلاهما من أكابر فقهاء الطبقات العليا في المذهب الحنفي، ولكن الكرخي أكثر شهرة وذكراً. فالكرخي: هو الإمام أبو الحسن عبيد الله بن الحسين الكرخي، نسبة إلى كرخ العراق ولد سنة / ٢٦٠ هـ، وتوفي سنة / ٣٤٠ هـ، وانتهت إليه رئاسة المذهب الحنفي.

والدباس : هو الإمام أبو طاهر محمد بن محمد الدباس كان إمام أهل الرأي في العراق حفاظاً خبيرا بالروايات ومن أقران الكرخي. وقد ولي القضاء بالشام. (ر: الفوائد البهية في تراجم الحنفية للشيخ عبد الحي اللكنوي ص / ۱۰۹ و ۱۸۷).

الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية في الفقه
ويرى الناظر في قواعد الإمام الكرخي هذه أن بعضاً منها ليس من قبيل القواعد بالمعنى الذي حددناه للقاعدة، وإنما هو من قبيل الأفكار التوجيهية لرجال المذهب في تعليل المسائل، كقول الكرخي مثلاً فيها : «الأصل: أنَّ كل آية تخالف قول أصحابنا فإنها تحمل على النسخ أو على الترجيح أو على التأويل من جهة التوفيق».
ونستخلص من ذلك أن هذه القواعد وهي مفاهيم ومبادىء فقهية کبری ضابطة لموضوعاتها قد بدأت حركة تقعيدها وتدوينها أواخر القرن الثالث الهجري وأنَّ معانيها الفقهية كانت مقررة لدى الأئمة المجتهدين تعتبر أصولاً عملية لهم يقيسون بها ويبنون عليها ويعللون بها، وإن كانت لم تُفرد بالتدوين قبل ذلك، ولم تأخذ الصياغة التي انتهت إليها فيما بعد إلا بالصقل والتحوير وهي غير أصول الفقه التي هي علم يقرر الطريقة العلمية في تفسير النصوص وفهمها والاستنباط منها.
٥/٨٠ - ثم جاء الإمام أبو زيد عبيد الله بن عمر الدبوسي الحنفي، فوضع كتابه «تأسيس النظر وضَمَّنه طائفة هامة من الضوابط الفقهية الخاصة بموضوع معين، ومن القواعد الكلية التفريع عليها (۱). مع
٦/٨٠ - ثم جاء أخيراً العلامة زين العابدين إبراهيم بن نجيم المصري المتوفى سنة ٩٧٠هـ فجمع في الفن الأول من كتابه «الأشباه والنظائر) خمساً وعشرين قاعدة، وصنفها إلى نوعين :
(1) الدبوسي من أكابر فقهاء المذهب الحنفي كان يُضرب به المثل في النظر والاحتجاج الفقهي. وذكر ابن خلكان أنه أول من وضع علم خلاف الفقهاء. والمراد بعلم خلاف الفقهاء ما نسميه اليوم في اصطلاح علم الحقوق الحديث باسم : الفقه المقارن». فالدبوسي رحمه الله أول من عني بتنظيم البحث في الفقه المقارن بين المذاهب وجعل منه علماً مستقلاً.
وكتابه هذا، تأسيس النظر مطبوع مع قواعد الإمام الكرخي في المطبعة الأدبية بمصر.
۹۷۲
Chapter (80): A Historical Glimpse at the Formulation of the Universal Maxims Among these maxims (1), and among them the five that are considered the mothers of the maxims and the foundations of legal rulings, both text-based and ijtihadi: 1. Matters are determined by their objectives; 2. Harm must be removed; 3. Custom is authoritative; 4. Certainty is not overruled by doubt; 5. Hardship begets facility. [End of page 906] (2) 4/80 – The oldest collection of these universal maxims that has reached us in the form of a treatise is the Maxims of Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi, which were explained and clarified with examples by Imam Najm al-Din Abu Hafs Umar al-Nasafi al-Hanafi, who died in 537 AH (3). It appears that al-Karkhi took the maxims collected by Abu Tahir al-Dabbas and added to them; indeed al-Karkhi's collection contained thirty-seven maxims, whereas we saw earlier in what Ibn Nujaym narrated that the maxims collected by Imam al-Dabbas were seventeen. (1) Ibn Nujaym narrates the story of this transmission in an improbable manner whose details are not sound. But it appears that it has a solid basis, as it was narrated by Jalal al-Suyuti al-Shafi'i in his book al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir (pp. 4-5), and by Kamal Ibn al-Humam al-Hanafi in Fath al-Qadir. (See: Introduction to al-Ashbah by Ibn Nujaym and its gloss by al-Humawi). (2) Some Shafi'is versified it, indicating its fundamentality in their school as well, saying: Five established maxims of the school of al-Shafi'i, so be well acquainted with them: Harm is removed, custom is ruled, and likewise hardship brings ease; doubt does not remove certainty, and intention is sincere if you desire reward. (3) The two Imams al-Karkhi and al-Dabbas were contemporaries, both among the leading jurists of the highest ranks in the Hanafi school, but al-Karkhi is more famous and renowned. Al-Karkhi: He is Imam Abu al-Hasan Ubaydullah ibn al-Husayn al-Karkhi, relative to Karkh in Iraq; born in 260 AH, died in 340 AH, and the leadership of the Hanafi school ended with him. And al-Dabbas: He is Imam Abu Tahir Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-Dabbas; he was the imam of the people of opinion in Iraq, a memorizer and expert in narrations, and a peer of al-Karkhi. He served as judge in Syria. (See: al-Fawa'id al-Bahiyyah on the biographies of the Hanafis by Shaykh Abd al-Hayy al-Laknawi, pp. 109 and 187). [Page 907] Chapter Ten: The Universal Maxims in Fiqh And the observer of these maxims of Imam al-Karkhi sees that some of them are not of the category of maxims in the sense we have defined for a maxim, but rather are of the category of directive ideas for the scholars of the school in the rationalization of issues, such as al-Karkhi's statement therein: 'The principle: Every verse that contradicts the statement of our companions is to be interpreted as abrogated, or as giving preponderance, or as interpretively reconciled.' And we deduce from this that these maxims, which are major jurisprudential concepts and principles governing their subjects, began their movement of formulation and codification at the end of the third century AH, and that their jurisprudential meanings were established among the mujtahid imams, considered as operative principles for them by which they analogize, build, and rationalize, even if they were not individually codified before that, and did not take the formulation they later reached except through refinement and modification. And they are distinct from usul al-fiqh, which is a science that establishes the scientific method for interpreting texts, understanding them, and deriving rulings from them. 5/80 – Then came Imam Abu Zayd Ubaydullah ibn Umar al-Dabusi al-Hanafi, who wrote his book Ta'sis al-Nazar and included in it an important group of jurisprudential controls specific to a particular subject, and of universal maxims for deriving rulings (1). 6/80 – Then finally came the scholar Zayn al-Abidin Ibrahim ibn Nujaym al-Misri, who died in 970 AH, and he collected in the first art of his book al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir twenty-five maxims, and classified them into two types: (1) Al-Dabusi was among the leading jurists of the Hanafi school; he was a model for legal reasoning and argumentation. Ibn Khallikan mentioned that he was the first to establish the science of juristic disagreement. What is meant by the science of juristic disagreement is what we today call in modern legal terminology: comparative fiqh. So al-Dabusi, may God have mercy on him, was the first to take care of organizing research in comparative fiqh among the schools and making it an independent science. And his book Ta'sis al-Nazar is printed together with the Maxims of Imam al-Karkhi in the Literary Press in Egypt.
Source pages p. 906

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview of the codification of the major legal maxims (القواعد الكلية) in the Hanafi school. It begins by listing the five cardinal maxims that serve as the foundations of Islamic legal rulings, both from textual sources and ijtihad. These five are widely recognized across schools of law. The passage then traces the earliest known collection, which is the Qawa'id of Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi (d. 340 AH), later explained by al-Nasafi. Al-Karkhi is said to have built upon the earlier collection of Abu Tahir al-Dabbas, which had seventeen maxims, expanding it to thirty-seven. The author notes that some of al-Karkhi's maxims are not maxims in the strict sense but rather directive principles for legal reasoning, such as the rule about interpreting Qur'anic verses that conflict with Hanafi positions as abrogated or subject to interpretation. This indicates that the movement of formulating maxims began in the late third century AH, and these concepts were already used by early mujtahids. However, they were not formally compiled until later. The author distinguishes these maxims from usul al-fiqh, which is a methodology for deriving rulings. Next, the passage mentions the contribution of Imam al-Dabusi (d. 430 AH) through his work Ta'sis al-Nazar, which contained important jurisprudential controls and universal maxims. Finally, it discusses the work of Zayn al-Abidin Ibn Nujaym (d. 970 AH), who in the first part of his al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir collected twenty-five maxims and classified them into two types. The footnotes also provide biographical details about al-Karkhi, al-Dabbas, and al-Dabusi, as well as a poetic summary of the five maxims from a Shafi'i perspective. This historical survey highlights the gradual development and formalization of qawaid kulliyya within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
القواعد الكلية (al-qawa'id al-kulliyyah): Universal legal maxims that are comprehensive rules governing many particular cases; they serve as foundations for deriving rulings and are distinct from specific legal principles.
الكرخي (al-Karkhi): Imam Abu al-Hasan Ubaydullah ibn al-Husayn al-Karkhi (d. 340 AH), a leading Hanafi jurist who compiled one of the earliest collections of qawa'id.
الدبوسي (al-Dabusi): Imam Abu Zayd Ubaydullah ibn Umar al-Dabusi (d. 430 AH), a Hanafi jurist known for his work Ta'sis al-Nazar, which contains legal controls and comparative fiqh.
الأشباه والنظائر (al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir): A genre of fiqh literature that collects similar cases and legal maxims; Ibn Nujaym's work is a major Hanafi reference in this field.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The five major legal maxims were systematically collected by al-Karkhi, building on al-Dabbas, and later refined by al-Dabusi and Ibn Nujaym, forming a core of Hanafi fatwa methodology.

Classification of the Majalla's Rules into Basic and Subsidiary
Classifying the Majalla's rules into basic and derivative categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between basic (asasiyya) and subsidiary (far'iyya) legal maxims and explain why the Majalla's rules require such classification.

Core Idea
The Majalla's qawa'id must be sorted into basic and subsidiary to resolve overlap and ensure proper application.

This passage critiques prior works on legal maxims and introduces a necessary classification of the Majalla's general rules. The author notes that the Majalla Commission listed the rules without order, causing synonymy and overlap. Therefore, he divides them into two types: basic rules that are independent principles, and subsidiary rules derived from them. Understanding this classification is crucial for correctly applying the Majalla's maxims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Recognize that the Majalla's rules were originally compiled without organization, leading to overlap and synonymy.
2
Define basic rules (qawa'id asasiyya)
Each basic rule is an independent principle that is not derived from a more general rule. It stands on its own.
Example from the text: the rule 'إعمال الكلام أولى من إهماله' (Giving effect to speech is better than neglecting it) is basic.
3
Define subsidiary rules (qawa'id far'iyya)
Subsidiary rules are derived from basic rules and are more specific applications.
Example: 'الأصل في الكلام الحقيقة' (The original state in speech is literal meaning) is a subsidiary rule derived from the above basic rule.
4
Apply the classification
Using the author's example, classify the three rules about speech into basic and subsidiary, and verify the derivation.
Articles 60, 12, 61 are related but scattered; recognizing their relationship clarifies their hierarchy.

Chapter Ten: The General Rules in Fiqh. However, this book, despite its great value and what it contains of investigations not previously undertaken, did not gather rules in the sense we mean here. Rather, by 'rules' it means the basic rulings in major juristic topics. So it presents these basic rulings for every two similar topics and clarifies the differences between them, saying for example: The difference between the rules of insha' and khabar, and the difference between the rule of what ignorance and gharar affect and the rule of what they do not affect among (dispositions), and (the difference between the rules of granting ownership of benefit and granting the right to benefit, and the difference between the rules of verbal custom and actual custom), and so on. Thus it intends by 'rule' the meaning of basic rulings in a specific topic, not the technical meaning we have explained for it. However, the book 'al-Furuq' has scattered in its chapters many constitutional juristic rules, dispersed on occasions of explaining rulings and establishing controls. C - And the book of Ibn Rajab al-Hanbali built its discussions on one hundred and sixty rules and concluded with twenty-one benefits. It likewise places under the heading 'rule' a juristic topic and then addresses it with extensive explanation and admirable detail. It is a book of great value, containing a juristic wealth beyond description. The author of Kashf al-Zunun described it as 'one of the wonders'. But in any case, it is not a collection of juristic rules with general constitutional texts in the previously mentioned sense. 976:
Chapter Eighty-One: Presentation of the rules brought by the Majalla, arranged and classified into basic and subsidiary, with a very brief explanation. 1/81: Indeed, the rules of the Majalla, which are the goal of our research in this last section of the book, are all general rules with technical legislative formulation in the previously mentioned sense. However, some of them contain a degree of synonymy or overlap with others. Hence it was necessary to classify them into two types: 1. The basic rules, each of which is an independent principle not derived from a more general rule. 2. The subsidiary rules derived from those basic rules. And the Majalla Commission did not classify these rules, nor did it observe proportionality and coherence in their presentation; rather, it listed them in an unorganized sequence in which similar or overlapping rules in meaning and subject became scattered and distant. For example, we find: - In Article 60 the rule: 'Giving effect to speech is better than neglecting it.' - And in Article 12 of the Majalla the rule: 'The original state in speech is literal meaning.' - And in Article 61 the rule: 'If the literal meaning is impossible, recourse is made to metaphor.' 977
Source pages p. 911

Commentary
This passage is a critical review of prior works on legal maxims and an introduction to the author's own classification of the Majalla's rules. The author first distinguishes the approach of the book 'al-Furuq' and Ibn Rajab's work from his own conception of 'general rules' (qawaid kulliya). He notes that those works deal with basic rulings on specific topics, not the comprehensive constitutional maxims he intends. Then, in Chapter Eighty-One, he turns to the Majalla's rules, which he considers true general rules with legislative formulation. However, he points out a flaw: the Majalla's compilers did not organize the rules logically; they simply listed them, causing overlap and redundancy. Therefore, he classifies them into basic rules (independent principles) and subsidiary rules (derived from basics). He gives an example: the three rules about speech (Articles 60, 12, 61) are related but scattered. This classification is essential for proper understanding and application of the Majalla's maxims.

Key Terms
قواعد أساسية (qawa'id asasiyya): Basic rules that are independent principles, not derived from a more general rule.
قواعد فرعية (qawa'id far'iyya): Subsidiary rules derived from the basic rules.
القواعد الكلية (al-qawa'id al-kulliyya): General juristic rules that have a legislative formulation and serve as overarching principles in fiqh.
المجلة (al-Majalla): The Ottoman civil code (Majalla al-Ahkam al-'Adliyya) that codified Hanafi fiqh rules.

The Maxim 'Actions Are Judged by Intentions'
Explaining the first major qawida with its sub-rule and applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' to determine the legal ruling of an act when the intention is known.

Core Idea
The ruling of an act changes based on the intention behind it.

This passage introduces the first general maxim of Islamic jurisprudence: 'Actions are judged by intentions.' The author explains that the legal outcome of any action depends on the actor's intention, providing examples from homicide, financial transactions, and lost property. Understanding this maxim is fundamental for a mufti, as it underlies many rulings in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim
Locate the Arabic text of the maxim in the passage: 'الأمور بمقاصدها'. Its English translation is 'Actions are judged by intentions.'
2
Understand its meaning
The maxim means that the legal rulings attached to actions vary according to the intention of the doer. The passage gives examples: intentional vs. mistaken homicide, gift vs. loan, and finder as trustee vs. usurper.
3
Apply to the homicide example
If someone kills intentionally without justification, the ruling is different (e.g., qisas or diyah) than if the killing was accidental (only diyah, no qisas).
Intention distinguishes murder from manslaughter.
4
Apply to the loan/gift example
If someone says 'Take these dirhams' intending charity, it is a gift; if intending return, it is a loan. The ruling on liability differs.
The same words can have different legal effects based on intention.
5
Apply to the lost property example
If a person picks up a lost item intending to keep it, they are a usurper (liable). If intending to preserve and return it, they are a trustee (not liable if it perishes without fault).
Intention determines the legal status of the finder.

الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية في الفقه
٤/٨١ - القاعدة الأولى: «الأمور بمقاصدها» (المجلة (۲) .
أي إنَّ أعمال الشخص وتصرفاته من قولية أو فعلية تختلف نتائجها وأحكامها الشرعية التي تترتب عليها باختلاف مقصود الشخص من تلك الأعمال والتصرفات : - فمن قتل غيره بلا مسوّغ مشروع إذا كان عامداً فلفعله حكم، وإذا كان مخطئاً فله حكم آخر .
ومن قال لآخر: خذ هذه الدراهم، فإن نوى التبرع كان هبة وإلا كان قرضاً واجب الإعادة .

Chapter Ten: The General Maxims in Fiqh. 4/81 - The First Maxim: 'Actions are judged by intentions' (Majalla (2)). That is, a person's actions and dispositions, whether verbal or physical, differ in their results and legal rulings that attach to them according to the difference of the person's purpose in those actions and dispositions. So whoever kills another without legitimate justification, if he was intentional, his act has a ruling, and if he was mistaken, it has another ruling. And whoever says to another: 'Take these dirhams,' if he intended donation, it is a gift, otherwise it is a loan obligatory to return. And whoever picks up a lost item with the intention of taking it for himself is a usurper, but if he picks it up with the intention of preserving it, announcing it, and returning it to its owner when he appears, he is a trustee; so he is not liable if it perishes without his transgression or negligence in preserving it, and so on. What branches off from it of rules: 5/81 - Consideration in contracts is for purposes and meanings, not for words and structures (Majalla / 3) (1). So bay' al-wafa' (sale with right of redemption) applies the rulings of several contracts, most prominently the rulings of pledge (rahn), because that is the purpose of the two contracting parties in bay' al-wafa'. And a gift (hiba) if compensation is stipulated in it, as when one says to another: 'I gift you this thing for such and such' or 'on condition that you give me such and such,' the contract takes the rulings of sale because it has become in its meaning, despite the contracting party using the word gift; so the gifted item is returned for defect, and likewise the donee recovers the paid consideration if the gifted item is claimed (3) from his hand, and so on for all rulings of sale. (1) It is mentioned in the book al-Mabsut by al-Sarakhsi (23/22) his statement: '...because consideration in contracts is for meanings, not for words.' So this formulation of the maxim was established in the Hanafi madhhab before the era of Imam al-Sarakhsi, even to the point that it was used as a basis. (2) The meaning of istihqaq (claim/right) has preceded, so refer to (F 2/37 footnote). And suretyship (kafala) if it is stipulated in it that the creditor may not claim the principal debtor (the guaranteed), it becomes a transfer of debt (hawala) and takes its rulings, because it becomes in its meaning; likewise, hawala if it is stipulated in it that the creditor has the right to claim both the transferor debtor and the transferee together, it becomes kafala (see F 5/46-6).
Source pages p. 915

Commentary
This passage introduces the first major legal maxim: 'الأمور بمقاصدها' (Actions are judged by intentions). The author explains that the legal outcome of any act depends on the intention behind it, giving examples from homicide (intentional vs. mistaken), loan vs. gift, and lost property (finder as trustee vs. usurper). He then presents a sub-rule that branches from this maxim: 'العبرة في العقود للمقاصد والمعاني لا للألفاظ والمباني' (Consideration in contracts is for purposes and meanings, not for words and structures). This sub-rule is a cornerstone of Hanafi contract law. The author supports it by quoting al-Sarakhsi's al-Mabsut. Then he applies the sub-rule to specific contracts: bay' al-wafa' (sale with repurchase), where the underlying purpose is a pledge (rahn) despite the sale wording; a gift with compensation, which is treated as a sale; suretyship (kafala) that becomes a transfer of debt (hawala) if the creditor cannot claim the original debtor; and hawala that becomes kafala if the creditor can claim both parties. These applications show how the maxim governs practical fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
المقاصد (al-maqasid): Intentions and purposes behind actions and contracts
بيع الوفاء (bay' al-wafa'): A sale where the seller retains the right to repurchase, functioning effectively as a pledge

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Finder (multaqit)
D) Borrower (musta'ir)

Key Takeaway
The legal outcome of any act depends on the intention behind it; this maxim is a fundamental tool for a mufti to discern the true nature of transactions.

Sub-Rule: Consideration in Contracts Is for Purposes
Explaining the first major qawida with its sub-rule and applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the sub-rule 'al-ibra fi al-'uqud li al-ma'ani' to reclassify contracts based on their underlying purpose.

Core Idea
In contracts, the underlying purpose overrides the literal wording.

This lesson focuses on the sub-rule that branches from the first maxim: 'Consideration in contracts is for purposes and meanings, not for words and structures.' The author illustrates with four contract types: bay' al-wafa' (treated as a pledge), gift with compensation (treated as a sale), suretyship that becomes transfer of debt, and transfer of debt that becomes suretyship. Understanding this sub-rule is essential for a mufti to correctly classify contracts in modern financial transactions.

Guided Path
1
State the sub-rule
The sub-rule is: 'العبرة في العقود للمقاصد والمعاني لا للألفاظ والمباني' (Consideration in contracts is for purposes and meanings, not for words and structures). It is a direct branch of the first maxim.
It is codified as article 3 of the Majalla.
2
Apply to bay' al-wafa'
Bay' al-wafa' (sale with repurchase) is treated as a pledge (rahn) because the parties' purpose is security, not ownership transfer. Thus, rulings of rahn apply (e.g., possession is fiduciary).
The word 'sale' is disregarded; the meaning controls.
3
Apply to gift with compensation
A gift (hiba) conditioned on compensation takes the rulings of a sale. The item can be returned for defect; if the item is rightfully claimed by a third party, the donee recovers the compensation.
Despite using the word 'gift', the compensatory element makes it a sale.
4
Apply to suretyship and transfer of debt
If kafala (suretyship) stipulates that the creditor cannot claim the principal debtor, it becomes hawala (transfer of debt). Conversely, if hawala allows claiming both transferor and transferee, it becomes kafala. The purpose dictates the contract type.
The labels are interchangeable based on the effect.
5
Recognize the authority
Al-Sarakhsi in al-Mabsut established this sub-rule in the Hanafi school. It has been consistently applied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter Ten: The General Maxims in Fiqh. 4/81 - The First Maxim: 'Actions are judged by intentions' (Majalla (2)). That is, a person's actions and dispositions, whether verbal or physical, differ in their results and legal rulings that attach to them according to the difference of the person's purpose in those actions and dispositions. So whoever kills another without legitimate justification, if he was intentional, his act has a ruling, and if he was mistaken, it has another ruling. And whoever says to another: 'Take these dirhams,' if he intended donation, it is a gift, otherwise it is a loan obligatory to return. And whoever picks up a lost item with the intention of taking it for himself is a usurper, but if he picks it up with the intention of preserving it, announcing it, and returning it to its owner when he appears, he is a trustee; so he is not liable if it perishes without his transgression or negligence in preserving it, and so on. What branches off from it of rules: 5/81 - Consideration in contracts is for purposes and meanings, not for words and structures (Majalla / 3) (1). So bay' al-wafa' (sale with right of redemption) applies the rulings of several contracts, most prominently the rulings of pledge (rahn), because that is the purpose of the two contracting parties in bay' al-wafa'. And a gift (hiba) if compensation is stipulated in it, as when one says to another: 'I gift you this thing for such and such' or 'on condition that you give me such and such,' the contract takes the rulings of sale because it has become in its meaning, despite the contracting party using the word gift; so the gifted item is returned for defect, and likewise the donee recovers the paid consideration if the gifted item is claimed (3) from his hand, and so on for all rulings of sale. (1) It is mentioned in the book al-Mabsut by al-Sarakhsi (23/22) his statement: '...because consideration in contracts is for meanings, not for words.' So this formulation of the maxim was established in the Hanafi madhhab before the era of Imam al-Sarakhsi, even to the point that it was used as a basis. (2) The meaning of istihqaq (claim/right) has preceded, so refer to (F 2/37 footnote). And suretyship (kafala) if it is stipulated in it that the creditor may not claim the principal debtor (the guaranteed), it becomes a transfer of debt (hawala) and takes its rulings, because it becomes in its meaning; likewise, hawala if it is stipulated in it that the creditor has the right to claim both the transferor debtor and the transferee together, it becomes kafala (see F 5/46-6).
Source pages p. 915

Commentary
This passage introduces the first major legal maxim: 'الأمور بمقاصدها' (Actions are judged by intentions). The author explains that the legal outcome of any act depends on the intention behind it, giving examples from homicide (intentional vs. mistaken), loan vs. gift, and lost property (finder as trustee vs. usurper). He then presents a sub-rule that branches from this maxim: 'العبرة في العقود للمقاصد والمعاني لا للألفاظ والمباني' (Consideration in contracts is for purposes and meanings, not for words and structures). This sub-rule is a cornerstone of Hanafi contract law. The author supports it by quoting al-Sarakhsi's al-Mabsut. Then he applies the sub-rule to specific contracts: bay' al-wafa' (sale with repurchase), where the underlying purpose is a pledge (rahn) despite the sale wording; a gift with compensation, which is treated as a sale; suretyship (kafala) that becomes a transfer of debt (hawala) if the creditor cannot claim the original debtor; and hawala that becomes kafala if the creditor can claim both parties. These applications show how the maxim governs practical fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
المقاصد (al-maqasid): Intentions and purposes behind actions and contracts
بيع الوفاء (bay' al-wafa'): A sale where the seller retains the right to repurchase, functioning effectively as a pledge

A) A valid gift with no changes
B) A loan
C) A sale
D) A pledge

Key Takeaway
In contracts, the underlying purpose overrides the literal wording, so that contracts are recharacterized according to their true meaning.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

The Maxim 'Certainty Is Not Removed by Doubt' and Its Applications
Explaining the second major qawida with proofs and applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'اليقين لا يزول بالشك', explain its proofs (reason, Quran, Sunnah), and apply it to at least two fiqh scenarios, including wudu' doubt and debt/discharge cases.

Core Idea
Certainty is stronger than doubt; established rulings remain until the removing factor is confirmed.

This lesson examines the second of the five major legal maxims (al-qawa'id al-kubra) in Hanafi fiqh: 'Certainty is not removed by doubt.' This maxim is foundational across all chapters of fiqh, governing how we treat established facts when doubt arises. The passage provides rational, Quranic, and prophetic proofs, then applies the maxim to concrete cases in purity, debt, and discharge. Understanding this maxim is essential for any mufti or jurist to maintain legal continuity and avoid invalidating certain rulings based on mere uncertainty.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its definition
Read the maxim in Arabic and its definition: when something is established with certainty, it remains in effect until its removal is also established with certainty. Doubt does not break certainty.
This is a universal principle (kulliya) that applies in all fiqh chapters.
2
Identify the three proofs supporting the maxim
The author cites three proofs: rational (certainty is stronger than doubt), Quranic (verse 10:36 - assumption does not avail against truth), and Prophetic Sunnah (hadith about wudu' doubt). Identify each and explain how it supports the maxim.
The Quranic proof uses 'zann' (assumption) which is weaker than certainty.
3
Apply the maxim to the wudu' scenario
Consider the case: a person is certain of wudu' but doubts its nullification. According to the maxim, the wudu' is considered intact. The prayer is valid despite the doubt.
This is the classic application in Tahara.
4
Apply the maxim to debt and discharge scenarios
Two financial applications: if a debt is established but we doubt whether the debtor paid, the debt remains due. If a discharge (ibra') is established but we doubt whether the debtor accepted, the discharge is effective.
These show the maxim's reach in mu'amalat.
5
Consider the scope and any exceptions
The maxim governs all matters of fiqh. In Hanafi law, there is no true exception to this maxim; even cases where doubt is equal still revert to prior certainty.
This is a universal maxim with no real istithna'.

6/81 - The Second Maxim: 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' (Majalla 4). That is, if a matter or a state is established with certainty, i.e., decisively, then doubt occurs about the existence of what removes it, the certain matter remains the one considered until the removing cause is verified. This maxim is a legal principle supported by reason, the Quran, and the Prophetic Sunnah. From the perspective of reason: certainty is stronger than doubt, because in certainty there is a decisive, conclusive ruling, so it is not demolished by doubt. From the perspective of the Quran: Allah's statement (interpretation of meaning): 'And most of them follow nothing but assumption. Indeed, assumption avails nothing against the truth. Indeed, Allah is Knowing of what they do' [Yunus 10:36]. And 'truth' here means actual reality like certainty. From the perspective of the Sunnah: it is reported in authentic Prophetic hadiths that the one who performs wudu' and doubts its nullification remains in his previous certain wudu', and his prayer is valid with it until he verified the existence of what nullifies it, and no attention is paid to that doubt. Therefore, based on this legal ruling and its examples, this governing maxim is built in all matters of acts of worship, transactions, penalties, and judgments in all rights and obligations. So if a debt is established against a person, then he dies and we doubt in his (the debtor's) death? (or 'we doubt in his payment'? The text says 'وفاته' meaning 'his death'? Actually 'شككنا في وفاته' means 'we doubt in his death' - perhaps it means we doubt whether the debtor has died? But the example says 'فالدين باق' (the debt remains), so it means if we know the debt was established, then we doubt whether the debtor died (and thus the estate is liable) or not? Actually the typical Hanafi example: if there is a debt on a person, then the debtor doubts whether he paid it, the debt remains. But here it says 'شككنا في وفاته' which might be a typo? Alternatively, it might mean 'we doubt in his payment'? However, the Arabic is clear: 'وفاته'. I will translate literally. And if the creditor's discharge of his debtor is established, and doubt occurs about the debtor's return of the discharge (i.e., whether he accepted it?), then the discharge is effective and the debt is dropped.
Source pages p. 916

Commentary
This passage explains the second major legal maxim: 'اليقين لا يزول بالشك' (Certainty is not removed by doubt). The author defines it: when something is established with certainty, it remains in effect until the factor that removes it is confirmed with certainty. This maxim is supported by reason (certainty is stronger than doubt), the Quran (verse 10:36), and the Sunnah (the hadith about wudu' doubt). The author then gives two practical applications in financial matters: if a debt is established but we doubt whether the debtor has died (or paid), the debt remains; and if a discharge (ibra') is established but we doubt whether the debtor returned the discharge document (or accepted it), the discharge is effective. This maxim governs all areas of fiqh, including worship, transactions, penalties, and judgments.

Key Terms
اليقين (al-yaqin): Certain knowledge that is decisive and free from doubt
الشك (al-shakk): Doubt, i.e., uncertainty about the existence or non-existence of a fact

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) His wudu' remains valid and he may pray.
C) He must perform tayammum.
D) His prayer is invalid until certainty returns.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'اليقين لا يزول بالشك' ensures that established certainties are not overturned by mere doubt, maintaining legal stability across all areas of fiqh.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
15 min
The Second Major Maxim: Certainty Is Not Removed by Doubt
Explains the second major maxim with rational, Quranic, and Sunnah evidence and applications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' to identify the correct ruling in cases of doubt, and to distinguish when the exception (original non-existence) applies.

Core Idea
Certainty is only removed by certainty, not by doubt.

The second major maxim of the Majalla sits at the foundation of Hanafi legal reasoning. It protects established rights and obligations from being undermined by mere supposition. Understanding this maxim is essential for a mufti when encountering cases where doubt arises about a prior certain state. In this lesson, we explore its textual and rational proofs, work through concrete furūʿ, and examine its one critical exception.

Guided Path
1
Define the key terms: al-yaqin and al-shakk
Identify what constitutes certainty (yaqin) and doubt (shakk) in Hanafi usul. Yaqin is definitive knowledge equivalent to qat'; shakk is uncertainty where neither side is preponderant.
The maxim only applies when a prior yaqin exists; if only zann (preponderant belief) exists, other rules may apply.
2
Examine the three evidential bases
The maxim is supported by reason (certainty is stronger than doubt), the Quran (verse 10:36 contrasting assumption with truth), and the Sunnah (hadith on wudu doubt). Understand how each proof underpins the maxim.
The hadith explicitly instructs to remain on the certain state until certainty of nullification.
3
Apply the maxim to the four furuʿ
Walk through each case: debt remains despite death doubt, release effective despite return doubt, contract remains despite rescission doubt, and depositary not liable despite cause-of-loss doubt. Confirm how certainty prevails in each.
The attribute of trustworthiness (amana) in the deposit case is the certain element that is not removed by doubt.
4
Identify the exception and its governing maxim
When there is no prior certainty about the existence of something, the maxim does not apply. Instead, the principle 'Original non-existence' (al-asl al-ʿadam) governs.
This exception ensures the maxim is not overextended to situations lacking a certain baseline.

- وإذا ثبت عقد بين اثنين وقع الشك في فسخه فالعقد قائم.
- وإذا هلكت الوديعة عند الوديع وشككنا في أنها هلكت بتعديه عليها أو بتقصيره في حفظها ( فيضمنها) أو أنها هلكت قضاء وقدراً (فلا يضمنها) فإنه يعتبر غير ضامن، لأنَّ صفة الأمانة هي المتيقنة عند العقد فلا تزول بالشك في حصول التعدي أو التقصير وهكذا يقال في كل مشابه
Chapter (81) Explanation of the maxims of the Majalla and their classification. And if in a guaranty it is stipulated that the creditor cannot demand payment from the original debtor, it becomes a transfer (hawala) and takes its rulings because it becomes equivalent to it. Similarly, if in a transfer it is stipulated for the creditor the right to demand payment from both the transferor and the transferee together, it becomes a guaranty (reference: [؟]). 81/6 - The second maxim: 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' (Article 4). That is, if a matter or a situation is established with certainty, i.e., definitively, and then doubt occurs about the existence of something that removes it, the certain matter remains the valid one until the removing cause is verified. This maxim is a legal principle supported by reason, the Quran, and the Prophetic Sunnah. From the perspective of reason: certainty is stronger than doubt, because in certainty there is a definitive, decisive ruling, so it is not demolished by doubt. From the perspective of the Quran: Allah Almighty said: 'And most of them follow nothing but assumption; indeed, assumption does not avail against the truth at all. Indeed, Allah is Knowing of what they do' [Yunus 10:36]. And 'truth' here means the actual reality like certainty. From the perspective of the Sunnah: it is reported in authentic Prophetic hadiths that if one who performed ablution doubts whether his ablution was invalidated, he remains on his previous certain ablution, and his prayer is valid with it until he is certain of the existence of something that invalidates it, and that doubt is disregarded. So, based on this legal ruling and its likes, this governing maxim was established in all matters—acts of worship, transactions, penalties, and judgments—in all rights and obligations. For example: If a debt is established against a person, then he dies and we doubt whether he died, the debt remains. If the creditor's release of his debtor is established, and doubt occurs about the debtor returning the release, then the release is effective and the debt is dropped. [PAGE] And if a contract is established between two persons and doubt occurs about its rescission, the contract remains valid. And if a deposit perishes with the depositary and we doubt whether it perished due to his transgression or negligence (so he would be liable) or whether it perished by divine decree (so he is not liable), then he is considered not liable, because the attribute of trustworthiness is certain at the time of the contract and is not removed by doubt about the occurrence of transgression or negligence. And so on is said for every similar case.
Source pages p. 916

Commentary
This passage introduces the second major maxim of Islamic jurisprudence as codified in the Majalla: 'Certainty is not removed by doubt.' The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins with two practical examples from guaranty and transfer to illustrate how stipulations can transform one contract into another (though these examples may relate to the previous maxim). The core of the lesson is the maxim itself. The text explains that if a matter is established with certainty (yaqin), then even if doubt arises about something that might remove it, the certain state prevails until the removing factor is confirmed. Three types of evidence are provided: rational (certainty is stronger than doubt), Quranic (the verse from Surah Yunus, where 'assumption' does not avail against 'truth'), and Sunnah (the hadith about one who doubts his ablution—he remains on the certain state of purity). The maxim is then applied to various fields: debt remains despite doubt about death, a release is valid despite doubt about its return, a contract persists despite doubt of rescission, and a depositary is not liable when doubt exists about the cause of loss because the original trustworthiness is certain. This maxim is foundational in all areas of fiqh.

Key Terms
اليقين (al-yaqin): Certainty: a definitive, decisive knowledge that a matter is true, without any doubt.
الشك (al-shakk): Doubt: uncertainty about a matter where neither side is preponderant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person is certain they performed wudu, then doubts whether they passed wind. According to the maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt,' what is the ruling on the validity of their wudu?
A) The wudu is invalid because of the doubt.
B) The wudu remains valid until they are certain it was broken.
C) The wudu is invalid unless they repeat it.
D) They must perform tayammum instead.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' preserves established legal states until their removal is certain, ensuring stability in all fiqh rulings.
