Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
beginner
8 min
The Maxim of Continuity: Istishab (الأصل بقاء ما كان على ما كان)
Explains istishab and the first branch maxim from the Majalla with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define istishab, state the maxim 'الأصل بقاء ما كان على ما كان', and apply it to resolve disputes over debts, deposits, and similar obligations.

Core Idea
Istishab presumes that an established state continues until proven otherwise.

This lesson introduces the foundational concept of istishab (presumption of continuity) and the maxim 'Things remain as they were.' This maxim is the cornerstone of many evidentiary rules in Hanafi law. A mufti must understand when to apply this presumption and when it yields to contrary evidence.

Guided Path
1
Define Istishab
Understand the definition: considering an established state as continuing until change is proven. The text says: 'وهو اعتبار الحالة الثابتة في وقت ما مستمرة في سائر الأوقات حتى يثبت انقطاعها أو تبدلها.'
Istishab is based on certainty (yaqin).
2
State the First Maxim
The maxim 'الأصل بقاء ما كان على ما كان' (Majalla Art. 5) means that a thing remains in its original state until evidence of change appears.
This maxim is the primary expression of istishab.
3
Identify the Scope
This maxim applies to any previously certain state: debts, contracts, trusts, legal statuses. For example, if a debt was certainly owed, it is presumed still owed.
The state must have been established with certainty.
4
Apply to Examples
Work through the examples: borrower claims payment (debt presumed unpaid), deposit perishes (depositee presumed trustworthy), buyer claims payment (price presumed unpaid). In each, the denial of the change is accepted with oath.
The burden of proof is on the one claiming the change.
5
Note the Exception
The maxim applies only until evidence of change is provided. It is not absolute; if clear proof contradicts the presumption, the proof prevails.
The maxim itself states 'حتى يثبت انقطاعها أو تبدلها.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية في الفقه
- وإذا ثبت عقد بين اثنين ووقع الشك في فسخه فالعقد قائم.
- وإذا هلكت الوديعة عند الوديع وشككنا في أنها هلكت بتعديه عليها أو بتقصيره في حفظها ( فيضمنها) أو أنها هلكت قضاء وقدراً (فلا يضمنها) فإنه يعتبر غير ضامن، لأنَّ صفة الأمانة هي المتيقنة عند العقد فلا تزول بالشك في حصول التعدي أو التقصير وهكذا يقال في كل مشابه
ما يتفرع عنها من القواعد :
۷/۸۱ - أ - الأصل بقاء ما كان على ما كان (م/ ٥).
-
هذا الأصل يسمى الاستصحاب، وهو اعتبار الحالة الثابتة في وقت ما مستمرة في سائر الأوقات حتى يثبت انقطاعها أو تبدلها.
فلو ادعى المقترض دفع الدين إلى المقرض، أو ادعى المشتري دفع الثمن إلى البائع أو ادعى المستأجر دفع الأجرة إلى المؤجر، وأنكر المقرض أو البائع أو المؤجر، كان القول لهؤلاء المنكرين مع اليمين. أي أن هذه الديون تعتبر باقية في ذمم الملتزمين بها ما لم يثبتوا الدفع، لأنها كانت مستحقة عليهم بيقين فالأصل بقاؤها في ذممهم حتى يثبت سقوطها، وإنما لهم تحليف الدائنين اليمين على عدم القبض فإذا حلفوا قضي لهم. ۸/۸۱ - ب - ما ثبت بزمان يُحكم ببقائه ما لم يوجد دليل على
خلافه (م/ ۱۰)
هذه القاعدة في معنى سابقتها ؛ وجميع أمثلتهما في الأحكام العملية
۹/۸۱ - ج - الأصل في الأمور العارضة العدم» (م/۹).
المراد بالأمور العارضة ما كان عدمه هو الحالة الأصلية أو الغالبة، فيكون العدم هو المتيقن لأنه الحالة الطبيعية ويكون تغيره إلى الوجود عارضاً مشكوكاً فيه.
فلو ادعى شخص على آخر أنه عقد معه عقداً أو أتلف له مالاً أو ارتكب جريمة، وأنكر الآخر، فالقولُ لهذا حتى يثبت المدعي هذه الأفعال،
لأنها أمور عارضة وإنَّ الحالة الأصلية المتيقنة قبلاً هي عدمها .
- وهكذا لو تبايع اثنان فادَّعى أحدهما بعد ذلك أنه اشترط في العقد لنفسه الخيار، ويريد الفسخ والرد، وأنكر الآخر هذا الاشتراط فالقول للمنكر بيمينه حتى يثبت الخيار من يدعيه ذلك لأن الاشتراط أمر عارض، فالحالة الطبيعية الأصلية في العقد هي خلوه عن الشروط الزائدة فيكون عدمها هو المتيقن، ووجودها مشكوك فيه يحتاج إلى إثبات. - وكذلك لو اختلف المتبايعان في صحة الدابة المبيعة أو مرضها، فالقولُ للبائع في زعم الصحة، لأن المرض هو العارض، وإن السلامة هي الحالة الطبيعية الأصلية.
- وكذا لو زعم ورثة عاقد أن مورثهم كان حين التعاقد مجنوناً فعقده باطل وأنكر الخصم اعتبر العاقد عاقلاً حتى يثبت جنونه، لأن الجنونَ آفَةٌ عارضة، والأصل سلامة العقل فكان الظاهر شاهداً لمدعيها (١)
ملاحظة :
نص هذه القاعدة في المجلة الأصل في الصفات العارضة العدم». والفقهاء كثيراً ما يُعبرون في تعليلاتهم بلفظ «الأمور» بدلاً من «الصفات» وهذا هو المراد بالقاعدة فإن القاعدة لا يقتصر شمولها على الصفات كالجنون والمرض مثلاً، بل تشمل الأمور المستقلة، مثل العقود والإتلافات، كما يتضح من الأمثلة السابقة.
(1) المبدأ المقرّر في المذهب الحنفي أنه عند الاختلاف في صحة العقد وبطلانه فالقول مبدئياً لمدعي البطلان وعلى مدعي الصحة الإثبات لأن الباطل معدوم والأصل العدم . لكن يظهر أن هذا مقيد بما إذا لم يكن ظاهر الحال شاهداً لمدعي الصحة دون البطلان كما هنا (ر : القول الحسن بحث الاختلاف في الصحة والتلجئة من باب البيع ص/ ۱۰۲ الطبعة الحجرية سنة ١٢٧٦).
Chapter Ten: General Legal Maxims in Fiqh
- If a contract is established between two persons and doubt arises about its annulment, the contract remains valid.
- If a deposit perishes with the depositee and we doubt whether it perished due to his transgression or negligence (so he guarantees it) or that it perished by destiny (so he does not guarantee it), then he is considered non-guarantor, because the attribute of trustworthiness is the certain one at the time of contract, so it is not removed by doubt about the occurrence of transgression or negligence. And so it is said in every similar case.
What branches off from these rules:
7/81 - A - The principle is that things remain as they were (Majalla Article 5).
This principle is called istishab, and it is considering an established state at a certain time as continuing at all other times until its discontinuation or change is proven.
If the borrower claims payment of the debt to the lender, or the buyer claims payment of the price to the seller, or the tenant claims payment of rent to the lessor, and the lender, seller, or lessor denies, the word is with these deniers with their oath. That is, these debts are considered remaining in the obligations of those liable unless they prove payment, because they were owed with certainty, so the principle is their remaining in their obligations until their fall is proven. They have the right to have the creditors swear an oath of non-receipt; if they swear, judgment is given for them.
8/81 - B - What is established at a certain time is judged to remain unless evidence to the contrary exists (Majalla Article 10). This rule is similar to the previous one; all their examples are in practical rulings.
9/81 - C - The principle concerning incidental matters is non-existence (Majalla Article 9).
By incidental matters is meant that whose non-existence is the original or predominant state. Thus non-existence is the certain thing because it is the natural state, and its change to existence is incidental and doubtful.
If a person claims against another that he contracted with him, or destroyed his property, or committed a crime, and the other denies, the word is with the latter until the plaintiff proves these actions, because they are incidental matters, and the original certain state previously was their non-existence.
Likewise, if two parties make a sale and one later claims that he stipulated an option for himself in the contract and wants to rescind and return, and the other denies this stipulation, the word is with the denier with his oath until the one claiming the option proves it, because the stipulation is an incidental matter, and the original natural state of the contract is its being free from additional conditions, so its non-existence is certain, and its existence is doubtful requiring proof.
- Similarly, if the buyer and seller dispute the soundness or illness of the sold animal, the word is with the seller in claiming soundness, because illness is the incidental matter, and soundness is the original natural state.
- Likewise, if the heirs of a contracting party claim that their testator was insane at the time of contracting, making his contract void, and the opponent denies, the contracting party is considered sane until his insanity is proven, because insanity is an incidental defect, and the principle is soundness of mind, so the apparent state is a witness for the one claiming it.
Note: The text of this rule in the Majalla is "The original state concerning incidental attributes is non-existence." Jurists often express in their justifications the word "matters" instead of "attributes," and this is what is meant by the rule. The rule is not limited in its scope to attributes like insanity and illness, but rather includes independent matters such as contracts and torts, as is clear from the previous examples.
(1) The established principle in the Hanafi school is that when there is disagreement about the validity and invalidity of a contract, the word is initially with the one claiming invalidity, and the one claiming validity must provide proof, because the void is non-existent and the principle is non-existence. However, it appears that this is restricted to when the apparent state is not a witness for the claim of validity over invalidity as here (refer to al-Qawl al-Hasan on the discussion of disagreement about validity and dissimulation from the chapter of sale, p. 102, lithograph edition, 1276 AH).
Source pages p. 917

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of three fundamental legal maxims (qawaid kulliyah) from the Majalla, all centering on the concept of istishab (presumption of continuity). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, presents these maxims as the tenth chapter of his introduction to fiqh. The first maxim, 'الأصل بقاء ما كان على ما كان' (Things remain as they were), is the basis of istishab, which presumes that a previously established state continues until evidence of change arises. The author defines istishab explicitly and illustrates it with examples: in debt claims, the debt is presumed unpaid unless the debtor proves payment; in deposits, the depositee is presumed trustworthy unless breach is proven. The second maxim, 'ما ثبت بزمان يُحكم ببقائه' (What is established at a time is judged to remain), is described as equivalent to the first. The third maxim, 'الأصل في الأمور العارضة العدم' (The original state of incidental matters is non-existence), applies to events or conditions that deviate from the norm, such as contracts, torts, stipulations, or defects. The author clarifies that 'incidental matters' (الأمور العارضة) include both temporary attributes (like illness or insanity) and independent acts (like contracts or torts). The note highlights a nuance: while the Hanafi school generally favors the claimant of invalidity in contract disputes (since voidness is non-existence), this rule applies only when the apparent state does not support the claim of validity. This passage is foundational for understanding the evidentiary role of istishab in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Key Terms
الاستصحاب (al-istishāb): A legal presumption that a previously established state continues until evidence of change arises.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a debtor claims he paid the debt and the creditor denies, who is required to provide proof?
A) The debtor
B) The creditor
C) Both
D) None

Key Takeaway
Istishab presumes continuity of a known state until change is proven.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
beginner
8 min
The Maxim of Incidental Matters: Non-Existence as the Original State
Explains the maxim for incidental matters with examples from sales, torts, and inheritance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim for incidental matters, apply it to contractual and tort disputes, and identify the exception when the apparent state supports the claim of existence.

Core Idea
What is incidental is presumed non-existent; the burden of proof is on the claimant.

While the first maxim deals with continuity of established states, this second maxim governs new or incidental matters. It presumes that events or conditions deviating from the norm—like stipulations, defects, or obligations—do not exist until proven. This lesson explores its application and the crucial nuance when the apparent state (zahir al-hal) suggests otherwise.

Guided Path
1
State the Maxim
The maxim is 'الأصل في الأمور العارضة العدم' (Majalla Article 9). It means that the original state in incidental matters is non-existence.
Incidental matters are things whose non-existence is the natural or predominant state.
2
Define 'Incidental Matters'
These include attributes (like illness, insanity) and independent acts (like contracts, torts). The text says: 'ما كان عدمه هو الحالة الأصلية أو الغالبة'.
The Majalla uses 'صفات' (attributes), but the author clarifies it includes independent matters.
3
Apply to Examples
Work through the three examples: claim of contract (denier's word), claim of stipulation (denier's word), claim of defect (seller's claim of soundness). In each, the denial is accepted because the matter is incidental.
The burden of proof is always on the one alleging the incidental matter.
4
Apply to the Heir-Insanity Example
Heirs claim the testator was insane; the opponent denies. The testator is presumed sane because insanity is incidental. This shows the maxim extends to attributes.
The apparent state (zahir) is a witness for the claim of sanity.
5
Identify the Exception
The footnote mentions that when there is disagreement about contract validity, initially the claimant of invalidity is believed (since voidness is non-existence). But if the apparent state supports validity, the rule shifts.
This exception is from al-Qawl al-Hasan and shows the maxim is not absolute.

- If a contract is established between two persons and doubt arises about its annulment, the contract remains valid.
- If a deposit perishes with the depositee and we doubt whether it perished due to his transgression or negligence (so he guarantees it) or that it perished by destiny (so he does not guarantee it), then he is considered non-guarantor, because the attribute of trustworthiness is the certain one at the time of contract, so it is not removed by doubt about the occurrence of transgression or negligence. (1) The established principle in the Hanafi school is that when there is disagreement about the validity and invalidity of a contract, the word is initially with the one claiming invalidity, and the one claiming validity must provide proof, because the void is non-existent and the principle is non-existence. Source pages p. 917

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of three fundamental legal maxims (qawaid kulliyah) from the Majalla, all centering on the concept of istishab (presumption of continuity). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, presents these maxims as the tenth chapter of his introduction to fiqh. The first maxim, 'الأصل بقاء ما كان على ما كان' (Things remain as they were), is the basis of istishab, which presumes that a previously established state continues until evidence of change arises. The author defines istishab explicitly and illustrates it with examples: in debt claims, the debt is presumed unpaid unless the debtor proves payment; in deposits, the depositee is presumed trustworthy unless breach is proven. The second maxim, 'ما ثبت بزمان يُحكم ببقائه' (What is established at a time is judged to remain), is described as equivalent to the first. The third maxim, 'الأصل في الأمور العارضة العدم' (The original state of incidental matters is non-existence), applies to events or conditions that deviate from the norm, such as contracts, torts, stipulations, or defects. The author clarifies that 'incidental matters' (الأمور العارضة) include both temporary attributes (like illness or insanity) and independent acts (like contracts or torts). The note highlights a nuance: while the Hanafi school generally favors the claimant of invalidity in contract disputes (since voidness is non-existence), this rule applies only when the apparent state does not support the claim of validity. This passage is foundational for understanding the evidentiary role of istishab in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Key Terms
الأمور العارضة (al-umūr al-ʿāriḍah): Incidental matters whose original or predominant state is non-existence, such as contracts, torts, or defects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'الأصل في الأمور العارضة العدم', if a seller claims the buyer agreed to waive the option of inspection, and the buyer denies, who has the burden of proof?
A) The seller
B) The buyer
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Incidental matters are presumed non-existent until proven; the burden lies on the one alleging their existence.

The Maxim: 'The Origin in Incidental Matters is Non-existence'
Explaining the maxim with examples and a terminological note

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-asl fi al-umur al-‘aridah al-‘adam' to determine the burden of proof in disputes over incidental matters and identify the exception when the apparent state supports the claimant.

Core Idea
Incidental matters are presumed non-existent until proven; the original certain state prevails.

This lesson introduces a foundational legal maxim from the Majalla that governs the burden of proof in disputes over incidental matters. The maxim establishes a presumption of non-existence for any attribute, event, or action that is not the original state. Through examples from contracts, torts, and sales, we will see how Hanafi jurists apply this presumption. A note clarifies that the maxim covers not only attributes but also independent matters, and an exception arises when the apparent reality supports the claim.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Begin by stating the maxim plainly: 'The origin in incidental matters is non-existence.' Understand that it applies to any matter that is not the original or permanent state, including attributes and independent actions.
The Majalla uses 'attributes' but the intended meaning includes matters like contracts.
2
Identify the incidental matter in a dispute
When presented with a dispute, determine what is being claimed as new or additional. That claimed element is the incidental matter. The original state is its absence.
For example, claiming an option stipulation is incidental to the sale.
3
Apply the presumption to the parties
Since the incidental matter is presumed non-existent, the burden of proof falls on the claimant (the one asserting the existence of the incidental matter). The denier is believed on oath until proof is provided.
The denier's statement is accepted initially.
4
Recognize the exception: when the apparent state supports the claimant
The maxim has an exception: if the apparent state (zahir al-hal) supports the claimant's assertion, the presumption shifts. The burden then moves to the denier.
This exception is grounded in the qawida 'al-zahir shahid li-mudda‘ih'.
5
Apply the maxim to new cases
Using the examples and exception, practice applying the maxim to various contractual and tort disputes. Determine who bears the burden and when the exception applies.
Consult the furuat in the qawida panel for guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (۸۱) شرح قواعد المجلة وتصنيفها

فلو ادعى شخص على آخر أنه عقد معه عقداً أو أتلف له مالاً أو ارتكب جريمة، وأنكر الآخر، فالقولُ لهذا حتى يثبت المدعي هذه الأفعال، لأنها أمور عارضة وإنَّ الحالة الأصلية المتيقنة قبلاً هي عدمها .

- وهكذا لو تبايع اثنان فادَّعى أحدهما بعد ذلك أنه اشترط في العقد لنفسه الخيار، ويريد الفسخ والرد، وأنكر الآخر هذا الاشتراط فالقول للمنكر بيمينه حتى يثبت الخيار من يدعيه ذلك لأن الاشتراط أمر عارض، فالحالة الطبيعية الأصلية في العقد هي خلوه عن الشروط الزائدة فيكون عدمها هو المتيقن، ووجودها مشكوك فيه يحتاج إلى إثبات. - وكذلك لو اختلف المتبايعان في صحة الدابة المبيعة أو مرضها، فالقولُ للبائع في زعم الصحة، لأن المرض هو العارض، وأن السلامة الحالة الطبيعية الأصلية.

- وكذا لو زعم ورثة عاقد أن مورثهم كان حين التعاقد مجنوناً فعقده باطل وأنكر الخصم اعتبر العاقد عاقلاً حتى يثبت جنونه، لأن الجنونَ آفَةٌ عارضة، والأصل سلامة العقل فكان الظاهر شاهداً لمدعيها (١)

:
نص هذه القاعدة في المجلة الأصل في الصفات العارضة العدم». والفقهاء كثيراً ما يُعبرون في تعليلاتهم بلفظ «الأمور» بدلاً من «الصفات» وهذا هو المراد بالقاعدة فإن القاعدة لا يقتصر شمولها على الصفات كالجنون والمرض مثلاً، بل تشمل الأمور المستقلة، مثل العقود والإتلافات، كما يتضح من الأمثلة السابقة.
(1) المبدأ المقرّر في المذهب الحنفي أنه عند الاختلاف في صحة العقد وبطلانه فالقول مبدئياً لمدعي البطلان وعلى مدعي الصحة الإثبات لأن الباطل معدوم والأصل العدم . لكن يظهر أن هذا مقيد بما إذا لم يكن ظاهر الحال شاهداً لمدعي الصحة دون البطلان كما هنا (ر : القول الحسن بحث الاختلاف في الصحة والتلجئة من باب البيع ص/ ۱۰۲ الطبعة الحجرية سنة ١٢٧٦).

فلذا اخترنا التعبير في القاعدة بلفظ «الأمور العارضة».
Chapter (81) Explanation of the Rules of the Majalla and their Classification

If a person claims against another that he contracted with him, or caused damage to his property, or committed a crime, and the other denies it, then the statement is with the denier until the claimant proves these actions, because they are incidental matters, and the original certain state beforehand is their non-existence.

- Likewise, if two parties make a sale and thereafter one of them claims that he stipulated an option for himself in the contract, and wants to rescind and return, and the other denies this stipulation, then the statement is with the denier upon his oath until the option is proved by the one who claims it, because the stipulation is an incidental matter, and the natural original state of the contract is its being free from additional conditions, so its non-existence is certain, and its existence is doubtful requiring proof. - Similarly, if the buyer and seller differ about the health of the animal sold or its illness, then the statement is with the seller in the claim of health, because illness is incidental, and health is the natural original state.

- Likewise, if the heirs of a contractor claim that their ancestor was insane at the time of contracting so his contract is void, and the opponent denies, the contractor is considered sane until his insanity is proved, because insanity is an incidental defect, and the original state is soundness of mind, so the apparent state bears witness to the one who claims it (1)

Note:
:
The text of this rule in the Majalla is "The origin in incidental attributes is non-existence." And jurists often express in their reasoning with the word "matters" instead of "attributes," and this is what is meant by the rule, for the rule is not limited in its scope to attributes like insanity and illness, but rather includes independent matters, such as contracts and torts, as is clear from the preceding examples.
(1) The established principle in the Hanafi school is that when there is a dispute over the validity and invalidity of a contract, the statement is initially with the one claiming invalidity, and upon the claimant of validity is proof, because the invalid is non-existent and the origin is non-existence. But it appears that this is restricted to when the apparent state does not support the claimant of validity over invalidity, as here (see: al-Qawl al-Hasan, discussion of dispute over validity and taghliz from the chapter of sale, p. 102, lithographic edition 1276).

Therefore we chose to express the rule with the phrase "incidental matters."
Source pages p. 918

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Aam by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa explains a major legal maxim from the Majalla (the Ottoman civil code). The maxim is: "The origin in incidental matters is non-existence." The author begins by illustrating the maxim with four examples. First, if someone claims a debt or tort against another, the denier's statement is accepted because the claimed act is incidental; the default is its absence. Second, in a sale, if one party claims a condition (like a stipulation of option) that the other denies, the denier's statement prevails because such stipulations are incidental. Third, if buyer and seller dispute whether a sold animal was healthy or ill, the seller's claim of health is believed because illness is incidental. Fourth, if heirs claim the deceased was insane at the time of contract, the contract is presumed valid because sanity is the original state. The author then adds a note clarifying that the Majalla's text says "attributes" but the intended meaning includes independent matters like contracts and torts, as shown in the examples. He also references a Hanafi rule that in disputes over validity, the claimant of invalidity is initially believed, but this is qualified when the apparent state supports validity.

Key Terms
الأمور العارضة (al-umur al-‘aridah): Incidental matters; any event, attribute, or action that is not the original or permanent state
الحالة الأصلية المتيقنة (al-halah al-asliyyah al-mutayaqqinah): The original certain state that is presumed to exist until contrary proof

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The buyer, because he claims the incidental stipulation.
B) The seller, because he denies the stipulation.
C) Both must provide equal evidence.
D) The judge decides without proof.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-asl fi al-umur al-‘aridah al-‘adam' places the burden of proof on the claimant of any incidental matter, unless the apparent state supports the claim.

Original Innocence of Liability: The Default State
Explains the maxim of original innocence of liability and its evidentiary consequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-asl bara'at al-dhimmah' to determine the burden of proof in claims of debt, obligation, or tort, and recognize when the apparent state (zahir al-hal) supports the defendant.

Core Idea
A person's liability is born free; any claim against it must be proven.

This lesson introduces a foundational maxim of Hanafi jurisprudence: the original innocence of liability. Every person's legal responsibility (dhimmah) begins empty. Any debt, obligation, or liability arises only through external causes after birth. Since incidental matters are presumed non-existent (per the prior maxim), the burden of proof falls on the claimant. This principle governs debts, contracts, torts, and all forms of claims, making it a cornerstone of Hanafi evidentiary law.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its textual basis
Identify the Arabic wording of the maxim as given in the source: 'الأصل براءة الذمة'. Note that it is codified in Majalla article 8.
The maxim is a major (kubra) universal legal maxim.
2
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
The source explains that a person is born free of all debts and obligations. Any 'occupation' of the liability occurs after birth through incidental causes. Since the origin of incidental matters is non-existence, the liability remains free until proven otherwise.
This links to the previous maxim 'al-asl 'adam al-'arid' (origin is non-existence of incidental matters).
3
Identify the legal presumption and who it favors
The maxim creates a presumption in favor of the defendant (the one against whom a claim is made). The 'apparent state' (zahir al-hal) is that the defendant's liability is innocent, so the claimant must provide evidence.
Zahir al-hal here refers to the original state of innocence.
4
Apply the maxim to a general claim scenario
The source states: whoever claims a debt, an action, or any liability (from contract, tort, etc.) against another must prove it if the defendant denies. The defendant relies on the original state.
The burden is on the claimant, not the defendant.
5
Determine what shifts or overcomes the presumption
The presumption holds 'unless the contrary is proved'. Thus, any evidence from the claimant that establishes the liability overcomes the innocence of liability. The maxim does not exempt the defendant from proving affirmative defenses (e.g., payment, release).
The maxim governs initial burden; subsequent burdens may shift.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۱۰۸۱ - د - الأصل براءة الذمة» (م/۸).
-
لأن المرء يولد خالياً من كل دين أو التزام أو مسؤولية. وكل شغل لذمته بشيء من الحقوق إنما يطرأ بأسباب عارضة بعد الولادة، والأصل في الأمور العارضة العدم، كما رأينا في المادة السالفة
فمن ادعى على غيره التزاماً بدين أو بعمل ما، مهما كان سببه، من عقد أو إتلاف أو أي سبب آخر من أسباب الضمان، فعليه هو الإثبات إذا أنكر الخصم، لأنَّ هذا الخصم يتمسك بحالة أصلية هي براءة الذمة، فيكون ظاهر الحال شاهداً له ما لم يثبت خلافه .
1081 - D - "The origin is the innocence of the liability" (Majalla article 8).
-
Because a person is born free from any debt, obligation, or responsibility. Every occupation of his liability with something of rights only occurs through incidental causes after birth, and the origin in incidental matters is non-existence, as we saw in the preceding section.

So whoever claims against another an obligation of a debt or some action, whatever its cause, from contract, tort, or any other cause of liability, he must prove it if the opponent denies, because this opponent adheres to an original state which is the innocence of the liability, so the apparent state bears witness for him unless the contrary is proved.
Source pages p. 919

Commentary
This passage explains the second maxim: "The origin is the innocence of the liability" (al-asl bara'at al-dhimmah). The author reasons that every person is born with a liability free from any debt or obligation. Any right that occupies the liability arises through incidental causes after birth, and since incidental matters are presumed non-existent (as per the previous maxim), the burden of proof falls on the claimant. Thus, if someone claims that another owes a debt or must perform an action, the claimant must prove it if the defendant denies. The defendant's original innocence of liability is the apparent state that supports him until the contrary is proved.

Key Terms
براءة الذمة (bara'at al-dhimmah): The original state of a person's legal liability being free from any debt or obligation
ظاهر الحال (zahir al-hal): The apparent state that is legally considered as evidence until contrary proof

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The defendant must prove he did not cause damage.
B) The claimant must prove the damage and causation.
C) The judge must investigate without any presumption.
D) Both parties must present evidence equally.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-asl bara'at al-dhimmah' establishes that every person's liability is originally innocent, placing the burden of proof on the claimant for any alleged debt, action, or obligation.

The Maxim: Attributing an Event to its Nearest Time
Explaining the maxim with example of sales defect and exception for congenital defects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-asl idafat al-hadith ila aqrab awqatihi' to resolve disputes over the timing of defects in sales and identify the exception for congenital defects.

Core Idea
When in doubt about timing, attribute the event to the nearest agreed time—unless it could only have been earlier.

This lesson examines a crucial maxim from the Majalla that governs how judges and muftis resolve disputes about when an event occurred. The rule states that an event is attributed to the nearest time on which both parties agree, because that is certain. This maxim is especially useful in sales when a defect appears after delivery—the Hanafi school presumes it happened with the buyer unless the defect is congenital. Let's explore the reasoning and the exception.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its justification
The maxim 'al-asl idafat al-hadith ila aqrab awqatihi' means an event is attributed to its nearest time. This is because the nearest time is agreed upon and certain; an earlier time is doubtful.
2
Identify the scope of the maxim
This maxim applies to disputes over timing in any legal event, especially in sales (buyu') when a defect is discovered after delivery.
It is a general procedural rule.
3
Apply the maxim to the sales defect scenario
If a defect appears after the buyer takes possession, the seller claims it occurred with the buyer; the buyer claims it existed before. The maxim attributes the defect to the nearest time—after delivery—so the buyer cannot rescind unless he proves it was pre-existing.
The burden of proof is on the buyer.
4
Identify the exception (istithna')
If the defect is congenital and cannot arise after birth (like heterochromia in a horse called 'khayaf'), then it is attributed to the earlier time because it is not of a type that occurs later.
The exception is governed by another maxim: 'al-asl ma kana 'ala asl al-khilqah'.
5
Derive the practical ruling
The mufta bihi ruling: In a dispute over a defect, presume it occurred with the buyer unless the defect is congenital; in that case, presume it existed before sale.
This protects the seller from unfounded claims while protecting the buyer when the defect is inherently pre-existing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۱۱/۸۱ـ هـ ـ «الأصل إضافة الحادث إلى أقرب أوقاته (م(۱۱).
كثيراً ما تختلف أحكام الحوادث ونتائجها باختلاف تاريخ حدوثها. فعند التنازع في تاريخ الحادث يحمل على الوقت الأقرب إلى الحال حتى يثبت الأبعد. لأنَّ الوقت الأقرب قد اتفق الطرفان على وجود الحادث فيه، وانفرد أحدهما بزعم وجوده قبل ذلك.
فوجود الحادث في الوقت الأقرب متيقن، وفي الأبعد مشكوك (من

(1):

1081/11 - E - "The origin is attributing the event to its nearest time" (Majalla article 11).

Often the rulings of events and their results differ according to the date of their occurrence. So when there is a dispute over the date of an event, it is attributed to the time nearest to the present until the further time is proved. Because the nearest time both parties have agreed on the existence of the event, and only one of them claims its existence before that.

So the existence of the event at the nearest time is certain, and at the further time is doubtful (from the commentary of my father, may Allah have mercy on him, on the rules).

And upon this there are many branches:
Among them is if a defect appears in the sold item after delivery, and the seller claims it occurred while with the buyer, and the buyer claims it existed with the seller, then it is considered to have occurred while with the buyer, so he does not have the right to rescind the sale until he proves that it was old with the seller, unless the defect is of a type that does not occur, but is rather from the original creation, like khaif (one black eye and one blue eye) in a horse (1).
(1) Al-khayaf (with two fatha letters) is when one eye is black and the other is blue, and the adjective is akhyaf, like abayad and aswad.
Source pages p. 919

Commentary
This passage explains the third maxim: "The origin is attributing an event to its nearest time." The author notes that legal rulings often depend on when an event happened. When parties dispute the timing, the event is attributed to the nearest time to the present on which both agree, because that is certain; the claim of an earlier time is doubtful and requires proof. A key application is when a defect is discovered in a purchased item after delivery. The seller claims the defect occurred while the item was with the buyer; the buyer claims it existed with the seller. The maxim resolves this by presuming the defect occurred with the buyer (nearest time), so the buyer cannot rescind unless he proves the defect was pre-existing. An exception is if the defect is congenital, like khaif (heterochromia) in a horse, which does not arise after birth—such a defect is presumed to have existed before sale.

Key Terms
إضافة الحادث إلى أقرب أوقاته (idafat al-hadith ila aqrab awqatihi): Attributing an event to the nearest time that both parties agree upon
الخَيَف (al-khayaf): A congenital condition in a horse where one eye is black and the other blue

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'attributing an event to its nearest time' resolves timing disputes by presuming the later occurrence, unless the event is of a type that cannot occur later, in which case it is attributed to the earlier time.

intermediate
8 min
Maxim: No Weight to Indication Against Explicit Statement
Expansion and application of Majalla Article 13

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida 'لا عبرة للدلالة في مقابلة التصريح' to cases involving expression of will, distinguishing when circumstantial indication is overridden by explicit statement and when it remains operative.

Core Idea
Explicit statement trumps circumstantial indication because certainty overrides doubt.

This lesson introduces a foundational Hanafi maxim from the Majalla (Article 13) that governs how we weigh evidence of intent when both explicit speech and circumstantial clues exist. Understanding this maxim is crucial for a mufti who must resolve apparent conflicts between what is said and what is implied. The maxim clarifies that explicit statement (qawl or kitaba) is certain, while circumstantial indication (dalalat al-hal) is doubtful; thus, when they conflict, the explicit statement prevails.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its rationale
Read the qawida: 'لا عبرة للدلالة في مقابلة التصريح'. Understand that indication is only a substitute for explicit speech when the latter is absent. The rationale is that explicit statement yields certainty (yaqin), while indication yields doubt (zann).
The maxim applies to all expressions of will: offer, acceptance, permission, prohibition, etc.
2
Identify the two elements: explicit statement and circumstantial indication
Explicit statement (taṣrīḥ) includes both speech and writing. Circumstantial indication (dalālat al-ḥāl) includes silence, consistent practice, or any contextual clue. The maxim applies when both exist simultaneously and contradict each other.
Silence is a common form of indication; it is not inherently permission but can be interpreted as such in context.
3
Apply to the sale examples
Consider two scenarios: (a) Seller silently watches buyer take possession without prior explicit prohibition → silence indicates permission. (b) Seller explicitly forbids taking before payment, then buyer takes while seller is silent → explicit statement overrides indication, so silence is not permission.
The key difference is the presence or absence of an explicit statement from the seller.
4
Apply to the waqf examples
When the endowment deed is lost, the consistent practice of trustees is taken as indication of the endower's intent. But if a registered deed exists with explicit conditions, the practice is ignored. This shows that explicit written terms override subsequent conduct.
The maxim protects the integrity of written documents against later inconsistent behavior.
5
Handle the istithnā' (exception): earlier indication vs. later explicit statement
If indication alone (e.g., silence) established a ruling first, and then a later explicit statement contradicts it, the later statement is disregarded. Example: seller silently watches buyer take possession → ruling of permission is established. Seller later verbally objects → objection has no effect.
This prevents people from unilaterally overturning rights already established by their own conduct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا عبرة للدلالة في مقابلة التصريح (م/١٣)
هذه القاعدة مجالها في الأحكام المتعلقة بالتعبير عن الإرادة، من إيجاب وقبول وإذن ،ومنع ورضى ورفض، ونحو ذلك .
إن تعبير دلالة الحال عن الإرادة إنما هو خلف عن الكلام الصريح عند عدمه. فإذا وجد التصريح بخلاف ما تفيده دلالة الحال لم يبق للدلالة هذه الخلفية والنيابة في التعبير، لأن دلالة التصريح يقينية، ودلالة الحال والقرائن محل شك.
ولا فرق في التصريح بين القول والكتابة.
وعلى هذا نص الفقهاء أن للبائع حق احتباس المبيع عن المشتري حتى يدفع هذا إليه الثمن (مالم يكن مؤجّلاً) فإذا قبض المشتري المبيع قبل دفع ثمنه والبائع ناظر ساكت، اعتبر ذلك إذناً منه دلالة، فيسقط حقه في احتباس المبيع، وليس له إلا مطالبة المشتري بالثمن. لكن إذا صرح البائع للمشتري أنه لا يسمح له بأخذ المبيع قبل دفع الثمن، فأخذه المشتري قبل الدفع والبائع ساكت، لا يعتبر عندئذ سكوته إذناً منه، بل له استرداد المبيع، واحتباسه عن المشتري حتى يدفع الثمن.
- وكذلك قالوا : إذا فقد صَلَّ الوقفية يتبع في مصارف غلة الوقف تعامل المتولين السابقين فيعمل به لأنَّ تسلسل عملهم على طريقة واحدة فيه دلالة على أن هذه الطريقة هي التي شرطها الواقف. ولكن إذا وجد للوقف صَكَ مُسجّل، والمتولون يعملون بخلافه، فلا عبرة لتعاملهم المخالف لصراحة شرط الواقف. يتضح مما سلف أن المراد بهذه القاعدة حالة تعارض الدلالة تصريح قائم في وقت واحد. أما لو وجدت الدلالة وحدها وأخذت حكمها، ثم وجد تصريح متأخر بخلاف مدلولها، فلا عبرة عندئذ لهذا التصريح، ولا يرفع الحكم الذي تقرّر بتلك الدلالة، كتصريح البائع للمشتري بعدم موافقته على قبض المبيع قبل نقد الثمن، بعد ما أخذه المشتري والبائع شاهد ساكت؛ فتصريحه عندئذ لا عبرة له .
وقد تقدَّم لنا الاستشهاد بهذه القاعدة في بعض المناسبات فلتنظر .(E/V. :)
No weight is given to indication in the face of explicit statement (Majalla Article 13). This maxim applies to rulings related to expression of will, such as offer, acceptance, permission, prohibition, consent, rejection, and the like. Indeed, expression of will by circumstantial indication is a substitute for explicit speech in its absence. So if explicit statement exists contrary to what the circumstantial indication suggests, the indication loses its substitutive and representational role in expression, because explicit indication is certain while circumstantial indication and evidence are subject to doubt. There is no difference in explicit statement between speech and writing. On this basis, the jurists ruled that the seller has the right to retain the sold item from the buyer until the buyer pays the price (unless it is deferred). If the buyer takes possession of the sold item before paying its price while the seller looks on silently, that is considered permission by indication, and his right to retain the sold item lapses; he may only demand the price from the buyer. But if the seller explicitly tells the buyer that he does not permit him to take the sold item before paying the price, and the buyer takes it before payment while the seller is silent, then his silence is not considered permission; rather, he may reclaim the sold item and retain it from the buyer until he pays the price. Similarly, they said: When the endowment deed is lost, one follows the practice of previous trustees in how the endowment's proceeds are disbursed, because their consistent practice indicates that this method is what the endower stipulated. But if a registered deed exists, and the trustees act contrary to it, no weight is given to their practice contradicting the explicit condition of the endower. It becomes clear from the above that what is meant by this maxim is a situation where indication and explicit statement conflict at the same time. But if indication alone existed and its ruling was applied, then later an explicit statement contrary to its implication appears, then this explicit statement is disregarded and does not overturn the ruling established by that indication, as in the case of the seller explicitly telling the buyer after the buyer has taken possession with the seller silently watching that he does not agree to the taking of the item before payment; his explicit statement at that time is disregarded. This maxim has been cited previously on some occasions, so refer to it.
Source pages p. 920

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi legal maxim known as Majalla Article 13: "No weight is given to indication in the face of explicit statement." The passage type is a qawida definition with applications. The author first defines the scope of the maxim: it governs expressions of will (offer, acceptance, permission, etc.). The rationale is that explicit statement (qawl or kitaba) is certain (yaqini), while circumstantial indication (dalalat al-hal) and contextual clues are doubtful. Therefore, indication only serves as a substitute when explicit statement is absent; if explicit statement exists contrary to the indication, the indication is disregarded. The author then provides two illustrative furuat (applications): First, in a sale contract, the seller's silence after the buyer takes possession without payment normally indicates permission, but if the seller explicitly forbade taking before payment, his silence does not imply permission. Second, regarding waqf, if the deed is lost, the consistent practice of trustees is followed as indication of the endower's condition; but if a registered deed exists explicitly stating otherwise, the practice is ignored. The passage also clarifies a critical refinement: the maxim applies only when indication and explicit statement coexist at the same time. If indication alone established a ruling, a later explicit statement contrary to that ruling is ineffective – the prior ruling stands. This is demonstrated by the seller who silently watches the buyer take the item, then later verbally objects; his objection is disregarded because the earlier indication (silence) already established permission. This distinction prevents legal uncertainty.

Key Terms
دلالة الحال (dalālat al-ḥāl): Circumstantial indication that conveys meaning without explicit speech; a substitute for explicit expression when absent.
التصريح (al-taṣrīḥ): Explicit statement, whether spoken or written, that clearly expresses intent.
الخلفية (al-khilafiyya): The role of being a substitute or representative for explicit speech.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'No weight to indication in the face of explicit statement,' if a seller explicitly prohibits the buyer from taking the goods before payment, but then silently watches the buyer take them, what is the ruling?
A) The seller's silence is deemed permission, and he can only claim the price.
B) The seller's silence is not considered permission; he may reclaim the goods.
C) The buyer automatically owns the goods regardless.
D) The contract becomes void.

Key Takeaway
Explicit statement always prevails over circumstantial indication when both exist simultaneously, but if indication alone established a ruling, a later explicit statement cannot overturn it.

Silence as Expression: The Maxim and Its Istihsan Exception
Explanation and exceptions of Majalla Article 57

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'silence in a context requiring explanation is an explanation' to determine when silence constitutes acknowledgment in financial transactions, and identify the exception based on istihsan for close relatives.

Core Idea
Silence can speak — but only when circumstances demand a response; otherwise, it remains ambiguous.

This lesson unpacks a key Hanafi legal maxim: silence in a context where speech is required can be treated as an explanation. Originating from Majalla Article 57, the maxim balances two opposing considerations: the general principle that silence does not imply consent, and the need to prevent fraud and manipulation, especially among close relatives. As a mufti, you must recognize when silence carries legal weight and when it does not.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its rank
Read the maxim: 'No statement is attributed to a silent person, but silence in a situation requiring explanation is an explanation.' It is a wusta (intermediate) qawida governing evidence and acknowledgments.
The second part is the exception that creates a presumption when circumstances demand speech.
2
Identify the default ruling
Default: silence does not constitute an acknowledgment. The text gives the rationale that the absence of speech is certain, while the indication of silence is doubtful unless supported by preponderant evidence.
This protects the silent person's rights.
3
Analyze the furu' case
Apply the rule: a person sees his property being sold by a stranger and remains silent. He may later claim the property; silence is not an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership nor an approval of the sale.
4
Identify the istithna' and its rationale
Exception: if the seller is the husband or a close relative (mahram), the silence is considered an acknowledgment. This is based on istihsan to prevent deception and manipulation by relatives, as close association provides a strong circumstantial evidence that the claimant would have spoken if he had a right.
Qiyas would treat relative and stranger alike, but istihsan departs from qiyas.
5
Apply the maxim to a new scenario
Consider a modern case: A sees his cousin B selling a car that A claims to have co-owned. A says nothing. B sells to C. Later A sues C. The mufti would check if the cousin is mahram; if yes, A's silence is an acknowledgment, and the claim fails. If not, A may proceed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۱۳/۸۱ - ز - لا يُنسب إلى ساكت قول، ولكن السكوت معرض الحاجة إلى البيان بيان (م/٥٧). لأن عدم القول هو المتيقن ودلالة السكوت مشكوك فيها ما لم تدعمها قرائن مرجحة : فلو رأى الشخص بعض ماله في يد غيره يبيعه وظل ساكتاً، كان له أن يدعي به بعد ذلك، ولا يعتبر سكوته عند البيع اعترافاً بالملكية للبائع، ولا إجازة للبيع (١) (١) هذا ما لم يكن البائع زوجاً أو قريباً محرماً من الشخص المدعي. فإن كان البائع زوجاً أو قريباً يعتبر سكوت زوجه أو قريبه عند البيع اعترافاً منه بأنه لا حق له في المبيع. وهذا استحسان منعاً للتلاعب والتغرير بواسطة الأقارب، ولأن الخلطة بينهما قرينة تجعل من سكوته اعترافاً بنفاذ البيع والقياس عدم الفرق بين القريب والغريب. من شرح والذي رحمه الله على القواعد).
13/81 - Z - No statement is attributed to a silent person, but silence in a situation that requires explanation is an explanation (Majalla Article 57). Because the absence of speech is certain, and the indication of silence is doubtful unless supported by preponderant evidence. For example, if a person sees some of his property in the hand of another selling it and remains silent, he may afterwards claim it; his silence at the time of sale is not considered an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership, nor an approval of the sale. (1) (1) This applies unless the seller is the husband or a close relative (mahram) of the claimant. If the seller is the husband or relative, the silence of the spouse or relative at the time of sale is considered an acknowledgment that he has no right in the sold item. This is istihsan to prevent manipulation and deception by relatives, because close association is a preponderant circumstance that makes his silence an acknowledgment of the validity of the sale, while qiyas would have made no distinction between a relative and a stranger. From the commentary of [al-Qarafi?] on the Qawa'id.
Source pages p. 921

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi legal maxim of Majalla Article 57. The passage type is a qawida definition with applications and an exception. The maxim states: 'No statement is attributed to a silent person, but silence in a situation that requires explanation is an explanation.' The author provides the rationale: the absence of speech is certain, while the indication of silence is doubtful unless supported by preponderant circumstances. The main application: if a person sees his property being sold by someone else and remains silent, this silence does not constitute acknowledgment of the seller's ownership or approval of the sale; he may later claim his property. This is the default ruling. However, the author notes an important exception made by istihsan: if the seller is the husband or a close relative (mahram) of the silent claimant, then the silence is considered an acknowledgment that the claimant has no right to the property. This exception prevents abuse among relatives, as close ties create a strong presumption that the claimant would speak up if he had a right. The comment notes that this is a departure from qiyas (analogy), which would treat relative and stranger alike. This istihsan ruling reflects the Hanafi concern for preventing manipulation through familial relationships.

Key Terms
السكوت في معرض الحاجة إلى البيان (al-sukūt fī maʿraḍ al-ḥāja ilā al-bayān): Silence in a context where speech is naturally expected, which then becomes a form of expression.
الاستحسان (al-istiḥsān): Juristic preference that sets aside the result of qiyas in favor of a ruling that better serves justice and prevents harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under the maxim about silence, what is the ruling if a person sees a stranger selling the person's property and remains silent?
A) The silence constitutes acknowledgment of the stranger's ownership.
B) The silence is considered approval of the sale.
C) The person may later claim the property from the stranger.
D) The sale is void automatically.

Key Takeaway
Silence can be considered an explanation only when the context demands speech, and this is especially applied to close relatives via istihsan to prevent deception.

لا عبرة للتوهم: Maxim of Disregarding Conjecture
Definition and single furu' of the maxim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the maxim, DEFINE conjecture (tawahhum), and APPLY it to a furu' case from Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
No legal ruling is based on remote conjecture; only probable possibilities matter.

This lesson introduces a foundational Hanafi legal maxim that eliminates speculative reasoning from the adjudication process. The maxim 'لا عبرة للتوهم' protects the integrity of judgments by ensuring that only realistic probabilities are considered. You will learn its precise definition and one key application in inheritance and testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its source
Note that the maxim is number 74 of the Majalla. It is a universal principle (kulliya) of intermediate rank.
2
Define the key term 'توهم'
Tawahhum is a remote mental probability that rarely occurs. It is weaker than doubt (shakk).
3
State the legal effect
No ruling is based on it; it does not prevent judgment, nor does it delay rights.
This applies across all chapters of fiqh.
4
Examine the application example
If witnesses testify that the deceased's heirs are limited to certain individuals, the remote possibility of other hidden heirs is disregarded.
This comes from inheritance and testimony.
5
Connect to related maxims
It parallels 'اليقين لا يزول بالشك' and complements 'الأصل بقاء ما كان على ما كان'.
These together reinforce certainty over speculation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
14/81 - ح - لا عبرة للتوهم» (م/٧٤).
المراد بالتوهم الاحتمال العقلي البعيد النادر الحصول. فهذا لا يبنى عليه حكم، ولا يمنع القضاء، ولا يؤخر الحقوق.
فلو شهد الشهود بانحصار إرث المتوفى بوارث أو بورثة معينين
14/81 - H - 'No consideration is given to conjecture' (M/74). What is meant by conjecture is a remote mental probability that rarely occurs. So no ruling is based on it, it does not prevent judgment, and it does not delay rights. If witnesses testify that the estate of the deceased is limited to a specific heir or heirs...
Source pages p. 922

Commentary
This passage presents the maxim 'لا عبرة للتوهم' (No consideration is given to conjecture) which is maxim 74 of the Majalla. It defines 'التوهم' (conjecture) as a remote rational probability that is rarely realized. Such probability is legally irrelevant: it cannot serve as a basis for a ruling, prevent a judgment from being issued, or delay the enforcement of rights. The text gives a partial example: if witnesses testify that the decedent's estate is limited to certain heirs, any far-fetched possibility of other hidden heirs is disregarded. The maxim ensures that legal proceedings are not hindered by speculative doubts. This principle is foundational in Hanafi usul, as it promotes certainty and efficiency in law.

Key Terms
توهم (tawahhum): A remote mental probability that is rarely realized; conjecture without substantial evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The possibility that a missing person might still be alive after 80 years.
B) The possibility that a witness might be mistaken despite credible testimony.
C) The possibility that a thief might have returned stolen goods after being caught.
D) The possibility that a contract might be invalid due to a hidden defect in the object.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'لا عبرة للتوهم' ensures that legal rulings are based on reality, not remote speculation.

The Maxim 'La Ibra li al-Tawahhum': No Regard for Baseless Conjecture
Explains the maxim 'La Ibra li al-Tawahhum' with applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the maxim 'لا عبرة للتوهم', identify its scope, and apply it to cases where baseless conjecture is disregarded in legal rulings.

Core Idea
Baseless conjecture does not delay justice: reliable evidence overrides remote possibilities.

This lesson examines the Hanafi legal maxim that baseless conjecture (tawahhum) is given no weight in law. Derived from the Majalla (Article 74), this maxim ensures that legal proceedings are not stalled by fanciful possibilities. We will explore its definition, rationale, and three practical applications in inheritance, bankruptcy, and witness testimony.

Guided Path
1
Define tawahhum
Understand that tawahhum is a remote rational possibility that rarely occurs, as opposed to shakk (doubt) or wahm (probability).
2
State the maxim and its legal effect
The maxim 'لا عبرة للتوهم' means no ruling is built upon conjecture, it does not prevent judgment, nor does it delay rights.
3
Apply to inheritance case
If witnesses testify that the deceased has only specific heirs, judgment is given to those heirs; the mere possibility of unknown heirs is disregarded.
Fara'id chapter
4
Apply to bankruptcy case
If witnesses testify that a bankrupt has no other creditors, property is given to the known creditors; possibility of unknown creditors is ignored.
Ifals chapter
5
Apply to witness credibility
Testimony of trustworthy witnesses is accepted; the possibility of error or lying is not considered unless evidence arises.
Shahada chapter

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٤/٨١ - ح - لا عبرة للتوهم» (م/٧٤).
المراد بالتوهم الاحتمال العقلي البعيد النادر الحصول. فهذا لا يبنى عليه حكم، ولا يمنع القضاء، ولا يؤخر الحقوق.
فلو شهد الشهود بانحصار إرث المتوفى بوارث أو بورثة معينين وقالوا: لا تعلم له وارثاً غيرهم يقضى لهم بالإرث، ولا عبرة لاحتمال ظهور وارث آخر، لأنَّ ذلك موهوم، فلا يعوق القضاء.
- وكذلك لو أثبت الدائنون ديونهم على مفلس أو على تركته، وقال الشهود لا نعلم له غريماً آخر يعطى المال للحاضرين.
- وكذا أمر الشهود الثقات : يُعتمد على شهادتهم ويقضى بها، ولا عبرة لاحتمال خطئهم أو كذبهم .
توهم.
14/81 - H - 'No regard is given to baseless conjecture' (Article 74). What is meant by 'baseless conjecture' is a remote rational probability that rarely occurs. So upon this no ruling is built, nor does it prevent judgment, nor delay rights. For if witnesses testify that the inheritance of the deceased is limited to a specific heir or heirs, and they say: 'We do not know of any other heir for him,' judgment is given to them regarding inheritance, and no regard is given to the possibility of the appearance of another heir, because that is conjectural, so it does not hinder judgment. Likewise, if creditors prove their debts against a bankrupt or his estate, and the witnesses say 'We do not know of any other creditor for him,' the property is given to those present. Similarly, regarding trustworthy witnesses: their testimony is accepted and judgment is based on it, and no regard is given to the possibility of their error or lying. Baseless conjecture.
Source pages p. 922

Commentary
This passage presents the legal maxim that baseless conjecture (tawahhum) is ignored in legal matters. The maxim is from the Majalla Article 74. The author clarifies that tawahhum is a remote rational possibility that is unlikely to occur. Such a possibility does not form the basis of a ruling, does not prevent a court from issuing a judgment, and does not delay the enforcement of rights. Three examples are given. First, inheritance: if witnesses testify that the deceased has only specific heirs, the court gives them the inheritance despite the abstract possibility that an unknown heir might appear. Second, bankruptcy: if witnesses testify that there are no other creditors, the property is given to the known creditors. Third, witness credibility: the court accepts the testimony of reliable witnesses and does not consider the abstract possibility that they might be mistaken or lying. The passage concludes with the word 'tawahhum' (baseless conjecture) as a summary.

Key Terms
التوهم (al-tawahhum): A remote rational possibility that rarely occurs, which is disregarded in law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The judge must investigate further because there might be unknown heirs.
B) The judge should accept the testimony and grant the inheritance to the claimant.
C) The judge should disregard the testimony because it is based on conjecture.
D) The judge should require additional witnesses to confirm the absence of other heirs.

Key Takeaway
Baseless conjecture (tawahhum) is ignored in Islamic law; reliable evidence takes priority over remote possibilities.

The Third Major Maxim: No Harm and No Reciprocating Harm
Stating the maxim as a memorization anchor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the third major maxim of Islamic jurisprudence, explain the difference between 'darar' and 'dirar', and identify its scope and application in fatwa.

Core Idea
No harm and no reciprocating harm — a foundational shield for all transactions.

This lesson introduces one of the five major comprehensive maxims (al-qawaid al-kulliya al-kubra) that underpin Islamic law. The maxim 'la darar wa la dirar' is a cornerstone for protecting individuals and society from injury. Understanding it is essential for any mufti tasked with issuing sound fatwas.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Memorize the Arabic text: لا ضَرَرَ ولا ضرار. Translate it: 'No harm and no reciprocating harm.' Note that it is the third major maxim, as cited in the Majalla (Article 19).
The maxim is often expressed in two parts: 'la darar' (no causing harm) and 'la dirar' (no responding with harm).
2
Distinguish darar from dirar
Understand that 'darar' refers to initiating harm, while 'dirar' refers to reciprocating harm. Both are prohibited. The second part emphasizes that even if someone harms you, you may not respond with harm in a way that violates the law.
This distinction prevents a cycle of retaliation.
3
Identify its rank and scope
This maxim is one of the five major comprehensive maxims (al-qawaid al-kubra) that apply across all chapters of fiqh. It governs every area from worship to transactions to criminal law.
Its authority is universal within the Hanafi school.
4
Relate to other maxims
This maxim is closely related to 'al-darar yuzal' (harm must be removed). While 'la darar wa la dirar' prevents harm, 'al-darar yuzal' directs removal of existing harm. Together they form a complete system.
Both are among the five major maxims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٨/٨١ - القاعدة الثالثة : لا ضَرَرَ ولا ضرار» (م/ ١٩).
18/81 - The Third Maxim: No harm and no reciprocating harm (Article 19).
Source pages p. 924

Commentary
This is one of the five major comprehensive maxims of Islamic jurisprudence. It means that causing harm to others is prohibited, and likewise, retaliating with harm in response to harm is not allowed. The maxim serves as a foundation for many rulings in all areas of fiqh. However, the text in this passage only states the maxim without elaboration, as the author is listing the maxims of the Majalla. Further explanation of this maxim is to follow in subsequent pages.

Key Terms
ضرر (darar): Harm or injury
ضرار (dirar): Reciprocating harm; responding to harm with harm

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the lesson, what is the third major maxim of Islamic jurisprudence?
A) الأمور بمقاصدها
B) لا ضرر ولا ضرار
C) الضرر يزال
D) المشقة تجلب التيسير

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'La darar wa la dirar' prohibits both initial harm and retaliatory harm, serving as a foundational principle across all areas of fiqh.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
8 min
The Major Maxim: No Harm and No Reciprocating Harm
Explanation of the major maxim with its evidence and applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'لا ضرر ولا ضرار' to determine permissible and impermissible actions in torts and penalties, and identify its exception for legitimate punishments.

Core Idea
Harm is prohibited unconditionally; only the greater harm justifies a lesser harm, and vengeance is not a valid remedy.

This lesson unpacks one of the five major maxims of Hanafi fiqh, grounded in a prophetic hadith. You will learn its precise meaning, its scope covering private and public harm, and its key applications: choosing the lesser harm, the justification of penal sanctions, and the prohibition of retaliation without compensation. Understanding this maxim is essential for a mufti to navigate liability and punishment.

Guided Path
1
Understand the wording and meaning
Read the maxim 'لا ضرر ولا ضرار'. Note the definitions: الضرر is inflicting harm on another; الضرار is reciprocating harm for harm (vengeance).
The negation is general, covering all forms of unjust harm.
2
Identify the prophetic evidence
Recognize that the maxim is a hadith narrated by Malik, Ibn Majah, and al-Daraqutni (hasan rank). This is a dalil naqli that gives the maxim authority.
The hadith is the primary source; Quranic and Sunnah texts support it.
3
Derive the two key implications
First, harm must be prevented before occurrence and removed after occurrence. Second, reciprocating harm (pure vengeance) is prohibited unless absolutely necessary and more beneficial than alternative remedies.
The maxim requires choosing the lesser of two evils when harm cannot be entirely prevented.
4
Apply to furuat (sub-cases)
Work through the three furuat: (1) choosing the lesser harm, (2) imposing prescribed punishments (حدود) does not violate the maxim because it prevents greater harm, (3) destroying property in retaliation is not allowed; compensation is superior.
Each furu' illustrates how the maxim governs real rulings.
5
Recognize exceptions and related maxims
The exception to the maxim is legitimate prescribed punishments, which are justice and prevent greater harm. The complementary maxim 'الضرر يزال' ('Harm must be removed') reinforces this.
A mufti must know when the maxim applies and when it is overridden by necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۱۸/۸۱ - القاعدة الثالثة : لا ضَرَرَ ولا ضرار» (م/ ١٩).

الضرر إلحاق مفسدة بالغير. والضرار مقابلة الضرر بالضرر.
هذه القاعدة بلفظها نص حديث نبوي في رتبة الحسن، رواه مالك الموطأ، وأخرجه ابن ماجه والدارقطني في سننهما .
في
وهذه القاعدة من أركان الشريعة وتشهد لها نصوص كثيرة في الكتاب والسنة، وهي أساس لمنع الفعل الضار، وترتيب نتائجه في التعويض المالي والعقوبة، كما أنها سند لمبدأ الاستصلاح في جلب المصالح ودرء وهي عدة الفقهاء وعمدتهم وميزانهم في طريق تقرير الأحكام
المفاسد. الشرعية للحوادث.
أ - ونصها ينفي الضّرر نفياً فيوجب منعه مطلقاً، ويشمل الضرر الخاص والعام. ويشمل ذلك دفعه قبل الوقوع بطرق الوقاية الممكنة، ورفعه بعد الوقوع بما يمكن من التدابير التي تزيل آثاره وتمنع تكراره. وتدل أيضاً على وجوب اختيار أهونِ الشَّرين لدفع أعظمهما، لأنَّ في ذلك تخفيفاً للضرر عندما لا يمكن منعه بتاتاً.
ومن ثم كان إنزال العقوبات المشروعة بالمجرمين لا ينافي هذه القاعدة، وإن ترتّب عليها ضررٌ بهم، لأن فيها عدلاً ودفعاً لضرر أعم
وأعظم.
ب - والمقصود بمنع الضرار نفي فكرة الثأر المحض الذي يزيد في الضرر ولا يفيد سوى توسيع دائرته، لأن الإضرار ولو على سبيل المقابلة - لا يجوز أن يكون هدفاً مقصوداً وطريقاً عامة، وإنما يلجأ إليه اضطراراً، عندما لا يكون غيره من طرق التلافي والقمع أفضل منه وأنفع .
فمن أتلف مال غيره مثلاً لا يجوز أن يقابل بإتلاف ماله، لأن ذلك توسيع للضرر بلا منفعة وأفضل منه تضمين المتلف قيمة ما أتلف، فإن فيه نفعاً بتعويض المضرور وتحويل الضرر نفسه إلى حساب المعتدي، فإنه سيان بالنسبة إليه إتلاف ماله وإعطاؤه للمضرور لترميم الضرر الأول.
۹۹۰
18/81 - The third maxim: No harm and no reciprocating harm. Harm is inflicting mischief upon another. Reciprocating harm is returning harm for harm. This maxim in its wording is the text of a prophetic hadith of hasan rank, narrated by Malik in al-Muwatta, and by Ibn Majah and al-Daraqutni in their Sunans. This maxim is one of the pillars of the Sharia, and many texts from Quran and Sunnah testify to it. It is the basis for preventing harmful acts, and arranging their consequences in financial compensation and punishment, and it is also a support for the principle of istislah in bringing benefits and repelling harms. It is the weapon of the jurists, their mainstay, and their scale in determining legal rulings for events. A - Its text negates harm absolutely, requiring its prevention unconditionally, including private and public harm. This includes preventing it before occurrence by possible preventive means, and removing it after occurrence by possible measures that eliminate its effects and prevent recurrence. It also indicates the obligation to choose the lesser of two evils to repel the greater, because that is a mitigation of harm when it cannot be prevented entirely. Hence, imposing prescribed punishments on criminals does not contradict this maxim, even if it causes them harm, because it involves justice and repelling a greater and more widespread harm. B - The purpose of prohibiting reciprocating harm is to negate the idea of pure vengeance, which increases harm and only widens its circle, because inflicting harm even in retaliation is not permissible as a deliberate goal and general method; rather, it is resorted to out of necessity when there is no other method of rectification and suppression better and more beneficial. For example, if someone destroys another's property, it is not permissible to retaliate by destroying his property, because that is an expansion of harm without benefit. Better than that is to oblige the destroyer to pay the value of what he destroyed, for therein is the benefit of compensating the harmed party and transferring the harm itself to the aggressor's account, since it is the same to him whether his property is destroyed or given to the harmed party to repair the initial harm.
Source pages p. 924

Commentary
This passage presents one of the five major maxims of fiqh, based on a hadith. It opens by defining 'harm' (ضرر) as inflicting harm on others, and 'reciprocating harm' (ضرار) as returning harm for harm. The author then provides the hadith source and notes that the maxim is a pillar of Sharia. The commentary explains two key applications: first, it requires preventing harm before it occurs and removing it after; and it commands choosing the lesser harm. This is why penal sanctions are not prohibited—they prevent greater societal harm. Second, it prohibits pure vengeance (ضرار); for example, if someone destroys your property, you cannot destroy his property in retaliation. Instead, financial compensation (تضمين) is superior because it compensates the victim and transfers the loss to the aggressor. This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on compensation over vengeance.

Key Terms
الضرر (al-ḍarar): Inflicting mischief (مفسدة) upon another
الضرار (al-ḍirār): Reciprocating harm for harm, i.e., vengeance without necessity
الاستصلاح (al-istiṣlāḥ): Seeking public benefit (مصلحة) in legal rulings

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is imposing a prescribed punishment (like lashing) on a criminal not a violation of the maxim 'لا ضرر ولا ضرار'?
A) Because the criminal deserves it
B) Because it prevents a greater harm to society
C) Because it is specifically allowed by the hadith
D) Because the harm is temporary

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'لا ضرر ولا ضرار' prohibits unjust harm and vengeance, requiring compensation and permitting only the lesser harm to prevent greater harm.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

The Maxim 'Harm Must Be Removed' and Its Applications
Explaining the maxim 'harm must be removed' with its fiqhi applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-darar yuzal' to identify when harm must be prevented or removed, and distinguish it from prohibited retaliatory harm (al-dirar).

Core Idea
Harm must be removed, but not by greater harm—qisas is the exception that restores balance.

This lesson introduces one of the five universal maxims of Hanafi fiqh: 'الضرر يزال' (Harm must be removed). Based on a prophetic hadith, this maxim underpins countless rulings across all chapters of fiqh. We will explore its textual basis, its scope, and how it is applied in real cases. Understanding this maxim equips you to derive and evaluate rulings in contracts, torts, and criminal law, and to distinguish between permissible removal of harm and prohibited retaliation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its textual basis
Read the passage and note the exact wording of the maxim and the hadith cited as its source. Understand the isnad and rank (hasan).
The hadith is narrated by Malik, Ibn Majah, and al-Darqutni.
2
Define key terms: darar vs. dirar
Distinguish between 'الضرر' (harm inflicted on another) and 'الضرار' (meeting harm with harm). The maxim prohibits the former and restricts the latter to cases of necessity like qisas.
3
Analyze the maxim's implications
The maxim requires preventing harm before it occurs and removing it after. It also commands choosing the lesser harm when total avoidance is impossible. Note that prescribed punishments do not violate the maxim because they serve a greater good.
4
Explore the exception: qisas
Understand why retaliation for murder and bodily harm is allowed: it is the only effective deterrent and does not expand harm. Contrast with property damage where compensation is preferred.
5
Apply the maxim to the furuat
Work through each of the four applications listed. For each case, identify the harm being removed and how the ruling prevents or removes it.
These are from Ijarah, Buyu', and Kafalah chapters.

الضرر إلحاق مفسدة بالغير. والضرار مقابلة الضرر بالضرر.
هذه القاعدة بلفظها نص حديث نبوي في رتبة الحسن، رواه مالك الموطأ، وأخرجه ابن ماجه والدارقطني في سننهما .
وهذه القاعدة من أركان الشريعة وتشهد لها نصوص كثيرة في الكتاب والسنة، وهي أساس لمنع الفعل الضار، وترتيب نتائجه في التعويض المالي والعقوبة، كما أنها سند لمبدأ الاستصلاح في جلب المصالح ودرء المفاسد. وهي عدة الفقهاء وعمدتهم وميزانهم في طريق تقرير الأحكام الشرعية للحوادث.
أ - ونصها ينفي الضّرر نفياً فيوجب منعه مطلقاً، ويشمل الضرر الخاص والعام. ويشمل ذلك دفعه قبل الوقوع بطرق الوقاية الممكنة، ورفعه بعد الوقوع بما يمكن من التدابير التي تزيل آثاره وتمنع تكراره. وتدل أيضاً على وجوب اختيار أهونِ الشَّرين لدفع أعظمهما، لأنَّ في ذلك تخفيفاً للضرر عندما لا يمكن منعه بتاتاً.

فمن أتلف مال غيره مثلاً لا يجوز أن يقابل بإتلاف ماله، لأن ذلك توسيع للضرر بلا منفعة وأفضل منه تضمين المتلف قيمة ما أتلف، فإن فيه نفعاً بتعويض المضرور وتحويل الضرر نفسه إلى حساب المعتدي، فإنه سيان بالنسبة إليه إتلاف ماله وإعطاؤه للمضرور لترميم الضرر الأول.
فأصبحت مقابلة الإتلاف بالإتلاف مجرد حماقة
وذلك بخلاف الجناية على النفس والبدن مما شرع فيه القصاص. فمن قتل يُقتل، ومن قَطَعَ يُقطع، لأنَّ هذه الجنايات لا يقمعها إلا عقوبة من جنسها، كي يعلم الجاني أنه في النهاية كمن يعتدي على نفسه. وكل عقوبة أخرى لا تعيد للمجني عليه ما فقد من نفس أو عضو، فتظل حزازات النفوس كامنة تندفع إلى الثأر، وتجر وراءها ذيول الويلات والفساد فيبقى طريق القصاص في الجنايات على النقوس والأعضاء أنفع، لأنه أعدل وأقمع.
أما إتلافُ المال، فإنَّ التضمين فيه هو التدبير السديد المفيد دون مقابلة الإتلاف بالإتلاف.

۲ - ولو باع شيئاً مما يسرع إليه الفساد كالفواكه مثلاً، وغاب المشتري قبل نقد الثمن وقبض المبيع وخيف فساده، فللبائع أن يفسخ البيع ويبيع من غيره دفعاً لضرره (رد المحتار من متفرقات البيوع).
