Chapter Ten: Universal Juridical Maxims
Harm is the infliction of harm upon another. And mutual harm is the retaliation of harm with harm.
This maxim in its wording is a text of a prophetic hadith of the rank of hasan, narrated by Malik in the Muwatta, and extracted by Ibn Majah and al-Darqutni in their Sunan.
This maxim is one of the pillars of the Sharia, and numerous texts from the Book and Sunnah testify to it. It is the foundation for preventing harmful acts and arranging their consequences in financial compensation and punishment, and it is also a support for the principle of istislah (public interest) in attracting benefits and warding off harms. It is the toolkit of the jurists, their mainstay, and their balance in the way of determining the legal rulings for events.
A - Its text negates harm absolutely, thus requiring its prevention unconditionally, and it includes private and public harm. That includes preventing it before occurrence through possible preventive means, and removing it after occurrence through whatever measures are possible that remove its effects and prevent its recurrence. It also indicates the obligation of choosing the lesser of two evils to ward off the greater, because in that is a reduction of harm when it cannot be prevented entirely.
Hence, the infliction of prescribed punishments upon criminals does not contradict this maxim, even if it results in harm to them, because it contains justice and the warding off of a more general and greater harm.
B - What is meant by prohibiting mutual harm is the denial of the idea of pure revenge that increases harm and only serves to expand its circle. Because causing harm, even by way of retaliation, is not permissible as a deliberate goal and a general method; rather, it is resorted to out of necessity when there is no other method of redress and suppression that is better and more beneficial.
So, for example, one who destroys another's property cannot be met with the destruction of his property, because that is an expansion of harm without benefit. Better than that is holding the destroyer liable for the value of what he destroyed, for in that there is benefit by compensating the harmed person and transferring the harm itself to the account of the aggressor. It is the same for him whether his property is destroyed or he gives its value to the harmed person to repair the initial harm.
Thus, meeting destruction with destruction becomes mere folly.
This is contrary to offenses against life and limb for which retaliation is prescribed. So whoever kills is killed, and whoever cuts off is cut off, because these offenses are not suppressed except by a punishment of their own kind, so that the criminal knows that he is in the end like one who has wronged himself. Any other punishment does not restore to the victim what he has lost of life or limb; thus, the rancor of souls remains latent, driving them to revenge, and dragging behind it tails of calamities and corruption. Therefore, the path of retaliation in offenses against lives and limbs remains more beneficial because it is more just and more suppressive.
As for the destruction of property, liability (damages) in it is the sound and beneficial measure, not meeting destruction with destruction.
Upon this maxim the jurists have built countless rulings from various chapters.
They ruled that:
1 - If the term of a lease of agricultural land expires before the crop is ready to harvest, it remains in the lessee's possession with a comparable rent until it is harvested, to prevent harm to the lessee from uprooting the crop before its time.
2 - If someone sells something that quickly spoils, such as fruits, and the buyer delays in paying the price and taking delivery, and it is feared that it will spoil, the seller may rescind the sale and sell it to someone else to ward off harm to himself. (Radd al-Muhtar from miscellaneous sales sections).
3 - If someone buys something and leases it, then discovers an old defect in it, that is considered an excuse for him to rescind the lease to enable him to return it to its seller, removing harm from himself. A lease is rescinded for excuses as mentioned earlier (vol. 7 p. 40).
4 - All cases of being compelled to pay off a debt for another without his permission, where the payer is not considered a donor; rather, he has the right to recourse against the debtor for what he paid from his liability, to prevent harm to himself, as previously mentioned, are among the branches of this maxim.
Source pages p. 925

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm introduces the universal maxim 'الضرر يزال' (Harm must be removed). The author begins by defining two key terms: 'الضرر' (harm) as the infliction of a harmful consequence upon another, and 'الضرار' (mutual harm) as meeting harm with harm. He then establishes the textual basis of the maxim in a hadith of hasan rank, recorded by Imam Malik, Ibn Majah, and al-Darqutni. The maxim is presented as a cornerstone of Sharia, supported by many Quranic and Sunnah texts, and serves as the foundation for prohibiting harmful acts, arranging financial compensation and punishment, and as a tool for jurists in deriving rulings. The author explains that the maxim mandates the prevention of harm absolutely, covering both private and public harm, and applies before occurrence through prevention and after occurrence through removal of effects. It also requires choosing the lesser of two harms when complete avoidance is impossible. He clarifies that prescribed punishments do not violate this maxim because they serve a greater good. The author then distinguishes between 'ضرر' and 'ضرار', emphasizing that retaliatory harm is prohibited as a deliberate goal; it is only permitted when necessary and when no better alternative exists, such as in cases of qisas (retaliation for murder or bodily harm) where it is the most effective deterrent. For property damage, the proper remedy is financial compensation, not reciprocal destruction. Finally, the author lists four practical applications of the maxim: (1) extending a lease of agricultural land until harvest to prevent loss to the lessee; (2) allowing a seller to rescind a sale of perishable goods if the buyer delays; (3) permitting a lessee to rescind a lease upon discovering a pre-existing defect in the leased item; (4) allowing a person who pays off another's debt under compulsion to seek reimbursement. These examples illustrate how the maxim operates in various chapters of fiqh.

Key Terms
الضرر (al-ḍarar): Infliction of a harmful consequence upon another (مفسدة بالغير).
الضرار (al-ḍirār): Meeting harm with harm, i.e., retaliatory harm without benefit.
القصاص (al-qiṣāṣ): Retaliation in kind for murder or bodily harm, prescribed in the Quran.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a valid application of the maxim 'الضرر يزال' as described in the passage?
A) Extending a lease of agricultural land until harvest to prevent loss to the lessee.
B) Permitting a seller to rescind a sale of quickly spoiling goods if the buyer delays payment.
C) Allowing a victim of theft to break into the thief's house to recover the stolen property.
D) Requiring compensation for damage to another's property instead of reciprocal destruction.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'الضرر يزال' is a foundational Hanafi principle commanding the prevention and removal of harm, with qisas as the only exception for bodily harm, while retaliation in property is replaced by compensation.

The Maxim 'Harm Must Be Eliminated' and Its Six Fiqh Applications
Explaining the universal maxim 'Harm must be eliminated' and six illustrative rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'al-darar yuzal', explain its scope, and apply it to the six furuat given in the text.

Core Idea
Harm must be eliminated — whether by compensation, rescission, or preventive detention.

This lesson explores one of the five universal maxims of Islamic law: 'Harm must be eliminated'. The text presents the maxim's theoretical basis and then illustrates it with six concrete rulings covering lease, sale, debt, public order, and property rights. Understanding these applications enables a mufti to correctly invoke the maxim in new cases.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its rank
Identify the maxim as 'al-darar yuzal', which is a kubra (major) maxim governing all fiqh chapters.
The maxim is derived from the hadith 'La darar wa la dirar'.
2
Understand the distinction between bodily harm and property harm
Note that retaliation (qisas) is for life and limb, while compensation (tadmin) is for property damage. This distinction is foundational for applying the maxim.
The text explains that retaliation is more effective for bodily harm, but property harm is best remedied through financial redress.
3
List the six furuat
Read each application in the text: (1) lease extension for crops, (2) seller rescission for perishables, (3) lessee rescission for hidden defect, (4) recovery of debt paid under compulsion, (5) preventive detention of criminals, (6) protection of old rights.
The text jumps from 2 to 4; the three missing number is likely a scribal omission, but the six cases are distinct.
4
For each case, identify how harm is eliminated
Describe the mechanism: (1) extension with rent, (2) rescission, (3) rescission, (4) recourse, (5) detention, (6) non-interference.
The maxim is applied positively to remove harm or negatively to avoid causing harm.
5
Apply the maxim to a new scenario
Consider a modern scenario: A farmer rents land for seasonal crops, but the landlord dies and heirs demand immediate eviction. Use the maxim to argue that the farmer may stay until harvest.
Reason by analogy to the first application.

الفصل (۸۱) شرح قواعد المجلة وتصنيفها
فأصبحت مقابلة الإتلاف بالإتلاف مجرد حماقة
وذلك بخلاف الجناية على النفس والبدن مما شرع فيه القصاص. فمن قتل يُقتل، ومن قَطَعَ يُقطع، لأنَّ هذه الجنايات لا يقمعها إلا عقوبة من جنسها، كي يعلم الجاني أنه في النهاية كمن يعتدي على نفسه. وكل عقوبة أخرى لا تعيد للمجني عليه ما فقد من نفس أو عضو، فتظل حزازات النفوس كامنة تندفع إلى الثأر، وتجر وراءها ذيول الويلات والفساد فيبقى طريق القصاص في الجنايات على النقوس والأعضاء أنفع، لأنه أعدل وأقمع.
أما إتلافُ المال، فإنَّ التضمين فيه هو التدبير السديد المفيد دون مقابلة الإتلاف بالإتلاف.
وعلى هذه القاعدة بنى الفقهاء أحكاماً لا تحصى من شتى الأبواب،
فقرروا أنه :
۱ - لو انتهت مدة إجارة الأرض الزراعية قبل أن يستحصد الزرع تبقى في يد المستأجر بأجر المثل حتى يستحصد منعاً لضرر المستأجر بقلع الزرع قبل أوانه .
۲ - ولو باع شيئاً مما يسرع إليه الفساد كالفواكه مثلاً، وغاب المشتري قبل نقد الثمن وقبض المبيع وخيف فساده، فللبائع أن يفسخ البيع ويبيع من غيره دفعاً لضرره (رد المحتار من متفرقات البيوع). - ولو اشترى شيئاً وآجره، ثم اطلع على عيب قديم فيه، يعتبر عذراً له يسوغ له فسخ الإجارة ليتمكن من رده على بائعه إزالة للضرر عن نفسه، والإجارة تفسخ بالأعذار كما تقدم ف ٧/٤٠).
هذا
٤ - وجميع صور الاضطرار إلى دفع الدين عن الغير بلا إذنه، حيث لا يعتبر الدافع متبرّعاً، بل يحق له الرجوع على المدين بما دفعه عن ذمته منعاً للضرر عن نفسه كما تقدّم هي من فروع هذه القاعدة.
الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية في الفقه
٥- ويجوز حبس المشهورين بالدعارة والفساد حتى تظهر توبتهم، ولو لم يثبت عليهم جرم معيّن بطريق قضائي، دفعاً لشرهم، لأنهم قد يحتاطون ويتحفظون فقد يملأون الدنيا فساداً وأضراراً، ولا يمكن إثبات شيء عليهم بطريقه القضائي. (ر : معين الحكام القسم الثالث في بالسياسة الشرعية ص/ ٢١٥ و٢١٨).
القضاء
٦ - وأوجب الفقهاء احترام الحقوق القديمة من منافع ومرافق وتصرفات، ولو لم يكن في أيدي أصحابها وثائق مثبتة، لأن في إزالتها بهم، مالم يثبت إحداثها بطريق غير مشروع، أو يكن فيها ضرر إضراراً بحقوق العامة، كما سيأتي.
إلى غير ذلك من الفصول والأحكام الفقهية الكثيرة.
Chapter (81): Explanation of the Majallah Rules and Their Classification. So confronting harm with harm becomes mere foolishness. This is contrary to offenses against life and limb for which retaliation is prescribed: whoever kills is killed, and whoever cuts is cut, because these crimes are not suppressed except by a punishment of their kind, so that the criminal knows that in the end he is like one who attacks himself. Every other punishment does not restore what the victim lost of life or limb, so the rancor of souls remains latent, driving to revenge, and dragging behind it tails of calamities and corruption. Thus the path of retaliation for offenses against lives and limbs remains more beneficial, because it is more just and more repressive. As for property damage, compensation (tadmin) is the sound and beneficial measure, without confronting harm with harm. Upon this rule, the jurists built countless rulings from various chapters. They decided: 1 - If the term of a lease of agricultural land ends before the crop is harvested, it remains in the lessee's hand with rent of the like until harvest, to prevent harm to the lessee by uprooting the crop before its time. 2 - If someone sells something that spoils quickly, like fruits, and the buyer is absent before payment of the price and taking possession of the sold item, and its spoilage is feared, the seller may rescind the sale and sell to another to repel his harm. (Radd al-Muhtar from miscellaneous sales sections). And if someone buys something and leases it, then discovers a prior defect, this is considered an excuse for him permitting him to rescind the lease to be able to return it to its seller, removing harm from himself, and lease is rescinded for excuses as previously in section 7/40. This. 4 - All cases of necessity to pay a debt on behalf of another without his permission, where the payer is not considered a volunteer, but has the right to recourse against the debtor for what he paid from his liability, to prevent harm from himself, as previously stated, are branches of this rule. Chapter Ten: The Universal Rules in Fiqh. 5 - It is permissible to detain those notorious for prostitution and corruption until their repentance becomes apparent, even if no specific crime has been proven against them through a judicial process, to repel their evil, because they may be cautious and cautious, and they may fill the world with corruption and harms, and it is not possible to prove anything against them through a judicial process. (Reference: Mu'in al-Hukkam, Part Three, on al-Siyasa al-Shar'iyya, pp. 215, 218). Judiciary. 6 - The jurists obligated respect for old rights of benefits, easements, and dispositions, even if their owners do not have established documents, because removing them would harm them, unless their creation is proven by an unlawful means, or there is harm to public rights, as will come. And other such numerous chapters and fiqh rulings.
Source pages p. 926

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the universal maxim 'Harm must be eliminated' (الضرر يزال). The author begins by contrasting retaliation for bodily harm with compensation for property damage, explaining that while retaliation is necessary to quell blood feuds, property damage is best remedied by financial compensation (tadmin). Upon this principle, the jurists built many rulings. The text then lists six applications: (1) A lessee of agricultural land whose lease expires before harvest may keep the land with a rent of the like until harvest, to avoid the harm of premature uprooting. (2a) A seller of perishables may rescind the sale if the buyer is absent and goods may spoil, to avoid loss. (2b) A lessee who discovers a prior defect in a leased item may rescind the lease to return it. (3 is missing from the text; probably a scribal omission). (4) Those who pay off another's debt under compulsion may reclaim the amount from the debtor, as they are not considered volunteers. (5) Notorious wrongdoers may be preventively detained even without specific evidence, under siyasah shar'iyyah. (6) Old established rights must be respected even without documents, unless they cause harm. These rulings all aim at preventing or removing harm, whether private or public.

Key Terms
التضمين (al-tadmin): Compensation for property damage, specifically financial redress
القصاص (al-qisas): Retaliation for homicide or battery, equal penalty for equal harm

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi jurists, what is the ruling if a lessee of agricultural land finds that the lease expires before the crop is harvested?
A) The lessee must immediately vacate the land.
B) The lessee may keep the land with a rent of the like until harvest.
C) The lessor must compensate the lessee for the crop.
D) The lease is automatically extended without additional rent.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-darar yuzal' requires eliminating harm through the least harmful means, respecting the distinction between bodily and property harm.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate

Harm is Repelled as Much as Possible: The Preventive Sub-Maxim
Defining and applying the maxim of harm prevention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the preventive nature of the sub-maxim 'Harm is repelled as much as possible', distinguish it from its counterpart 'Harm must be removed', and apply it to at least five concrete cases from different fiqh chapters.

Core Idea
Prevention is an obligation in Shari'a: harm must be repelled BEFORE it occurs, using all means possible.

This lesson explores a key sub-maxim derived from the greater principle 'Harm must be removed' (al-darar yuzal). While the parent maxim addresses removal of existing harm, this sub-maxim focuses on proactive measures to prevent harm from arising in the first place. The passage by Mustafa al-Zarqa provides a clear definition and a wealth of applications from both public and private law, demonstrating how Hanafi fiqh operationalizes prevention.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its place in the qawida hierarchy
Identify that this is a sughra (sub-maxim) of the kubra 'Harm must be removed', and it is a dabt (restrictive sub-maxim) that defines the proactive aspect of harm prevention.
The Majalla codified it as article 31.
2
Understand the obligation of prevention
Explain that Shari'a requires proactive measures to stop harm before it occurs, limited only by capacity (bi-qadr al-imkan), as prevention is better than cure.
This is based on the principle of maslaha mursala and siyasa shar'iyya.
3
Categorize the furuat
Group the applications into public interest (jihad, punishments, sadd al-dhara'i') and private rights (shuf'a, hajr, khiyarat, etc.). This helps see the scope.
The passage explicitly separates 'public interests' and 'private rights'.
4
Apply the maxim to each furu'
For each case, articulate how the ruling prevents harm. For example, shuf'a prevents harm from a bad neighbor; hajr over a bankrupt prevents harm to creditors.
Focus on the 'preventive' aspect—what harm would occur if the ruling were absent.
5
Distinguish from the reactive maxim
Contrast with 'Harm must be removed' (al-darar yuzal), which applies after harm has occurred. Understand that both maxims complement each other for comprehensive harm management.
For example, khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) removes harm after purchase, while khiyar al-shart (option of condition) prevents harm before contract is finalized.

What branches from them of the maxims
19/81 - A - ‘Harm is repelled as much as possible’ (Majalla article 31)
This maxim expresses the obligation to repel harm before its occurrence, by all sufficient and adequate means in accordance with the maxim of public interests (masalih mursala) and shar'i policy. Because prevention is better than cure, and that is as much as possible, because the shar'i obligation is according to capacity.
.
In the field of public interests:
Jihad was legislated to repel the evil of enemies, punishments were obligated to suppress crime and maintain internal security, and blocking the means of corruption and its doors of all types was obligated, besides other necessary measures to repel evil and prevent it.
And in the field of private rights:
- The right of pre-emption was legislated for purposes including repelling the harm of bad neighbors.
- Guardianship over the foolish was legislated to repel the harm of his bad actions from himself and his family.
- Guardianship over the bankrupt debtor was legislated to prevent harm to creditors from his actions.
- The judge has the right to prevent the debtor from traveling at the request of the creditor until he appoints an agent for litigation, and his dismissal of this agent is not valid as long as he is traveling, to prevent harm to the creditor (Ref: F 6/40).
- Judicial compulsion was legislated for the division of jointly owned property that is divisible at the request of one of the partners to repel the harm of joint ownership (Ref: F 8/24).
- They ruled that a partner must contribute to the expenses of developing or repairing jointly owned property if it deteriorates and is indivisible. If he does not, the other partner may develop it and retain it through judicial power or exploit it entirely until he recovers his costs.
- Some options in contracts were legislated to repel harm, such as the option of condition, the option of inspection, and the option of specification in sales due to some buyers' need for deliberation and consultation before finalizing (Ref: F 7/3 ninthly, 8/36, and 2/55).
Source pages p. 927

Commentary
This passage presents the first sub-maxim under the principle 'Harm must be removed': 'Harm is repelled as much as possible' (الضرر يدفع بقدر الإمكان). The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, opens by explaining that this maxim obligates preemptive action to prevent harm before it occurs, using all available means in accordance with public interest (maslaha mursala) and shar'i policy (siyasa shar'iyya), because prevention is better than cure. The obligation is limited to what is possible, as shari'a only requires according to capacity. The passage then provides numerous practical applications from different areas of fiqh. In the realm of public interest, jihad repels enemy harm, punishments deter crime, and blocking the means (sadd al-dhara'i') of corruption is required. In private rights, the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) allows a neighbor to buy adjoining property to prevent harm from a bad neighbor. Guardianship (hajr) over a foolish person or a bankrupt debtor protects them and creditors from harmful financial mismanagement. A judge can prevent a debtor from traveling until he appoints a representative for litigation, to protect the creditor's right. Co-owners can be compelled to partition divisible property to end harmful co-ownership. If property is indivisible and deteriorating, a partner can be forced to contribute to repairs, and if he refuses, the other partner can repair and recover costs through use or retention. Finally, several contractual options (khiyar al-shart, khiyar al-ru'ya, khiyar al-ta'yin) exist to allow a buyer time to deliberate, preventing the harm of a hasty decision. These examples all illustrate the proactive application of preventing harm before it materializes.

Key Terms
دفع الضرر (daf' al-darar): Preventing harm before it occurs by taking proactive measures

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an application of the maxim 'Harm is repelled as much as possible' in the Hanafi school?
A) Granting the buyer a right to return defective goods (khiyar al-'ayb)
B) Imprisoning notorious criminals without a specific proven crime to prevent future harm
C) Dividing joint property when it is indivisible and causing loss
D) Removing an overhanging branch that damages a neighbor's property

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi sub-maxim 'Harm is repelled as much as possible' obligates proactive measures to prevent harm before it occurs, with applications ranging from preventive detention to pre-emption.

Applying the Maxim 'Harm Must Be Removed' (الضرر يزال)
Defining and applying the maxim of harm removal after occurrence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-darar yuzal' to identify remedial rulings in public rights, private rights, and contracts.

Core Idea
Harm that has occurred must be removed and its effects repaired — by removal, compensation, or contractual options.

The maxim 'Harm must be removed' (الضرر يزال) is the second sub-maxim of the greater maxim 'Harm cannot be met by harm.' This lesson focuses on the remedial dimension: once harm has materialized, Islamic law imposes an obligation to eliminate it and repair its consequences. The source passage provides concrete examples from three domains: public rights (encroachments on roads), private rights (tort compensation, neighbor's overhanging branches), and contracts (options to rescind).

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Read the Arabic text and note that this maxim governs harm after occurrence, not prevention. Identify that it is article 20 of the Majalla.
Remedial, not preventive.
2
Categorise the furuat
Group the examples in the passage into three categories: public rights (spout, building on road), private rights (daman, branches), and contractual remedies (khiyar al-ayb, khiyar al-ghubn, khiyar tafriq al-safqa).
Each category shows a different form of removal.
3
Analyse the first category: public rights
For the spout on the public road, explain why it must be removed: it harms passersby who have a right to the road. The removal is physical and immediate.
No compensation alternative; harm is direct and must be eliminated.
4
Analyse the second category: private rights
For the person who causes damage (mutlif), the ruling is compensation (daman). The harm is repaired financially. For overhanging branches, the remedy is physical removal (raise or cut).
Compensation is a form of removal when physical removal is not possible.
5
Analyse the third category: contractual options
Options like khiyar al-ayb allow rescission to remove the harm of a defect. Each option is a legal remedy for harm discovered post-contract.
These options are legislated specifically to remove harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲۰/۸۱ - ب - الضرر يزال» (م/ ۲۰) .
هذه القاعدة تعبر عن وجوب رفع الضرر وترميم آثاره بعد الوقوع. ففي ميدان الحقوق العامة، إذا سَلَّطَ الإنسان ميزابه على الطريق العام بحيث يضر بالمارين فإنه يزال ؛ وكذلك إذا تعدى على الطريق ببناء أو غيره .
- وفي ميدان الحقوق الخاصة، يضمن المُثلِف عِوَض ما أتلف للضرر الذي أحدثه .
- وإذا طالت أغصان شجرة لشخص وتدلّت على دار جاره فأضرته يُكلف رفعها أو قطعها.
- وقد شُرِهَع كثير من الخيارات في العقود لإزالة الأضرار الواقعة على أحد المتعاقدين كخيار العيب وخيار الغبن بالتغرير وخيار تفريق الصفقة ر ف ۲/۳۷)
۹۹۳
20/81 - B - ‘Harm must be removed’ (Majalla article 20).
This maxim expresses the obligation to remove harm and repair its effects after occurrence. In the field of public rights, if a person places his spout on a public road such that it harms passersby, it must be removed; similarly, if he encroaches on the road with a building or otherwise.
- In the field of private rights, the destroyer is liable for compensation for what he destroyed due to the harm he caused.
- If the branches of a person's tree grow long and hang over his neighbor's house and cause harm, he is required to raise or cut them.
- And many options in contracts have been legislated to remove harms that occur to one of the contracting parties, such as the option of defect, the option of deception by fraud, and the option of splitting a transaction (Ref: F 2/37).
993
Source pages p. 928

Commentary
This passage presents the second sub-maxim: 'Harm must be removed' (الضرر يزال). The author explains that this maxim imposes the obligation to eliminate harm after it has already occurred and to repair its consequences. Unlike the previous maxim which focuses on prevention, this one deals with remedial action. The passage provides several practical examples. In public rights: if a person places a water spout (mizab) on a public road that harms passersby, it must be removed; similarly, any encroachment on the public road by building or other means must be cleared. In private rights: the person who causes damage (mutlif) is liable for compensation (daman) for the harm he inflicted. If a neighbor's tree branches overhang into another's property and cause harm, the owner is required to lift or cut them. Finally, various contractual options serve to remove harm after it occurs: the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb) allows a buyer to return a defective item; the option of deception (khiyar al-ghubn bil-taghrir) allows rescission if a party was defrauded; and the option of splitting a transaction (khiyar tafriq al-safqa) applies when a bundled sale is harmful. All these are remedial, removing harm that has already materialized.

Key Terms
إزالة الضرر (izalat al-darar): Removing harm after it has occurred and repairing its effects
الضرر يزال (al-darar yuzal): Harm must be removed

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under the maxim 'Harm must be removed' (الضرر يزال), which of the following is a remedy for harm that has already occurred?
A) Guardianship over a foolish person to prevent future financial loss
B) Requiring a debtor to appoint an agent before traveling
C) The option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb) allowing return of a defective item
D) Imprisoning a notorious criminal before he commits a crime

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-darar yuzal' obligates the removal of harm after it occurs, through physical removal, compensation, or contractual options.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate

The Grand Maxim: Harm Must Be Removed (الضرر يزال)
Presenting the legal maxim with practical applications from various fiqh chapters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the maxim 'al-darar yuzal', state its scope, and apply it to concrete cases from various fiqh chapters to determine the ruling.

Core Idea
Harm must be removed: a universal maxim obligating elimination of harm and repair of its effects, subject to balancing constraints.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's commentary on the Majalla introduces one of the five grand maxims of Islamic law. The author demonstrates its power by listing concrete rulings from different areas of fiqh, showing how the maxim generates both preventive (daf') and remedial (izalah) measures. Understanding this maxim is essential for a mufti to derive rulings in novel cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its formulation
Locate the maxim in Arabic 'الضرر يزال' and its English translation 'Harm must be removed'. Note that it is Article 20 of the Majalla.
This is a kulliya (universal) maxim at the kubra rank.
2
Distinguish preventive and remedial applications
Categorize each example as daf' al-darar (preventing potential harm) or izalat al-darar (removing existing harm).
Options in contracts are preventive; removing a spout is remedial.
3
Classify each example by fiqh chapter
Map each case to its fiqh chapter (e.g., hajr, qada', shirkah, buyu', etc.). This shows the maxim's cross-chapter scope.
The maxim applies across ibadat and muamalat.
4
Apply the maxim to a new scenario
Given a novel case (e.g., a neighbor's noise), determine if harm exists and whether removal is obligatory, considering the restrictions from related maxims.
Remember: harm cannot be removed by its like, and greater harm is removed by lesser harm.
5
Note restrictions from related maxims
The maxim is qualified by 'al-darar la yuzalu bi mithlih' and 'al-darar al-ashad yuzalu bi al-darar al-akhaf'.
These prevent abuse of the maxim.

Source Text

الفصل (۸۱) شرح قواعد المجلة وتصنيفها
- وشرع الحجر على المدين المفلس منعاً لضرر الدائنين من تصرفاته.
- وحُقَّ للقاضي منع المدين من السفر بناء على طلب الدائن حتى يوكل وكيلاً عنه بالخصومة، ولم يصح منه عزل هذا الوكيل مادام هو مسافراً منعاً لضرر الدائن (ر : ف ٦/٤٠).
وشرع الإجبار القضائي على قسمة المال المشترك القابل للقسمة بناءً على طلب أحد الشركاء دفعاً لضرر شركة الملك (ر: ف ٨/٢٤).
وقرروا إجبار الشريك على المساهمة في نفقات تعمير العقار المشترك أو ترميمه إذا توهن وكان غير قابل للقسمة. وإذا لم يفعل فلشريكه الآخر أن يعمره ويحبسه عند بقوة القضاء أو يستغله كله حتى يستوفي.
- وقد شرعت بعض الخيارات في العقود لدفع الضرر، كما في خيار الشرط وخيار الرؤية وخيار التعيين في البيوع لحاجة بعض المشترين إلى التروّي والاستشارة قبل البت ( ر : ف ۷/۳ تاسعاً و ٨/٣٦ و ٢/٥٥).
۲۰/۸۱ - ب - الضرر يزال» (م/ ۲۰) .
هذه القاعدة تعبر عن وجوب رفع الضرر وترميم آثاره بعد الوقوع. ففي ميدان الحقوق العامة، إذا سَلَّطَ الإنسان ميزابه على الطريق العام بحيث يضر بالمارين فإنه يزال ؛ وكذلك إذا تعدى على الطريق ببناء أو غيره .
- وفي ميدان الحقوق الخاصة، يضمن المُثلِف عِوَض ما أتلف للضرر الذي أحدثه .
- وإذا طالت أغصان شجرة لشخص وتدلّت على دار جاره فأضرته يُكلف رفعها أو قطعها.
- وقد شُرِع كثير من الخيارات في العقود لإزالة الأضرار الواقعة على أحد المتعاقدين كخيار العيب وخيار الغبن بالتغرير وخيار تفريق الصفقة (ر ف ۲/۳۷)
Section (81): Explanation of the Majalla's Rules and Their Classification. - The interdiction (hajr) on the bankrupt debtor is legislated to prevent harm to creditors from his actions. - The judge has the right to prevent the debtor from traveling upon the creditor's request until he appoints an agent for litigation, and his dismissal of this agent is invalid as long as he is traveling, to prevent harm to the creditor (ref: F 6/40). Compulsory judicial division of joint property eligible for division is legislated upon the request of one partner to repel the harm of co-ownership (ref: F 8/24). They decided to compel a partner to contribute to expenses for building or repairing joint property if it weakens and is indivisible. If he does not, his partner may build and detain it by judicial power or exploit it entirely until he recovers. - Some options (khiyarat) in contracts are legislated to repel harm, such as the option of stipulation, option of inspection, and option of specification in sales due to some buyers' need to deliberate and consult before deciding (ref: F 7/3, ninth, and 8/36 and 2/55). 20/81 - B - 'Harm must be removed' (M/20). This rule expresses the obligation to remove harm and repair its effects after occurrence. In the field of public rights, if a person directs his spout onto the public road such that it harms passersby, it is removed; likewise if he encroaches on the road with a building or otherwise. - In the field of private rights, the one who causes damage guarantees compensation for what he destroyed due to the harm he caused. - If the branches of a person's tree lengthen and hang over his neighbor's house causing harm, he is required to lift them or cut them. - Many options in contracts have been legislated to remove harms occurring to one of the contracting parties, such as the option of defect, option of deception by fraud, and option of splitting the transaction (ref: F 2/37).
Source pages p. 928

Commentary
This passage presents the famous maxim 'Harm must be removed' (الضرر يزال) from the Majalla (Article 20). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, first illustrates the maxim through several applications from different areas of fiqh before explicitly stating the maxim. These applications include: (1) interdiction on a bankrupt debtor to protect creditors, (2) preventing a debtor from traveling without appointing an agent, (3) compulsory division of jointly owned property, (4) compelling a co-owner to contribute to repairs, (5) various options in contracts like khiyar al-shart, khiyar al-ru'ya, and khiyar al-ta'yin to prevent harm from hasty decisions, (6) removing a spout or building encroaching on a public road, (7) liability of a tortfeasor for damages, (8) trimming branches that overhang a neighbor's property, and (9) options for defects, fraud, and splitting transactions to remedy harm after a contract. After listing these, the author explains that the maxim imposes a duty to eliminate harm and repair its consequences. The examples cover both preventive measures (دفع الضرر) and remedial measures (إزالة الضرر). In Hanafi usul, this maxim is a core principle, and it is qualified by the two subsequent maxims: harm cannot be removed by its like, and greater harm is removed by lesser harm.

Key Terms
الضرر (al-darar): Any harm, injury, or damage that affects a person's rights, property, or well-being, whether material or moral.
دفع الضرر (daf' al-darar): Preventive removal of potential harm before it occurs, e.g., options in contracts to avoid hasty decisions.
إزالة الضرر (izalat al-darar): Removing harm after it has occurred, e.g., compensation or returning defective goods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the examples in the text, what is the ruling for a person whose tree branches overhang a neighbor's house causing harm?
A) The neighbor must tolerate the harm.
B) The owner is required to cut or lift the branches to remove the harm.
C) The neighbor can cut the branches himself without permission.
D) The owner must compensate but may keep the branches.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-darar yuzal' is a foundational principle in Hanafi fiqh, obligating the removal of harm through any means that do not cause equal or greater harm, and it applies across all chapters of Islamic law.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

Harm Cannot Be Removed by Its Like (الضرر لا يزال بمثله)
Qualifying the first harm maxim with prohibition of equal harm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'الضرر لا يزال بمثله' to identify when removing harm by equal or greater harm is prohibited, and distinguish between permissible compensation and prohibited transposition of harm.

Core Idea
Harm cannot be removed by causing equal or greater harm to another.

The previous maxim taught us that harm must be removed. But removal must be genuine—not a mere transfer of harm to another party. This lesson introduces the crucial qualifier: you cannot remove harm by causing an equivalent or greater harm. This prevents the 'solution' from becoming a new injustice.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its restriction
Identify the maxim 'الضرر لا يزال بمثله' (Harm cannot be removed by its like). Understand that it restricts the first maxim: removal must not produce equal harm.
The maxim is article 25 of the Majalla.
2
Apply al-awlawiyyah (priority)
If equal harm is prohibited, then greater harm is even more prohibited by logical priority (al-awlawiyyah). This extends the scope.
3
Analyze the four furuat examples
For each example: (1) starving person and equally needy owner, (2) poor relative's maintenance, (3) forced division of indivisible property, (4) defective item with new defect. Identify why the proposed action would cause equal or greater harm.
Notice that in each case, the law provides an alternative remedy (compensation, sale, etc.) that avoids transposing harm.
4
Recognize the istithna (exception)
The maxim is overridden when the alternative is greater harm—then lesser harm is allowed by the complementary maxim 'الضرر الأشد يُزال بالضرر الأخف'.
This exception ensures that the lesser of two harms is chosen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية في الفقه
۲۱/۱ - ج - الضرر لا يزال بمثله» (م/ ٢٥). 
هذه القاعدة تضع قيداً يقيد سابقتها فإن إزالة الضرر لا يجوز أن تكون بإحداث ضرر مثله، لأن هذا ليس إزالة. ويفهم من ذلك أنه لا تجوز إزالته بضرر أعظم منه بحكم الأولوية.
وعلى هذا لو لم يجد الإنسان المحتاج إلى دفع الهلاك عن نفسه جوعاً إلا مال محتاج مثله لا يجوز له أخذه.
- ولا تفرض النفقة للفقير على قريبه إذا كان فقيراً مثله .
- وكذا لا الشريك على قسمة المال المشترك غير القابل يجبر للقسمة، لأن في قسمته ضرراً أعظم من ضرر الشركة (وفي القانون اليوم يزال هذا الشيوع بالبيع وتوزيع الثمن.
- وإذا ظَهَرَ في المبيع عيب قديم وحَدَث فيه عند المشتري عيب جديد امتنع رد المبيع بالعيب القديم، لأن البائع عندئذ يتضرر بالعيب الحادث، بل يزال ضرر المشتري بإلزام البائع بالتعويض عن العيب القديم ما لم يرض برد المبيع مع عيبه الحادث.
Chapter Ten: Universal Rules in Fiqh. 21/1 - C - 'Harm cannot be removed by its like' (M/25). This rule places a restriction on its predecessor (the previous maxim). For the removal of harm is not permissible by causing a similar harm, because this is not removal. And it is understood from this that it is not permissible to remove it by a greater harm as a matter of priority. Accordingly, if a person in need of warding off destruction from himself due to hunger finds nothing but the property of another who is similarly needy, it is not permissible for him to take it. - And maintenance (nafaqah) is not imposed for a poor person on his relative if he is similarly poor. - Likewise, a partner is not compelled to divide joint property that is not divisible, because in its division there is harm greater than the harm of co-ownership (and in law today, this common ownership is removed by sale and distribution of the price). - And if in the sold item an old defect appears and a new defect occurs in the possession of the buyer, returning the item due to the old defect is prevented, because the seller would then be harmed by the new defect. Rather, the buyer's harm is removed by obligating the seller to compensate for the old defect unless the seller agrees to accept the return with the new defect.
Source pages p. 929

Commentary
This passage introduces the second major maxim: 'Harm cannot be removed by its like.' It serves as a crucial qualification to the first maxim. The author explains that the removal of harm cannot be achieved by causing an equivalent harm, as that would not be true removal; by priority, causing greater harm is also forbidden. The examples illustrate this: (1) A starving person cannot take food from another equally starving person — the harm to the owner would be equal to the harm prevented. (2) Maintenance (nafaqah) is not due from a poor relative to a similarly poor relative, as it would merely shift the harm. (3) In joint property that is not divisible, a partner cannot be compelled to divide it because the damage from forced division (e.g., loss of value) would outweigh the harm of continued co-ownership. The author notes that modern law resolves this by selling the property and distributing the proceeds. (4) In sales, if the item has an old defect and the buyer causes a new defect, the buyer cannot return the item for the old defect because that would harm the seller (who would receive a defective item). Instead, the buyer's harm is removed by compensation from the seller for the old defect, unless the seller agrees to take it back with the new defect. This maxim ensures that when multiple harms exist, the solution must not simply transpose the harm onto another party equally.

Key Terms
بمثله (bi-mithlihi): By something that is equal or similar in degree of harm.
الأولوية (al-awlawiyyah): The principle of priority: if something is forbidden in a lesser case, it is even more so in a greater case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'الضرر لا يزال بمثله', if a person's property is damaged due to an old defect and a new defect occurs, what is the ruling?
A) The buyer can return the item and the seller must accept it.
B) The buyer cannot return the item and receives no remedy.
C) The buyer cannot return the item but can claim compensation for the old defect.
D) The seller can force the buyer to keep the item.

Key Takeaway
Removing harm by causing equal or greater harm is not true removal—it is injustice; the law requires a remedy that does not simply transpose the harm.

Greater Harm Is Removed by Lesser Harm (الضرر الأشد يُزال بالضرر الأخف)
Presentation of the third maxim with illustrative fiqh cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim, explain its derivation via mafhum al-mukhalafah, and apply it to novel cases by comparing harms to determine the lighter one.

Core Idea
When two harms conflict, choose the lighter — the greater harm is removed by tolerating the lesser.

This lesson introduces the third major maxim of the Hanafi legal maxims: 'Greater harm is removed by lesser harm.' It is derived directly from the second maxim ('Harm is not removed by its like') through its converse (mafhum al-mukhalafah). While the second maxim prohibits causing equal harm, this one permits inflicting a lesser harm to prevent a greater one. The text provides three vivid examples from maintenance law, property disputes, and torts that illustrate how a mufti balances conflicting harms. Understanding this maxim is essential for resolving conflicts where any solution involves some degree of harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its rank
State the maxim in Arabic and English. Note that it is a wusta (intermediate) maxim under the kubra 'al-darar yuzal' (harm is removed). It is a kulliya (universal) maxim applicable across all fiqh chapters.
The maxim is codified as Article 27 of the Majalla.
2
Understand the derivation via mafhum al-mukhalafah
The second maxim 'al-darar la yuzal bi mithlihi' (harm is not removed by its like) implies that if harms are not equal, the greater may be removed by the lesser. This is the converse meaning. Trace the logical inference from prohibition of equal harm to permission of lesser harm.
Hanafi usul accepts mafhum al-mukhalafah under conditions of irtikaz (settled understanding).
3
Apply to the maintenance example
Analyze the first case: wealthy relatives must support poor relatives. Compare the two harms – the wealthy losing some money (lesser) vs. the poor suffering destitution (greater). Conclude that imposing maintenance is the correct application.
The ruling is found in the chapter of nafaqah (maintenance).
4
Apply to the building on disputed land example
When a buyer builds on land later claimed by another, compare the value of the building vs. the land. If the building is worth more, the buyer can compel the owner to sell the land (lesser harm to owner) rather than demolish the building (greater harm to buyer). Reverse if the land is more valuable.
This balances the interests of both parties, avoiding total loss.
5
Apply to the chicken swallowing pearl example
A chicken swallows a valuable pearl. The pearl owner can buy the chicken at market value to slaughter it and retrieve the pearl. Compare: the harm to the chicken owner (losing the chicken) is lesser than the harm to the pearl owner (losing the pearl). This is a classic Hanafi case in ghasp (torts).
The ruling prevents the greater injustice of losing the pearl entirely.
6
Note the limitation: harms must not be equal
The maxim only applies when the lesser harm is genuinely lighter than the greater. If the two harms are equal, the second maxim ('al-darar la yuzal bi mithlihi') governs, and neither harm can be removed. Always compare objectively using shari'ah criteria.
This limitation is an istithna' (exception) that restricts the maxim's scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲۲/۸۱
- الضرر الأشد يُزال بالضرر الأخف (م/۲۷).
هذه القاعدة تصريح بمفهوم المخالفة المستفاد من سابقتها : فتفرض النفقة للفقراء على الأغنياء من الأقارب، لأن ضرر الأغنياء بفرضها أخف من ضرر الفقراء بعدمه.
- ولو بنى مشتري الأرض فيها أو غرس ثم ظهر لها مستحق، فإذا كانت قيمة البناء أكثر حقّ للمشتري أن يتملك الأرض بقيمتها جبراً على صاحبها المستحق، والعكس بالعكس.
- وكذا لو ابتلعت دجاجة شخص لؤلؤة ثمينة لغيره فلصاحب اللؤلؤة أن يتملك الدجاجة بقيمتها كي يذبحها ويستخرج لؤلؤته.
22/81 - Greater harm is removed by lesser harm (M/27). This rule is an explicit statement of the converse meaning derived from its predecessor (the previous maxim). Maintenance is imposed for the poor upon the wealthy relatives, because the harm to the wealthy by imposing it is lighter than the harm to the poor by its absence. - And if a buyer builds or plants on land and then a rightful claimant appears, then if the value of the building is higher, the buyer has the right to take ownership of the land by paying its value, compelling the rightful owner, and vice versa. - Likewise, if a person's chicken swallows another's valuable pearl, the owner of the pearl has the right to take ownership of the chicken by paying its value, so that he may slaughter it and extract his pearl.
Source pages p. 929

Commentary
This passage presents the third maxim: 'Greater harm is removed by lesser harm.' It is a direct application of the previous maxim's converse (مفهوم المخالفة). Where the second maxim forbids equating harms, this one permits inflicting a lesser harm to avoid a greater one. The examples: (1) Wealthy relatives must provide maintenance to poor relatives, because the burden on the wealthy is lighter than the destitution of the poor. (2) If someone builds on land that later turns out to belong to another, and the building's value exceeds the land's, the builder can compel the owner to sell the land at its value rather than demolish the building (and vice versa if the land is more valuable). This avoids the greater harm of wasted investment. (3) If a chicken swallows a valuable pearl, the pearl's owner can buy the chicken at its market value to slaughter it and retrieve the pearl, rather than suffer the loss of the pearl. This is a well-known Hanafi example of balancing harms. The maxim is widely used in property, contracts, and torts to choose the least harmful solution when conflicts arise.

Key Terms
الأشد (al-ashadd): The greater or more severe harm.
الأخف (al-akhaff): The lesser or lighter harm that is tolerable in order to prevent the greater harm.
مفهوم المخالفة (mafhum al-mukhalafah): The converse meaning derived from a statement; here, from the prohibition of equal harm, the permissibility of lesser harm is inferred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'الضرر الأشد يُزال بالضرر الأخف', if a chicken swallows someone's valuable pearl, what right does the pearl's owner have?
A) He can take the chicken without compensation.
B) He can require the chicken's owner to slaughter it and return the pearl.
C) He can take ownership of the chicken by paying its value.
D) He can only claim compensation from the chicken's owner.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'Greater harm is removed by lesser harm' is a fundamental principle for resolving conflicts, requiring careful comparison of harms and derived from the prohibition of equal harm.

The Maxim: Severe Harm is Removed by Lesser Harm
Explains the maxim of choosing lesser harm with three fiqh applications and a Quranic proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'Severe harm is removed by lesser harm' to identify permissible actions in cases of conflicting harms and cite the Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
When two harms conflict, the lesser harm is permitted to avoid the greater harm.

This lesson introduces a cornerstone Hanafi maxim: the severe harm is removed by the lesser harm. It is the logical counterpart of the previous maxim (harm is not removed by its like). We'll explore three classical applications and the Quranic evidence from the story of al-Khidr and Musa.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its relationship
Write the Arabic maxim and note that it complements 'Harm is not removed by its like'. The latter prohibits equal harm; this one permits lesser harm to avoid greater harm.
Both are among the five major maxims (al-qawa'id al-kubra).
2
Analyze the three furuat
For each of the three cases (maintenance, building on land, chicken swallowing pearl), identify the two competing harms and explain why the lesser harm is chosen.
In each case, the lesser harm is financial or property loss, while the greater harm involves livelihood or complete loss of property.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Read the story of Khidr scuttling the ship. Identify the two harms: minor damage to the ship (lesser) vs complete loss of the ship to a tyrant (greater). Note that Khidr acted by divine instruction.
The Quranic story establishes the principle at the level of revelation.
4
Apply the condition
Note the condition: the lesser harm must be genuinely lesser and not itself prohibited by a stronger text. The means to remove the greater harm must be permissible in themselves.
For example, one cannot kill a person to save property; life is inviolable.

Source Text

٢٢/٨١ - الضرر الأشد يُزال بالضرر الأخف (م/۲۷).
هذه القاعدة تصريح بمفهوم المخالفة المستفاد من سابقتها : فتفرض النفقة للفقراء على الأغنياء من الأقارب، لأن ضرر الأغنياء بفرضها أخف من ضرر الفقراء بعدمه.
- ولو بنى مشتري الأرض فيها أو غرس ثم ظهر لها مستحق، فإذا كانت قيمة البناء أكثر حقّ للمشتري أن يتملك الأرض بقيمتها جبراً على صاحبها المستحق، والعكس بالعكس.
- وكذا لو ابتلعت دجاجة شخص لؤلؤة ثمينة لغيره فلصاحب اللؤلؤة

موسی
الفصل (۸۱) شرح قواعد المجلة وتصنيفها
:
(۸۲
حين
يمكن أن يستدل لهذه القاعدة ما جاء في القرآن الكريم في قصة عليه السلام مع الخضر (سورة الكهف، الآيات ٦٥ - رافقه موسى على أن لا يسأله عن شيء يفعله حتى يحدث له الخضر منه ذكراً. فلما ركبا في السفينة خرقها الخضر فقال له موسى: أخرقتها لتغرق أهلها، لقد جئت شيئاً إمراً !! فقال له :الخضر ألم أقل إنك لن تستطيع صبراً؟! ثم لما فارقه قال الخضر: سأنبئك بما لم تستطع عليه صبراً ... أما السفينة فكانت لمساكين يعملون في البحر، فأردت أن أعيبها وكان وراءهم ملك يأكل كل سفينة غصباً ... وما فعلته عن أمري». فقد أوحى الله إليه أن يخرق السفينة لينقذ أصحابها من ضرر أعظم وهو أن يغتصبها الملك الظالم.
يعي
22/81 - Severe harm is removed by lesser harm (M/27). This rule is an explicit statement of the converse implication from its predecessor: Maintenance is imposed on wealthy relatives for the poor, because the harm to the wealthy by imposing it is lighter than the harm to the poor by its absence. And if a buyer of land builds or plants on it and then a rightful claimant appears, if the value of the building is greater, the buyer has the right to own the land by paying its value, compelling the rightful owner, and vice versa. Likewise, if someone's chicken swallows another's valuable pearl, the owner of the pearl may own the chicken by paying its value in order to slaughter it and extract his pearl. One can deduce for this rule what came in the Holy Quran in the story of Moses, peace be upon him, with al-Khidr (Surah al-Kahf, verses 65-82): Moses accompanied al-Khidr on condition not to ask about anything he did until al-Khidr himself mentioned it. When they boarded the ship, al-Khidr scuttled it. Moses said, 'Have you scuttled it to drown its people? You have certainly done a grievous thing!' Al-Khidr said, 'Did I not say that with me you would never be patient?' Then when they parted, al-Khidr said, 'I will inform you of the interpretation of that over which you could not have patience... As for the ship, it belonged to poor people working at sea, so I intended to damage it, and there was after them a king who seized every ship by force... And I did it not of my own accord.' So Allah revealed to him to scuttle the ship to save its owners from a greater harm, namely the tyrant king's seizure.
Source pages p. 929

Commentary
This passage expounds a second major maxim that is the logical counterpart of the first. Whereas the previous maxim prohibits equal or greater harm in removing harm, this one permits causing a lesser harm to avoid a greater harm. The author gives three applications: (1) Wealthy relatives must pay maintenance to poor relatives; the harm of payment is lesser than the harm of the poor's deprivation. (2) If a buyer builds on purchased land later claimed by another, and the building's value exceeds the land's value, the buyer may compel the rightful owner to sell the land at its value. This causes the owner a lesser harm (losing the land for its price) compared to the buyer losing his building. (3) If a chicken swallows someone's pearl, the pearl owner may buy the chicken (lesser harm) to retrieve the pearl. The author then cites the Quranic story of Khidr and Moses as evidence: Khidr scuttled the ship to prevent its seizure by a tyrant king, incurring a minor damage to avoid a greater loss. This demonstrates the principle's foundation in revelation.

Key Terms
الضرر الأشد (al-darar al-ashadd): The more severe or greater harm, which is to be avoided even if it requires causing a lesser harm.
الضرر الأخف (al-darar al-akhaff): The lighter or lesser harm, which may be inflicted to prevent the greater harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT an application of the maxim 'Severe harm is removed by lesser harm' provided in the passage?
A) Imposing maintenance on wealthy relatives for poor relatives.
B) The buyer of land being allowed to own it by paying its value after building on it if the building is more valuable.
C) A chicken swallowing a pearl, allowing the pearl owner to buy the chicken.
D) A debtor being forced to sell his house to pay a debt.

Key Takeaway
The maxim permits causing a lesser harm to prevent a greater harm, and is supported by the Quranic story of al-Khidr and Musa.

Private Harm Borne for Public Good: The Maxim and Its Applications
States the maxim allowing public interest over private harm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal maxim 'Private harm is borne to repel public harm' and apply it to identify when individual harm may be tolerated for public good.

Core Idea
Private harm must be endured to prevent widespread public harm.

This lesson introduces a key Hanafi legal maxim that balances individual rights and public welfare. The maxim states that when a private individual's action or practice causes general harm to the community, the state may restrain that individual, even if it causes them personal loss. The author provides three concrete applications: an incompetent doctor, a corrupt mufti, and a dishonest transport carrier. Understanding this maxim is essential for muftis and jurists dealing with public policy issues.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Read the Arabic maxim and understand its plain meaning: an individual's harm is tolerated to prevent harm to the public at large.
The maxim is a kulliya (universal principle) of the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the scope
The maxim applies to all areas where private conduct threatens public welfare, especially in professions affecting lives, religion, and property.
The text mentions lives (the doctor), religion (the mufti), and property (the carrier).
3
Work through the furuat
Analyze each of the three applications: the ignorant doctor, the licentious mufti, and the dishonest carrier. For each, explain why the state may impose restraint (hajr) even though it harms the individual.
The harm to the public outweighs the private harm of restraint.
4
Identify istithna' (exceptions)
Consider whether there are cases where private harm is NOT tolerated. The text does not mention exceptions, but in Hanafi fiqh, the maxim is limited to significant public harm, not trivial inconvenience.
Other related maxims provide boundaries.
5
Relate to other qawaid
Note the relationship with the parallel maxim 'Severe harm is removed by lighter harm' (al-darar al-ashad yuzalu bi al-darar al-akhaff).
Both maxims involve a harm balancing.

Commentary
This passage presents the maxim that private harm must be endured to prevent public harm. The author gives the example of placing a legal restraint (hajr) on three categories of people: an incompetent doctor, a corrupt mufti (one who gives unreliable fatwas), and a mukari (a carrier who may be dishonest or dangerous). Even though these individuals suffer harm from being restricted, the greater good of protecting the community's lives, religion, and wealth justifies it. The footnote explains that 'mukari' refers to a person who contracts for transport of people or goods; analogous to modern travel agencies. This maxim is fundamental in public policy and state intervention in Islamic law.

Key Terms
الضرر الخاص (al-darar al-khas): Harm that affects a specific individual or small group, which is tolerable to prevent widespread harm.
الضرر العام (al-darar al-amm): Harm that affects the general public or a large number of people; preventing it takes priority over individual harm.
الحجر (al-hajr): Legal restraint placed on a person due to incompetence, dishonesty, or public harm, restricting their right to act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) A poor person seeking charity.
D) A person who speaks loudly.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'Private harm is borne to repel public harm' is a fundamental Hanafi legal principle that allows the state to restrict individual rights when necessary to prevent serious public harm.

The Four Maxims on Preferring Lesser Harm and Preventing Evil
Explaining four related legal maxims with applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four Hanafi legal maxims on preferring lesser harm and repelling evil, and apply them to contemporary fatwa cases.

Core Idea
When harms conflict, choose the lesser; private harm is borne to prevent public evil.

Islamic law does not deal in absolutes when harms collide. These four maxims—woven into the Ottoman Majalla—provide a framework for a mufti to prioritize. They are derived from Quranic narratives, Prophetic guidance, and the cumulative wisdom of Hanafi jurists. Understanding them is essential for issuing balanced fatwas that protect both individual rights and public welfare.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four maxims from the passage
Read the Arabic text and locate the four maxims labeled with letters H, W, Z, and H (the last one). Write them in Arabic and English.
Maxims are: 23/81 (يختار أهون الشرين), 24/81 (إذا تعارض مفسدتان...), 25/81 (يُتحمل الضرر الخاص...), 26/81 (درء المفاسد أولى...).
2
State the core principle of each maxim
For each maxim, paraphrase its key idea in one sentence. How does it guide a mufti's decision when harms and benefits conflict?
Example: Maxim 26 says stopping evil is more important than gaining benefit.
3
Connect the Quranic evidence to the first principle
The story of Moses and al-Khidr (Surah al-Kahf) is cited. Explain how damaging the ship illustrates choosing the lesser harm.
The temporary damage (lesser harm) prevented the king's seizure (greater harm).
4
List three applications per maxim from the text
Scan the furuat (applications) in the passage and assign each to the maxim it best illustrates. Write at least three per maxim.
E.g., cutting open deceased mother → maxim 24 (lesser harm to save life).
5
Apply the maxims to a new scenario
Given a modern case (e.g., a company dumping waste to reduce costs), decide which maxim applies and what ruling a Hanafi mufti would give.
Consider proportionality: the private profit vs. public environmental harm.

حين يمكن أن يستدل لهذه القاعدة ما جاء في القرآن الكريم في قصة موسى عليه السلام مع الخضر (سورة الكهف، الآيات ٦٥ - ...). رافقه موسى على أن لا يسأله عن شيء يفعله حتى يحدث له الخضر منه ذكراً. فلما ركبا في السفينة خرقها الخضر فقال له موسى: أخرقتها لتغرق أهلها، لقد جئت شيئاً إمراً !! فقال له الخضر ألم أقل إنك لن تستطيع صبراً؟! ثم لما فارقه قال الخضر: سأنبئك بما لم تستطع عليه صبراً ... أما السفينة فكانت لمساكين يعملون في البحر، فأردت أن أعيبها وكان وراءهم ملك يأخذ كل سفينة غصباً ... وما فعلته عن أمري». فقد أوحى الله إليه أن يخرق السفينة لينقذ أصحابها من ضرر أعظم وهو أن يغتصبها الملك الظالم.

وكذلك يجوز، بل يجب هدم الدور الملاصقة للحريق، منعاً لتجاوزه إذا خيف سريانه.
٢٦/٨١ - ح - دَرء المفاسد أولى من جلب المنافع (م ۳۰). لأنَّ للمفاسد سرياناً وتوسعاً كالوباء والحريق. فمن الحكمة والحزم القضاء عليها في مهدها، ولو ترتب على ذلك حرمان من منافع أو تأخير لها. ومن ثم كان حرص الشارع على منع المنهيات أقوى من حرصه على تحقيق المأمورات. وقد روي عن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام أنه قال: «ما نَهَيْتُكم فاجتنبوه وما أمرتكم به فأتوا منه ما استطعتم». - وعلى هذا يجب شرعاً منع التجارة بالمحرمات من خمر ومخدرات ولو أن فيها أرباحاً ومنافع اقتصادية.
- ويمنع مالك الدار من فتح نافذة تطل على مقر نساء جاره ولو كان له فيها منفعة.
- وكذا يمنع كل جار من أن يتصرف في ملكه تصرفاً يضر بجيرانه، كاتخاذ معصرة أو فرن يؤذيان الجيران بالرائحة أو الدخان.
- ويحجر على الطبيب الجاهل ونحوه.
- ويمنع الاحتكار والتعدي في الأسعار كما تقدم في القاعدة السابقة.
Chapter (81): Explanation of the Rules of the Majalla and Their Classification
:
This rule may be supported by what came in the Noble Qur'an in the story of Moses, peace be upon him, with al-Khidr (Surah al-Kahf, verses 65-...). Moses accompanied him on condition that he would not ask him about anything he did until al-Khidr himself mentioned it to him. When they boarded the ship, al-Khidr made a hole in it. Moses said to him: 'Have you made a hole in it to drown its people? You have certainly done a grievous thing!' Al-Khidr said: 'Did I not say that you would never be able to have patience with me?' Then when he parted from him, al-Khidr said: 'I will inform you of the interpretation of that over which you could not have patience... As for the ship, it belonged to poor people working at sea, so I intended to damage it, for there was after them a king who seizes every ship by force... And I did it not of my own accord.' Allah had inspired him to damage the ship to save its owners from a greater harm, which was that the unjust king would seize it.
23/81 - H - The lesser of two evils is chosen (Article 29).
24/81 - W - If two harms conflict, the greater harm is avoided by committing the lesser (Article 28).
These two rules are similar in meaning to the preceding rule. The following branch from them: - Permissibility of cutting open the belly of a deceased woman to extract a fetus if its life is hoped for. And permissibility of remaining silent about condemning evils if condemning them would result in a greater harm.
25/81 - Z - A private harm is tolerated to repel a public harm (Article 26). - Thus an ignorant physician, a lascivious mufti, and a transporter (1) are placed under interdiction (ḥajr), even if they are harmed by that, to repel their harm from the community in terms of lives, religion, and wealth.
(1) The transporter (al-mukārī) is one who contracts with travelers to transport them or their luggage on his animals. He was, in the past, like travel agencies and transportation today.
- The judge [places under interdiction] the goods of hoarders, even if that harms them - and sells them to repel the harm of hoarding from the public.
- And price-fixing, i.e., setting prices for sellers when they exceed and become exorbitant, is permissible.
Likewise, it is permissible, rather obligatory, to demolish houses adjacent to a fire, to prevent its spread if its spread is feared.
26/81 - H - Repelling evils is preferable to bringing benefits (Article 30). Because evils spread and expand like epidemics and fire. It is wisdom and decisiveness to eliminate them in their cradle, even if that results in deprivation of benefits or their delay. Hence, the Lawgiver's concern to prevent prohibitions is stronger than His concern to achieve commands. It is narrated from the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, that he said: 'What I have forbidden you, avoid it; and what I have commanded you, do of it what you can.' - Accordingly, it is obligatory, legally, to prohibit trade in forbidden things like wine and drugs, even if they contain profits and economic benefits.
- And the owner of a house is prevented from opening a window overlooking his neighbor's women's quarters, even if he has benefit in it.
- Likewise, every neighbor is prevented from acting in his property in a way that harms his neighbors, such as setting up an oil press or an oven that harms neighbors with smell or smoke.
- And an ignorant physician and the like are placed under interdiction.
- And hoarding and price manipulation are prohibited, as stated in the previous rule.
Source pages p. 930

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on legal maxims (qawā‘id) explains four interconnected maxims that govern how Islamic law prioritizes between harms and benefits. The author, Muṣṭafā Aḥmad al-Zarqā, analyzes maxims codified in the Ottoman Majalla (the civil code of the Hanafi school). The passage begins by citing the Quranic story of Moses and al-Khidr as a proof for the principle that a lesser harm may be permitted to avoid a greater one. Al-Khidr's damaging the ship was a lesser harm compared to the king's unjust seizure. This is the basis for the first maxim: 'The lesser of two evils is chosen' (Article 29). The second maxim states: 'If two harms conflict, the greater harm is avoided by committing the lesser' (Article 28). These are essentially the same principle applied to conflicting harms. Their applications include: cutting open a deceased mother's abdomen to save a viable fetus (a lesser harm against the mother's dignity to save a life), and remaining silent about an evil when speaking out would cause greater harm (e.g., risk of greater oppression). The third maxim: 'A private harm is tolerated to repel a public harm' (Article 26). This justifies state intervention like banning incompetent doctors, untrustworthy muftis, and transporters who cause harm, hoarding goods (state can force sale), price controls, and demolition of buildings to stop fire spread. The fourth and most comprehensive maxim: 'Repelling evils is preferable to bringing benefits' (Article 30). Because evils spread like epidemics, it is wiser to eliminate them even at the cost of losing benefits. The Prophet's hadith 'Avoid what I forbid; do what I command as much as you can' supports prioritizing prohibition over command. Applications include: prohibiting trade in drugs/alcohol despite profits, preventing windows overlooking neighbors' privacy, stopping harmful use of property (smoke, smell), and continuing the earlier examples of interdiction and price controls. These maxims are foundational in Hanafi legal theory for balancing harms and benefits in fatwa and judicial decisions.

Key Terms
مفسدتان (mafsadatān): Two conflicting harmful situations or evils
حَجْر (ḥajr): Legal interdiction restricting a person's property rights due to incompetence or harm to the public
مُكاري (mukārī): A transporter who contracts to carry travelers or goods – akin to modern transport companies
تسعير (tas'īr): Price fixing or price control by the state

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'Repelling evils is preferable to bringing benefits' (درء المفاسد أولى من جلب المنافع), which of the following is a correct application?
A) Opening a window overlooking a neighbor's private quarters to increase natural light in one's own house
B) Demolishing a house adjacent to a fire to prevent the fire from spreading to other houses
C) Allowing a competent physician to practice medicine without any restrictions
D) Encouraging trade in wine because it generates high tax revenue for the state

Key Takeaway
The four maxims form a hierarchy: choose the lesser harm, tolerate private loss for public good, and always prioritize preventing evil over acquiring benefit.

Two Qawāʿid: Preventing Harm and Prioritizing the Preventer
Explaining two maxims with their legal applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'Repelling harms takes precedence over securing benefits' and its corollary 'When the preventer and the requirer conflict, the preventer is given precedence' to novel cases in fiqh and fatwa.

Core Idea
Repelling harm always outweighs gaining benefit; when a right blocks another, the blocker wins.

This passage from the Majallah al-Adliyyah presents two foundational Hanafi legal maxims that govern conflict resolution in fiqh. The first maxim establishes the priority of preventing harm over achieving benefit. The second is a direct corollary: when a preventer (an obstacle) clashes with a requirer (a reason), the preventer prevails. Together, these maxims shape rulings in transactions, property, testimony, and public welfare.

Guided Path
1
State the first maxim and its justification
Identify the Arabic text: 'درء المفاسد أولى من جلب المنافع'. Note its justification: harms spread like plague and fire, so they must be eliminated at their source.
The hadith 'avoid what I forbid, do what I command as you can' supports this priority.
2
List the furuat from the text
Go through each example: hoarding, price controls, fire demolition, intoxicants trade, neighbor window, harmful workshop, incompetent physician. For each, identify the harm being repelled.
3
State the second maxim and its relationship
Identify: 'إذا تعارض المانع والمقتضي يُقدم المانع'. Explain that this is a direct application of the first maxim, not a separate rule.
The second maxim is a dabt (restrictive sub-maxim) of the first.
4
Work through the second maxim's applications
For each example (partner disposition, death illness acknowledgment, depositee's acknowledgment, witness validation), identify the muqtaḍī (requirer) and the māniʿ (preventer). Explain why the preventer prevails.
In the witness example, impeachment (jarḥ) is a preventer that outweighs validation (ta'dīl).
5
Note the exception and its basis
In death illness acknowledgment, some later Hanafi jurists allowed division if the heir accepts. This shows that exceptions can exist when the preventer is removed.
This is based on the principle that acknowledgment can be divided (tajazzu' al-iqrār).

١ - يمنع الشريك من التصرف في المال المشترك بصورة تضر شريكه، لأن حق شريكه مانع وإن كان حق نفسه مقتضياً. وكذا أحد صاحبي السفل أو العلو في الأبنية ذات الطوابق.
