٤ - وقالوا في تزكية الشاهد (۳) : إذا وثقه بعض المزكين وجرحه بعض، أي طعن في صدقه وعدالته يرجّح جانب الجرح، فيرد القاضي شهادته .
·
(۱) ر: (ف) ١٥/٨١) وهذا مبني على الرأي المشهور في الاجتهاد الحنفي من عدم تجزؤ الإقرار ولو قيل بالنفاذ في حصة الأجنبي بناء على نظرية إمكان تجزؤ الإقرار لكان وجيهاً أيضاً. (۲) اشترطت نصوص الشريعة من الكتاب والسنة لقبول الشهادة أن يكون الشاهد عدلاً، أي ثقة. ولما كثر التزوير أوجب الفقهاء أن يسأل القاضي عن الشاهد أهل المعرفة به، فإذا وثقوه قبل شهادته، وإلا ردها . فهذا السؤال والتوثيق يسمي : تزكية الشاهد .
Chapter Ten: Universal Rules in Jurisprudence. The judge [has authority] over the hoarders regarding their hoarded wealth, even if that harms them - and he sells to repel the harm of hoarding from the public. - And it is permissible to impose price controls, i.e., fixing prices on sellers when they exceed and inflate prices. Likewise, it is permissible, rather obligatory, to demolish buildings adjacent to a fire to prevent its spread if its spread is feared.
26/81 - H - Repelling harms is better than securing benefits (al-Majallah article 30). Because harms have a tendency to spread and expand like plague and fire. So it is from wisdom and decisiveness to eliminate them in their cradle, even if that results in deprivation of benefits or delay thereof. Hence, the Lawgiver's concern to prevent prohibitions is stronger than His concern to fulfill commandments. And it is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'What I have forbidden you, avoid it; and what I have commanded you, do as much of it as you are able.' - On this basis, it is legally obligatory to prevent trade in prohibited things such as wine and intoxicants, even if they involve profits and economic benefits. - And the owner of a house is prevented from opening a window that overlooks the private quarters of his neighbor, even if he has benefit in it. - Likewise, every neighbor is prevented from using his property in a way that harms his neighbors, such as setting up an oil press or an oven that bothers neighbors with smell or smoke. - And the ignorant physician and the like are placed under interdiction. - And hoarding and transgression in prices are prevented as stated in the previous rule.
Chapter (81) Explanation of the Rules of the Majallah and their Classification
27/81 - T - When the preventer and the requirer conflict, the preventer is given precedence (al-Majallah article 46). That is, if a thing or action has drawbacks that necessitate its prohibition, and there are considerations that require its permissibility, its prohibition is given weight, because repelling harms takes precedence over securing benefits, as previously explained in the preceding article.
On this basis:
1 - A partner is prevented from disposing of joint property in a way that harms his partner, because the right of his partner is a preventer, even though his own right is a requirer. Likewise, either of the owners of the lower or upper floor in multi-story buildings.
2 - If a person in his death illness acknowledges a debt or a common asset to one of his heirs and to a stranger, it is not effective with respect to the stranger either, due to the presence of a preventer of effectiveness with respect to the heir.
3 - If a person claims to be the agent of the depositor for collecting the deposited item, and he has no evidence of agency, and the depositee acknowledges his agency, the depositee is not obliged to hand over the deposit to him, even though his acknowledgment would require him to deliver it, because the right of the depositor in the item requires not handing it over due to the possibility of his denial of the agency, as previously stated.
4 - They said regarding the validation of a witness: if some of those who validate him attest to his reliability and some impugn him, i.e., attack his truthfulness and uprightness, the side of impugnment is given weight, and the judge rejects his testimony.
Source pages p. 931

Commentary
This passage presents two foundational legal maxims from the Majallah al-Adliyyah. The first maxim (26/81) is: 'Repelling harms takes precedence over securing benefits.' The author explains that harms have a tendency to spread like epidemics and fires, so it is wise to eliminate them at their inception, even if it means forgoing some benefits. The Lawgiver's emphasis on avoiding prohibitions is stronger than on fulfilling commandments, as reflected in the hadith 'Avoid what I forbid; do what I command as much as you can.' Applications include: the state may force hoarders to sell at fair prices, impose price controls, demolish buildings to stop a fire from spreading, prohibit trade in intoxicants despite economic benefits, prevent a homeowner from opening a window that invades a neighbor's privacy, stop a neighbor from causing harm (e.g., smoke or smell), place an incompetent physician under guardianship, and prevent hoarding and price gouging.

The second maxim (27/81) is: 'When the preventer and the requirer conflict, the preventer is given precedence.' This is a sub-maxim derived from the first. When something has both drawbacks (requiring prohibition) and motivations (requiring permission), prohibition is given weight. Applications include: a partner cannot use joint property in a way that harms the other partner, because the other's right acts as a preventer; in death illness, an acknowledgment of debt to an heir and a stranger is ineffective for both because the preventer regarding the heir blocks the whole acknowledgment; a depositee is not bound to deliver goods to a claimed agent based on his own acknowledgment, due to the depositor's right; and in witness validation, impeachment outweighs validation—the judge rejects testimony if some witnesses are impeached, even if others validate them.

Key Terms
درء المفاسد (dar' al-mafāsid): Repelling harms; preventing detrimental acts or states
جلب المنافع (jalb al-manāfiʿ): Securing benefits; bringing about advantages
المانع (al-māniʿ): The preventer: a legal impediment that blocks a ruling from taking effect
المقتضي (al-muqtaḍī): The requirer: a legal ground that demands a certain ruling

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which ruling is derived from the maxim 'Repelling harms takes precedence over securing benefits'?
A) A judge must compensate a hoarder for losses incurred by forced sale.
B) Trade in intoxicants is prohibited even if it yields economic profits.
C) A neighbor may set up a loud workshop if it benefits him.
D) A homeowner may open a window overlooking his neighbor's courtyard if it provides light to his house.

Key Takeaway
When harm and benefit conflict, prevention of harm is the priority; when two rights clash, the one that blocks takes precedence.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

Harm Cannot Become Old: The Criterion for Respecting Antiquity
Explains the maxim 'harm cannot be old' and the criterion for respecting antiquity in Hanafi law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qā‘ida 'al-ḍarar lā yakūnu qadīman' to classify a harmful ancient structure as either protected by antiquity (due to possibility of legitimate acquisition) or subject to removal (because inherently prohibited).

Core Idea
Only ancient harm that could have been legitimately acquired is protected by time; inherently prohibited harm, however old, must be removed.

This lesson delves into the Hanafi maxim 'Harm cannot become old' (الضرر لا يكون قديماً) and its role as a restriction on the broader maxim that respects antiquity. You have learned that antiquity alone does not sanctify any action; only those ancient harms that could have been legitimately acquired by contract, partition, or similar causes are respected. The passage provides a detailed criterion (ḍābiṭ) to distinguish between protected and unprotected antiquity, and applies it to classic cases like waterspouts, drains, windows, and cesspools. As a mufti, you will use this criterion to resolve property disputes where time is pleaded as a defense.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two maxims in tension
Recognize that the base maxim 'القدم لا يغير الحكم' (antiquity does not change the ruling) is restricted by 'الضرر لا يكون قديماً' (harm cannot become old). The lesson explains that only non-harmful antiquity is respected.
2
Understand the criterion (ḍābiṭ)
The criterion: if the right in question could have been acquired via a legitimate Shar‘ī means (e.g., contract, partition), then antiquity presumes lawful origin; otherwise, it does not.
This criterion was deduced by Shaykh Ahmad al-Zarqa from the rationales of the jurists.
3
Apply to the furū‘
Work through the four cases: road drain (public harm) → removal; window overlooking women’s quarters (inherently prohibited) → removal; cesspool contaminating wells → repair or removal; easement/passage with unknown origin → respected.
Each case illustrates the operation of the criterion.
4
Identify the istithnā’
The exception: when the harm is not inherently prohibited and could have been legitimately acquired, antiquity is respected (e.g., a passage or drain that only harms a specific neighbor, as this can be compensated).
The base maxim governs this exception.
5
Formulate a fatwa
Given a new case (e.g., an old balcony that drips rainwater onto a neighbor’s courtyard), use the criterion to decide if it can be removed.
Practice applying the criterion in a structured fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (۸۱) شرح قواعد المجلة وتصنيفها لم يعرف بأي وجه ،وضع، لأنَّ قِدَمه دليل مشروعية وضعه، كما لو كان موضوعاً في الأصل لقاء عوض، أو بطريق القسمة، ونحو ذلك من الأسباب المشروعة. فمنعه ضررٌ لصاحبه لا مسوغ له، إذ لو ساغ ذلك لأدى إلى إهدار معظم الحقوق بعد تقادم عهودها . أما إذا أحدث الشخص شيئاً من ذلك في ملك غيره إحداثاً معروفاً مبدؤه، فإنه يكلف إثبات سببه ومسوّعه الشرعي، أو يمنع. وأما إذا كان الشيء القديم ليس من الأمور المشروعة في الأصل فإنه ضرر يزال ولا عبره لقدمه، وهذا موضوع القاعدة التالية : ٢٩/٨١ - ك - الضرر لا يكون قديماً» (م/٧). أي لا يحتج بتقادمه، وليس المراد عدم إمكان التقادم فيه. هذه القاعدة قيد لسابقتها : أي إن المنافع والمرافق التي يحترم قدمها هي التي لا تكون ضرراً ممنوعاً من أصله شرعاً. فإذا كانت كذلك وجبت إزالتها ولا عبرة لقدمها. - فلو كان لدار ميزاب أو مجرى أقذار على الطريق مضر بالجماعة يزال مهما تقادم، لأنه غير مشروع في الأصل، إذ الشرع لا يُقر لأحد بوجه من الوجوه حقاً يضر بالعامة. - وكذا لو كان لإنسان نافذة وطيئة تطل على مقر نساء جاره، فإنها تزال ولو كانت قديمة إلا أن تكون دار الجار هي المحدثة تحت المطل (۱). على أننا قد رأينا تحت القاعدة السابقة أن الممر والمسيل والبالوعة وأمثالها في دار الغير أو أرضه يُحترم ،قدمها ولو كان وجودها يقيد حريته تصرفه وذلك ضرر له . (1) يلحظ في هذا المقام أن القوانين العقارية النافذة لدينا منذ عهد الاحتلال الفرنسي قد جاءت بأحكام جديدة في النوافذ والمطلات لم تراع فيها هذه الاعتبارات الشرعية. ٩٩٩ الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية في الفقه فما هو الضابط في التفريق بين ما يحترم قدمه وإن أضرّ بالغير، وبين لأنه ضرر غير مشروع؟ ما لا يحترم لقد استنبط والدي الشيخ أحمد الزرقا رحمه الله تعالى في شرح لهاتين القاعدتين ضابطاً تؤيده تعليلات نصوص الفقهاء، خلاصته : أن ما يمكن استحقاقه على الغير بأحد الأسباب المشروعة يُحترم قدمه ؛ وإلا فلا. I فالممر والمسيل مرتفق يمكن أن يستحق بالتعاقد أو القسمة في الملك المشترك وغير ذلك من الأسباب المشروعة. فيُحترم قدمه عند جهل سببه .. وضرر الخصم منه في تقييد حريته يستحق عليه أمثاله بعقد المعاوضة وغيره لأن كل حق يكتسبه شخص على آخر هو منفعة المكتسبه، ونوع ضرر خاص للآخر، ومع ذلك يستحق عليه بعقد المعاوضة وغيره، ويورث عن مستحقه . ففي حالة القدم يمكن حمله على أنه في الأصل وضع بأحد الطرق المشروعة . أما إضرار جماعة الناس وإزعاجهم في مرافقهم العامة، فإنه لا يمكن أن يستحق عليهم شرعاً بوجه من الوجوه. وكذا الإطلال على مقرّ نساء الغير، فإنه فسق لا يستحق ولا يقره الشارع . ومثل ذلك ما لو كانت بالوعة الدار القديمة تنجس آبار الجيران، فإن قدمها على هذه الصورة لا يحترم كما نص عليه الفقهاء في هذا المقام (إذ لا يجوز شرعاً تنجيس المياه الطاهرة دون مصلحة ولا يستحق ذلك على أحد). ولكن صاحبها يكلف إزالة ضررها بإصلاح مجراها على شكل يضبطها ويمنع تسربها، فيبقى عندئذ مجرَّد مرورها من المنافع التي يمكن استحقاقها على الغير بوجه مشروع فإن لم يمكن إصلاحها وضبطها، أو لم يفعله صاحبها تزال . ۱۰۰۰
Section (81): Explanation and Classification of the Maxims of the Majalla. If [the origin of a thing] is not known in any manner, its antiquity is evidence of the legitimacy of its placement, as if it was originally placed in exchange for a consideration, or by way of partition, and similar legitimate causes. Preventing it is a harm to its owner with no justification, for if that were permissible, it would lead to the squandering of most rights after the lapse of their covenants. As for when a person creates something of that sort in another's property in a known manner with a known beginning, he is required to prove its cause and its legitimate justification, or else it is prevented. And as for when the ancient thing is not of the permissible matters in origin, then it is a harm that must be removed, and no consideration is given to its antiquity. This is the subject of the following maxim: 'Harm cannot become old' (Article 7 of the Majalla). That is, one cannot argue based on its aging; it does not mean that aging is impossible in it. This maxim is a restriction on its predecessor: that is, the benefits and facilities whose antiquity is respected are those which are not a harm prohibited by its origin according to the Law. If they are such, their removal becomes obligatory, and antiquity is disregarded. For example, if a house has a waterspout or a sewage drain onto the road that harms the public, it must be removed no matter how old it is, because it is not permissible in origin, since the Law does not establish for anyone in any way a right that harms the public. Likewise, if a person has a window or a small opening overlooking the private quarters of his neighbor's women, it must be removed even if it is old, unless the neighbor's house was newly built under the view. However, we have seen under the previous maxim that a passage, drain, cesspool, and the like in another's house or land are respected in their antiquity even if their existence restricts his freedom to dispose and that is a harm to him. (1) It is noted here that the property laws in effect with us since the French occupation have introduced new rulings regarding windows and views that did not observe these Shari considerations. Chapter Ten: The Universal Maxims in Fiqh. So what is the criterion to distinguish between what is respected in its antiquity even if it harms another, and what is not respected because it is an unlawful harm? My father, Shaykh Ahmad al-Zarqa (may Allah have mercy on him) deduced in his commentary of these two maxims a criterion supported by the rationales of the jurists' texts. Its summary: that which can be claimed as a right over another by one of the legitimate means is respected in its antiquity; otherwise, not. For passage and drainage are facilities that can be claimed by contract or partition in common property and other legitimate means. So their antiquity is respected when the cause is unknown. And the harm to the opponent from it in restricting his freedom is something that can be claimed from him by contract for consideration and otherwise, because every right acquired by a person over another is a benefit to the acquirer and a type of specific harm to the other, and yet it is claimed by contract for consideration and otherwise, and is inherited from its owner. In the case of antiquity, it can be assumed that originally it was placed by one of the legitimate means. As for harming the public and disturbing them in their public utilities, it can never be claimed over them by Shari in any way. Likewise, overlooking the private quarters of another's women is immorality that cannot be claimed and the Lawgiver does not approve of it. Similar to that is if the old cesspool of a house contaminates the wells of neighbors, then its antiquity in this condition is not respected, as the jurists have stated in this context (since it is not permissible by Shari to contaminate pure water without benefit and it cannot be claimed over anyone). However, its owner is required to remove its harm by repairing its channel in a way that confines it and prevents its leakage. Then at that point its mere passage remains among the benefits that can be claimed over another by a legitimate means. If it cannot be repaired and confined, or if the owner does not do it, it is removed.
Source pages p. 934

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi legal maxim 'Harm cannot become old' (الضرر لا يكون قديماً) and its relationship with the earlier maxim that respects antiquity. The author explains that this maxim is a restriction (قيد) on the previous one: the benefits and facilities whose antiquity is respected are only those that are not inherently prohibited by the Law. If they are inherently harmful (e.g., a drain that harms the public), they must be removed regardless of their age. The crucial question is: what distinguishes between respected antiquity and non-respected antiquity? The author presents a criterion (ضابط) derived by his father, Shaykh Ahmad al-Zarqa: if the right could have been acquired through a legitimate cause (such as contract, partition, etc.), then its antiquity is respected when the cause is unknown; otherwise, it is not. For example, a passage or drain on a neighbor's property can be acquired legitimately, so if found to be ancient, it is presumed to have been placed lawfully and cannot be removed. However, harming the public (e.g., obstructing a road) or invading privacy (e.g., a window overlooking women's quarters) can never be legitimately acquired, so antiquity does not justify it. Similarly, a cesspool contaminating neighbors' wells is inherently harmful, but if the harm can be fixed by repairing the channel to prevent leakage, the channel itself may remain as a legitimate right. The passage combines legal reasoning with practical applications, illustrating the balancing of rights and prevention of harm.

Key Terms
الضرر لا يكون قديماً (al-ḍarar lā yakūnu qadīman): This maxim means that one cannot argue from the mere passage of time that a harm has become permissible; if the harm is prohibited by the Law in its origin, it must be removed regardless of its age.
ضابط (ḍābiṭ): A criterion or controlling principle that distinguishes between cases where antiquity is respected and where it is not, based on the possibility of lawful acquisition.
الممر والمسيل (al-mamar wa al-masīl): A path (right of way) and a drainage channel; these are examples of rights that can be acquired through contract, partition, or custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the criterion (ḍābiṭ) presented in the passage, when is the antiquity of a harmful thing respected?
A) When it has existed for a very long time (over 30 years).
B) When the harm is to the public at large.
C) When the right could have been acquired through a legitimate cause such as contract or partition.
D) When the owner of the harmed property does not object.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'Harm cannot become old' restricts the protection of antiquity to only those harms that could have been legitimately acquired by a Shar‘ī means; inherently prohibited harms must be removed regardless of age.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

The Maxim 'Hardship Brings Ease' (المشقة تجلب التيسير)
Explaining the fourth major maxim with its proofs and scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the textual proofs for the maxim 'المشقة تجلب التيسير' and apply its scope by distinguishing between ordinary and excessive hardship.

Core Idea
Only excessive hardship, not ordinary strain, triggers legal concessions.

This lesson unpacks one of the five universal maxims of Islamic law: 'Hardship brings ease'. The author grounds it in clear Quranic verses and hadith, then draws a critical distinction between unavoidable ordinary hardship (inherent in duties) and excessive hardship that calls for relief. As a mufti, this distinction is the bedrock for issuing concessions correctly.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the maxim and its Arabic phrasing.
Commit 'المشقة تجلب التيسير' to memory; it is one of the five major qawaid kubra. Note its article number (م/١٧) from the Majalla.
This maxim is universal across all schools.
2
Identify the text-based proofs (dalil naqli).
Locate the Quranic verses (2:185, 22:78) and the hadith (رفع الخطأ والنسيان) cited as the basis. Understand that these texts establish the principle of ease.
The proofs show Allah's intent to remove hardship, not ordinary difficulty.
3
Apply the key restriction: excessive vs. ordinary hardship.
The maxim applies only to hardship that exceeds normal bounds (المشقة المتجاوزة للعادة). Ordinary exertion (e.g., work, prayer while healthy) does not trigger concessions; to grant ease there would be negligence.
This is the most common point of misapplication.
4
Consider the scope and exceptions.
The maxim covers all fiqh chapters where rukhsah (concession) appears. The sub-maxim (dabt) clarifies: 'المشقة المبيحة للتخفيف هي المتجاوزة للعادة'. Ordinary hardship remains under the original ruling (عزيمة).
Related maxims: 'الضرر يزال' and 'إذا ضاق الأمر اتسع' complement this rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (۸۱) شرح قواعد المجلة وتصنيفها
٣٠/٨١ - القاعدة الرابعة: «المشقة تجلب التيسير» (م/١٧).
المكلف لأن في المشقات إحراجاً، والحرج ممنوع عن
الشريعة .
بنصوص
هذه القاعدة هي من القواعد الخمس الكبرى التي تقدم أنها تعتبر من
أسس الشريعة في جميع المذاهب (ر: ف ۳/۸۰).
ومستندها قول الله تعالى في القرآن الكريم : ويُرِيدُ اللهُ بِكُمُ الْيُسْرَ
وَلَا يُرِيدُ بِكُمُ الْمُسْرَ ) [١٨٥/٢].
وقوله تعالى أيضاً : ﴿وَمَا جَعَلَ عَلَيْكُمْ فِي الدِّينِ مِنْ حَرَع ) [٧٨/٢٢]. وكذلك قول الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام: إن الله تعالى وضع عن
أمتي الخطأ والنسيان وما استكرهوا عليه» (ر: ف ۲/۱۰ ب). وأمثال ذلك من نصوص الشريعة التي تبين سماحتها وسجاحتها . والمراد بالمشقة المنفية بالنصوص، والداعية إلى التخفيف والترخيص بمقتضى القاعدة، إنما هي المشقة المتجاوزة للحدود العادية .
أما المشقة الطبيعية في الحدود العادية التي يستلزمها عادة أداء الواجبات والقيام بالمساعي التي تقتضيها الحياة الصالحة، فلا مانع منها بل لا يمكن انفكاك التكليفات المشروعة عنها لأن كل واجب لا يعرى عن مشقة : كمشقة العمل واكتساب المعيشة والصلاة والصيام في حال الصحة، وبذل النفقات الواجبة والجهاد لدفع غوائل الأعداء. فلكل من هذه التكاليف والواجبات نوع مشقة تستلزمها طبيعته وتختلف بحسبه درجتها. وهذا لا ينافي التكليف ولا يوجب التخفيف لأن التخفيف فيه عندئذ إهمال وتفريط (ر : الموافقات للشاطبي (۱۱۹/۲ - (۱۲۳) (۱) .
(۱) للشاطبي رحمه الله بيان بديع وتفصيل رائع في هذا المقام من الموافقات ج ۲ ص / ۱۱۹ - ۱۶۸ فلينظر .
Chapter (81): Explanation of the Rules of the Majalla and Their Classification. 30/81 - The Fourth Maxim: 'Hardship brings ease' (Article 17). Because there is difficulty in hardships, and difficulty is prohibited by the texts of the Sharia. This maxim is among the five major maxims which have been mentioned as being among the foundations of Sharia in all schools (see: Section 3/80). Its basis is the saying of Allah Almighty in the Noble Qur'an: 'Allah intends for you ease and does not intend for you hardship' [2:185]. And His saying also: 'And He has not placed upon you in the religion any hardship' [22:78]. Likewise, the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Indeed Allah has removed from my nation error, forgetfulness, and what they are coerced into' (see: Section 2/10b). And similar texts of the Sharia that show its leniency and tolerance. What is intended by hardship negated by the texts, which calls for alleviation and concession according to the maxim, is only hardship that exceeds the ordinary limits. As for natural hardship within ordinary limits, which is normally entailed by performing duties and carrying out efforts required for a righteous life, there is no objection to it; rather, the prescribed obligations cannot be separated from it, because every duty is not free from hardship: such as the hardship of work, earning a livelihood, prayer and fasting in a state of health, paying obligatory expenditures, and jihad to repel the evils of enemies. For each of these obligations and duties there is a kind of hardship required by its nature, and its degree varies accordingly. This does not contradict obligation nor necessitate alleviation, because alleviation in that case would be negligence and dereliction (see: al-Muwafaqat by al-Shatibi, 2/119-123). (1) Al-Shatibi, may Allah have mercy on him, has a wonderful exposition and excellent detail in this regard in al-Muwafaqat, vol. 2, pp. 119-168, so refer to it.
Source pages p. 936

Commentary
This passage introduces and defines the fourth major legal maxim: 'المشقة تجلب التيسير' (Hardship brings ease). The author explains that this maxim is universally recognized across schools, derived from clear Quranic verses and Prophetic hadith. The key nuance is that the hardship that triggers concessions is only the unusual hardship (المشقة المتجاوزة للحدود العادية), not the ordinary strain inherent in fulfilling religious duties and earning a living. Concessions such as shortening prayer on travel, breaking fast when ill, or using water for purification when available, are based on this principle. However, ordinary hardships like effort in working or praying while healthy do not justify concessions; to grant relief there would be negligence. This is a fundamental rule in issuing fatwas: distinguishing tolerable difficulty from excessive hardship.

Key Terms
المشقة (al-mashaqqah): Hardship that exceeds ordinary limits, which is the cause for legal concessions.
التيسير (al-taysīr): Ease or concession provided by Sharia in situations of hardship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what kind of hardship triggers the concession (تيسير) under the maxim 'المشقة تجلب التيسير'?
A) Any hardship, even minor.
B) Hardship that exceeds the ordinary limits.
C) Only physical hardship, not emotional.
D) Hardship that continues for more than three days.

Key Takeaway
Only hardship exceeding ordinary limits triggers legal concessions; ordinary difficulty is an inseparable part of duty.

The Fourth Major Maxim: Hardship Begets Ease
Expounding the fourth major maxim with proofs and applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'المشقة تجلب التيسير', identify its scriptural proofs, and apply it to at least five furuat from the text.

Core Idea
Only excessive, abnormal hardship triggers ease; ordinary difficulty is inherent and does not justify concessions.

This lesson unpacks one of the five universal maxims of Islamic law: Hardship begets ease. While the Shariah aims to remove difficulty, not every burden qualifies for relief. We explore proofs from Quran, Sunnah, and the precise scope of 'al-mashaqqah al-mu'tadilah' (normal hardship) versus 'al-mashaqqah al-ghalibah' (excessive hardship). The maxim governs rulings across worship, contracts, crimes, and procedure.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its rank
Begin by memorizing the maxim in Arabic: 'المشقة تجلب التيسير' and note it is a major (kubra) universal maxim accepted by all schools.
It is article 17 of the Majalla.
2
Identify the scriptural proofs
Find the Quranic verses (2:185, 22:78) and the hadith on lifting error/forgetfulness/duress. Understand how these texts establish the principle of removing hardship.
The key phrase is 'لا يريد بكم العسر' (does not desire hardship).
3
Distinguish excessive vs normal hardship
Understand the critical nuance: the maxim only applies to hardship that exceeds normal bounds ('al-mashaqqah al-mutajawizah li al-hudud al-'adiyyah'). Ordinary toil of worship, work, and obligations is not grounds for concession.
Al-Zarqa cites al-Shatibi's Muwafaqat on this point.
4
Explore the furuat (applications) in the text
Review each of the nine furuat given: illness/travel, prescription, error, forgetfulness, duress, agent ignorance, necessity, witness probity, custom. For each, connect the hardship to the concession.
Note that some concessions are full exemptions (e.g., Friday prayer dropped) while others are partial (e.g., contract voidable).
5
Apply the maxim to a new case
The student should be able to take a novel scenario (e.g., financial hardship due to inflation) and reason whether the maxim triggers a concession or not, using the 'excessive hardship' criterion.
Always check if the hardship is exceptional and Shariah-recognized.

المكلف لأن في المشقات إحراجاً، والحرج ممنوع عن الشريعة بنصوص.
هذه القاعدة هي من القواعد الخمس الكبرى التي تقدم أنها تعتبر من أسس الشريعة في جميع المذاهب (ر: ف ۳/۸۰).

وفي الحقوق المدنية يبطل الغلط العقد تارة، ويسلب لزومه تارة أخرى، كما تقدم (ر: ف (٥/٣٦) والغلط في العقد نوع من الخطأ.

Chapter (81): Explanation of the rules of the Majalla and their classification
30/81 - The fourth rule: 'Hardship begets ease' (Majalla article 17).
Because in hardships there is difficulty, and difficulty is prohibited by the texts of the Shariah.
This rule is among the five major rules which were mentioned as being among the foundations of the Shariah in all schools (see: paragraph 3/80).
Its basis is the saying of Allah the Most High in the Noble Quran: 'Allah desires ease for you and does not desire hardship for you' [2:185]. And also His saying: 'And He has not placed upon you in religion any difficulty' [22:78]. And also the saying of the Messenger, peace be upon him: 'Verily Allah the Most High has lifted from my nation error, forgetfulness, and what they are forced to do' (see: paragraph 2/10b). And similar texts of the Shariah that show its leniency and ease.
What is meant by hardship that is negated by the texts, and which calls for lightening and concession by virtue of the rule, is only the hardship that exceeds normal limits.
As for natural hardship within normal limits that is usually entailed by performing obligations and undertaking efforts required for a good life, there is no prohibition against it; rather, it is impossible for legitimate obligations to be free from it, because every duty is not devoid of hardship: like the hardship of work and earning a living, prayer and fasting in a state of health, spending required maintenance, and jihad to repel the attacks of enemies. For each of these duties and obligations there is a type of hardship that its nature entails and its degree varies accordingly. This does not contradict obligation nor necessitate lightening, because lightening in such matters would be negligence and dereliction (see: al-Muwafaqat by al-Shatibi, vol. 2, pp. 119-123).
- On this basis, illness and travel are made a concession in religious obligations, some of which are dropped like Friday prayer, and some deferred like fasting.
- And likewise in civil rights: prescription (passage of time preventing claim of right) is prevented or suspended by excuses such as lack of legal capacity and travel; otherwise it would be difficult for right holders.
- And error in actions and transactions changes the general rulings established for them in cases of intentionality, because taking to task for error as if intentional would be a great hardship for the obligated: - So in crimes, accountability for error is lightened to a lower degree commensurate with the assumption of only a failure to take precaution on the part of the mistaken person.
- And in civil rights, mistake invalidates the contract sometimes, and removes its binding effect at other times, as previously mentioned (see: paragraph 5/36); mistake in contract is a type of error.
- And forgetfulness relieves the person from responsibility for leaving religious obligations, although it has no effect on rights and judicial decisions.
- And duress prevents binding the coerced person to his contract, and if it is overwhelming duress, then the Shariah establishes an excuse for the coerced person regarding what he does of prohibited acts that he is forced to do.
- And the ignorance of the agent of his principal's dismissal of him – his agency continues and his contracts are valid, to avert hardship (see: paragraph 6/40).
- And necessity permits the prohibited things that one is compelled to do for the duration of its existence, as we will see (see: paragraph 32/81)
- And the lack of probity of witnesses justifies accepting the testimony of the better and then the next best, as previously mentioned (see: paragraph 7/77)
- And consideration of people's custom in many places is based on this rule.
Source pages p. 936

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm presents the fourth of the five major maxims: 'المشقة تجلب التيسير' (Hardship begets ease). This maxim is universally accepted across all Islamic legal schools. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, first provides the scriptural evidences: Quranic verses from Surah al-Baqarah (2:185) and al-Hajj (22:78) emphasizing Allah's desire for ease, and the hadith on the lifting of error, forgetfulness, and duress from the Ummah. These establish the principle that the Shariah aims to remove excessive difficulty.

A critical nuance is made: only hardship that exceeds normal limits triggers ease. Ordinary hardship inherent in fulfilling obligations (like working, praying, fasting, jihad) is not grounds for concessions. The maxim applies to abnormal hardship such as illness, travel, error, forgetfulness, duress, necessity, and the unreliability of witnesses. The author lists concrete applications across ibadat and mu'amalat:
- In religious duties: illness and travel allow dropping (e.g., Jumu'ah) or postponing (e.g., fasting) obligations.
- In civil rights: prescription (statute of limitations) is suspended due to mental incapacity or travel.
- Error in actions changes rulings compared to intentional acts; in crimes punishment is reduced, in contracts error may void or remove binding effect.
- Forgetfulness exempts from liability for religious acts but affects rights only if it causes loss.
- Duress invalidates contracts and excuses coerced prohibited acts if overwhelming.
- Ignorance of a principal's revocation of agency keeps the agent's authority valid to avoid hardship.
- Necessity permits otherwise unlawful acts.
- Lack of probity among witnesses allows accepting the best available.
Customary practice is also grounded in this maxim, as it reflects the community's ease. This chapter demonstrates how the maxim operates as a tool for legal rulings that align with the Shariah's spirit of removing difficulty.

Key Terms
المشقة (al-mashaqqah): Hardship that exceeds normal limits in performing obligations or in life circumstances
التيسير (al-taysir): Lightening of a ruling or granting a concession to remove hardship
الحرج (al-haraj): Difficulty or constraint that is prohibited in the Shariah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi maxim 'Hardship begets ease', which of the following is a correct application in mu'amalat?
A) An agent who is unaware that his principal dismissed him continues to transact validly until he learns of the dismissal.
B) A person who forgets to pay a debt is excused from payment.
C) An illness that prevents one from working entitles one to cancel all contracts.
D) Any customary practice is automatically valid without further conditions.

Key Takeaway
The fourth major maxim 'Hardship begets ease' operates only on excessive, abnormal hardship, and its applications span from dropped obligations to contractual leniencies, always balancing the Shariah's mercy with its discipline.

The Maxim: Hardship Brings Ease and Its Applications
Explaining the major maxim and listing its applications in worship, torts, contracts, and custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-mashaqqah tajlib al-taysir' to identify concessions in worship, contracts, and custom, and distinguish its scope from necessity (darura).

Core Idea
Hardship is a gateway to ease: Sharia relaxes rules when strict application would cause exceptional difficulty.

This lesson explores the major legal maxim 'al-mashaqqah tajlib al-taysir', one of the five universal maxims of Hanafi fiqh. We will unpack its meaning, see how it operates across multiple chapters, and learn its limitation: it does not override definitive texts (nass qat'i) unless necessity (darura) reaches that level.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its rank
Recognize that 'al-mashaqqah tajlib al-taysir' is a kubra (major) maxim with universal scope across all fiqh chapters.
It is one of the five great maxims alongside 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha', 'al-yaqin la yuzalu bi al-shakk', etc.
2
Understand the condition for application
Note that 'mashaqqah' must be exceptional (kharij 'an al-‘ada), not everyday inconvenience.
Trivial difficulty does not trigger the maxim.
3
Work through the furuat
Examine each of the ten applications in the passage: illness/travel concessions, prescription, error, forgetfulness, coercion, agency, necessity, witness probity, custom.
For each, see how the original strict rule is eased
4
Recognize the exception (istithna)
When the strict rule is based on a definitive text (nass qat'i), hardship does not ease it; only darura can override.
Example: the prohibition of pork cannot be lifted by mere difficulty, only by starvation (darura).
5
Apply the maxim to a new case
Practice using the maxim: given a scenario, decide if the hardship qualifies and which concession applies.
This builds the mufti's mental habit for casuistry.

Chapter Ten: The General Maxims in Fiqh. And on this basis, illness and travel were made a concession in religious obligations: some are dropped like Friday prayer, and some are delayed like fasting. Likewise in civil rights: prescription (passage of time preventing claim of a right) is prevented or suspended by excuses such as minority or travel; otherwise it would cause hardship to the right-holders. Error in acts and transactions changes the general rulings established for them in the case of intent, because holding someone accountable for error as if intent would be great hardship for the obligated persons. So in criminal matters, accountability for error is reduced to a lower level proportionate only to the assumption of neglect of caution by the mistaken person. In civil rights, error sometimes invalidates the contract and sometimes removes its binding force, as previously (ref: 5/36), and error in a contract is a type of mistake. Forgetfulness exempts a person from responsibility for leaving religious obligations, although it has no effect on rights and judgment. Coercion prevents binding the coerced person to his contract; and if it is overwhelming coercion, the Law establishes for the coerced an excuse regarding forbidden acts that he is forced to do. The agent's ignorance of his principal's dismissal means his agency continues and his contracts are valid, to avoid hardship (ref: 6/40). Necessity permits the prohibited things that one is compelled to, as long as it lasts (ref: 32/81). The lack of probity of witnesses permits accepting the testimony of the better among them, as previously (ref: 7/77). Consideration of people's custom in many situations is based on this maxim, as long as it does not conflict with the foundations of Sharia, because failure to observe custom and not consider its authority would be great hardship for people (ref: 3/72).
Source pages p. 937

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the major legal maxim 'al-mashaqqah tajlib al-taysir' (hardship brings ease). The author presents it as the foundational principle underlying numerous rulings. He begins by listing concrete applications: illness and travel as concessions in worship (dropping Friday prayer, postponing fasting); the suspension of prescription due to excuses like minority or travel; reduced liability for error in torts and contracts; the invalidation or non-binding effect of contracts due to mistake; exemption for forgetfulness in religious duties but not in civil rights; the effect of coercion in nullifying consent and excusing compelled acts; the continued validity of an agent's acts if he is unaware of his principal's revocation; necessity permitting ordinarily prohibited acts; and accepting less-than-ideal witnesses due to scarcity of upright people. Finally, consideration of custom (urf) is also derived from this principle. All these rulings illustrate that Shari'a avoids imposing hardship by relaxing rules when circumstances make strict application burdensome.

Key Terms
مشقة (mashaqqah): Hardship or difficulty that exceeds the normal tolerable level in a given context
تيسير (taysir): Easing of the original strict ruling, either by substitution, reduction, or postponement

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT an application of the maxim 'Hardship brings ease'?
A) Illness excuses from Friday prayer.
B) Error in a contract can invalidate it.
C) Intentional crime is punished equally as mistake.
D) Custom is considered in rulings to avoid hardship.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-mashaqqah tajlib al-taysir' is a foundational principle of Hanafi fiqh that allows easing of rulings when hardship is exceptional, but it does not override definitive texts; only necessity does.

The Maxim: When a Matter Constricts, It Expands
Explaining a key legal maxim with its converse and application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'Al-amr idha daqa ittasa'a' to a fiqh scenario and explain the temporal boundary of the leniency.

Core Idea
Temporary hardship brings temporary ease; the ruling returns when necessity ends.

This lesson explores a foundational Hanafi legal maxim: when circumstances become so constrained that applying the normal ruling causes unbearable hardship, the law provides an expansion—a temporary relaxation. However, this leniency is not permanent; once the constraint lifts, the original ruling must be restored. Understanding this dynamic is essential for any mufti who must navigate between strict principle and practical mercy.

Guided Path
1
Articulate the maxim and its converse
State the Arabic maxim 'al-amr idha daqa ittasa'a' and its opposite 'idha ittasa'a daqa'. Understand that these are two sides of the same principle: hardship triggers expansion, ease triggers contraction.
The maxim is codified in Majalla article 18.
2
Define the triggering conditions
Identify what constitutes 'constriction' (diq): a temporary necessity (darurah) or exceptional circumstance that makes the original ruling burdensome (haraj) for the individual or community.
Haraj is more than mere difficulty; it is oppressive hardship.
3
Describe the legal response
Explain that the law responds by lightening or expanding the ruling to remove hardship. The expansion is proportionate to the constraint—only as much as needed to alleviate the burden.
The principle is related to 'mashaqqa tujlib al-taysir'.
4
Recognize the temporal boundary
Highlight that the expansion is tied to the duration of the necessity. When the constraint ends, the original ruling automatically resumes. This prevents the temporary exception from becoming a permanent change.
Failing to revert is a common error.
5
Apply to a furu' scenario
Take a concrete example (e.g., missing a fard salah due to extreme illness) and trace how the maxim allows the ruling to expand (combining prayers or using tayammum), then contract when health returns.
The example must be from the Hanafi tradition.

Source Text

٣١/١ - ٢ - الأمر إذا ضاق اتسع (م/۱۸).

31/1-2: When a matter becomes constrained, it expands (Majalla article 18). And likewise, when it expands, it contracts (see Mir'at al-Majalla under this rule). That is, if a temporary necessity occurs for an individual or group, or an exceptional circumstance arises, making the original ruling legislated for normal situations burdensome and oppressive for the obligated persons, to the point that it causes them difficulty in application, then that ruling is lightened and expanded for them to facilitate ease, as long as that necessity persists. This is the meaning of 'When the matter becomes constrained, it expands.' Then when the necessity eases and disappears, the ruling returns to its original state. This is the meaning of 'When it expands, it contracts.' This rule is in the same meaning as its predecessor, and what is said about that one is said about this one.
Source pages p. 938

Commentary
This passage explains a key legal maxim from the Majalla, 'Al-amr idha daaqa ittasa'a' (When a matter becomes constrained, it expands) and its converse 'Idha ittasa'a daaqa' (When it expands, it contracts). The author clarifies that this maxim applies when a temporary necessity or exceptional circumstance makes the normal ruling unduly burdensome. In such a case, the law provides leniency by expanding the options available, but this leniency is temporary: once the necessity disappears, the original stricter ruling returns. This maxim is considered a corollary of the earlier principle of hardship (mashaqqa) bringing ease.

Key Terms
ضرورة (darurah): A situation of extreme necessity that compels departing from the normal rule to prevent harm
حرج (haraj): Burdensome difficulty or constraint that makes compliance oppressive

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'Al-amr idha daqa ittasa'a', when does the leniency cease?
A) It remains forever once granted
B) When the necessity or hardship ends
C) At the discretion of the judge
D) Never, because the ruling is permanently relaxed

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-amr idha daqa ittasa'a' provides temporary ease during hardship, but the converse 'idha ittasa'a daqa' mandates return to the original ruling once the constraint is removed.

Al-Darurat Tubihu al-Mahdhurat: Necessities Permit Prohibitions
Explaining the grand maxim with Quranic proof and applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim 'Necessities permit prohibitions,' cite its Quranic proof, apply it to cases of necessity, and identify the three prohibitions that are never permitted even under duress.

Core Idea
Necessity temporarily lifts prohibitions, but three acts are always forbidden: kufr, murder, and adultery.

This lesson examines the second grand maxim of Hanafi legal theory: 'Necessities permit prohibitions.' Derived from the Quranic exception, this maxim governs situations of extreme necessity. However, it has limits: three prohibitions are never permitted no matter the duress. Understanding this maxim is essential for issuing fatwas in medical, survival, and coercion cases.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its Quranic basis
State the maxim 'Al-darurat tubihu al-mahdhurat.' The proof is the Quranic phrase 'illa ma idturirtum' (except what you are compelled to) which appears after prohibitions in verses such as 6:119 and 6:145.
The exceptional clause is called 'istithna' and is a key usul principle.
2
Define the threshold of necessity
Necessity (darurah) is defined as a situation where refraining from the prohibited leads to death, unbearable weakness, or a health ailment. Certainty of death is not required; intolerable harm suffices.
The author gives his own view: the criterion is that the harm of refraining is greater than the harm of committing the prohibited act.
3
Apply the maxim to common cases
Examples: a doctor may uncover private parts for treatment; a starving person may eat carrion or pig to prevent death. The permission is limited to the extent necessary (الضرورات تقدر بقدرها).
Other cases are analogized; the principle is general.
4
Identify the three absolute exceptions
According to Hanafi jurists, three prohibitions are never permitted even under threat of death: kufr (disbelief), qatl (murder), and zina (adultery).
These are exceptions to the maxim; the act remains prohibited even if punishment is mitigated.
5
Explain the legal consequences for each exception
For kufr: outward pretence is allowed if the heart is firm, but martyrdom is better. For murder: the coerced may not kill; the coercer bears criminal liability. For zina: the hadd penalty is dropped but the act is still sinful.
The reasoning is that the harms of these acts outweigh the harm of death to the individual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۳۲/۸۱ - ب - الضرورات تبيح المحظورات» (م/۲۱).
هذه القاعدة مستفادة من استثناء القرآن حالات الاضطرار الطارئة ظروف استثنائية بقوله تعالى: إِلَّا مَا اضطررتم ) بعد تعداده طائفة من المحرمات .
- فيجوز كشف الطبيب على عورات الأشخاص إذا توقفت عليها مداواتهم.
- ومن خشي الهلاك جوعاً أو عطشاً أو غَصَصاً في مكان ما، ولم يجد سوى الميتة أو الخنزير أو الخمر، أو مال شخص آخر غير مضطر مثله، جاز له بل وجب عليه أن يتناول منه لدفع الهلاك.
وعلى ذلك يقاس غيره.
والذي أراه أنه لا يشترط تحقق الهلاك بالامتناع عن المحظور، بل يكفي أن يكون الامتناع مفضياً إلى وهن لا يحتمل، أو آفة صحية. والميزان في ذلك أن يكون ما يترتب على الامتناع أعظم محذوراً من إتيان المحظور فصيانة النفس عن الهلاك أعظم وأوجب من صيانة مال الغير واحترام حقه، أو من أكل الخنزير أو الميتة.
على أن من المحظورات ما لا يباح بحال وإن كان الاضطرار يخفف إثم بعضها وهي عند فقهائنا ثلاثة : الكفر، والقتل، والزني.
فالتهديد ولو بالقتل لإكراه الشخص على الكفر لا يعذره في الكفر، اطمئنان قلبه بالإيمان على أن له التظاهر به مع الصبر على القتل دون هذا التظاهر أفضل له، لأنه أعظم لقوة الإسلام في نظر أعدائه .
- والتهديد ولو بالقتل لإكراه الشخص على قتل غيره لا يبيع له قتله وإن كان لو فعل لا قصاص عليه، بل على من أكرهه كما تقدم (ر: ف ٥/٣٤ الحاشية) .
وإكراه الرجل ولو بالقتل على الزني لا يبيح له الإقدام شرعاً، وإن كان يُسقط عنه عقوبة الحد، لأن للزنى مفاسد ذات ذيول في الأسرة والمجتمع أعظم من موت شخص. (الدر المختار في الإكراه ٨٥/٥).
32/81-B: 'Necessities permit prohibitions' (Majalla article 21). This rule is derived from the Quran's exception for emergency situations in exceptional circumstances in His saying: 'except what you are compelled to' after enumerating a group of prohibited things. So it is permissible for a doctor to uncover the private parts of persons if their treatment depends on it. And whoever fears destruction from hunger, thirst, or choking in a place, and finds only carrion, pig, wine, or the property of another person who is not similarly compelled, it is permissible—rather obligatory—for him to take from it to ward off destruction. And other such cases are analogized to this. My view is that it is not required that destruction be certain by refraining from the prohibited; rather it suffices that refraining leads to unbearable weakness or a health ailment. The criterion is that what results from refraining is greater in harm than committing the prohibited. For protecting life from destruction is greater and more obligatory than protecting the property of another or respecting his right, or than eating pig or carrion. However, some prohibitions are never permitted, even if necessity lessens the sin of some of them, and according to our jurists they are three: disbelief, murder, and adultery. So threatening even by death to compel a person to disbelief does not excuse him in disbelief, although he may outwardly pretend while his heart is at peace with faith, but to endure death without such pretense is better for him because it is greater for the strength of Islam in the eyes of its enemies. And threatening even by death to compel a person to kill another does not permit him to kill, though if he did so, there is no retaliation on him, but rather on the one who compelled him, as previously mentioned (see F 5/34 footnote). And compelling a man, even by death, to commit adultery does not permit him to proceed legally, though it drops the punishment of hadd, because adultery has far-reaching corruptions in family and society greater than the death of a person. (Al-Durr al-Mukhtar on coercion, 5/85).
Source pages p. 938

Commentary
This passage explains the second grand maxim 'Al-darurat tubihu al-mahdhurat' (Necessities permit prohibitions). The author cites the Quranic basis: the exception phrase 'illa ma idturirtum' (except what you are compelled to) after listing prohibitions. Applications include: a doctor uncovering private parts for treatment; a starving person eating carrion or pig when necessary. The author notes his own view: the threshold is not absolute certainty of death but intolerable weakness or health harm. He then discusses three prohibitions that are never permitted even under duress: kufr (disbelief), qatl (murder), and zina (adultery). For kufr, outward show is allowed if the heart is firm, but martyrdom is better. For murder, the coerced person may not kill, but the threatener bears liability. For zina, the act is still prohibited but the hadd penalty is dropped due to duress. This section is crucial for understanding limits of the maxim.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrah): Coercion that overcomes a person's will, typically by threat of death or severe harm
اضطرار (idtirar): Compulsion due to extreme necessity that threatens life or limb
ضرورة (darurah): Necessity that permits otherwise prohibited acts

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Killing an innocent person when coerced
C) Using wine as medicine when no alternative

Key Takeaway
Necessity permits normally prohibited acts, but three prohibitions (kufr, murder, adultery) remain absolutely forbidden even under duress.

Necessities are Estimated by Their Measure
Constraining the scope of necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'Al-darurat tuqaddaru bi qadariha', explain its function as a restriction on necessity-based permissions, and apply it to determine the permissible limit in a given case of duress.

Core Idea
Necessity permits only the minimum needed — no more.

The previous maxim established that necessity permits what is normally prohibited. But does that give a blank check? This maxim, 'Necessities are estimated by their measure,' steps in as a crucial restriction. It teaches us that even under duress, you may only do the absolute minimum to remove the harm — no excess is allowed.

Guided Path
1
Appreciate the maxim as a qayd
Recognize that this rule restricts the broader principle about necessity. Understand that it prevents abuse of the permission.
This maxim is explicitly called a 'qayd' (restriction) on the previous one.
2
Identify the two sub-rules
First, if the harm of the prohibited act outweighs the harm of enduring the necessity, it is not permitted at all. Second, even when permitted, only the minimal amount to ward off harm is allowed.
The passage mentions both conditions.
3
Apply the furu example
Take the classic case of a starving person: they may eat only enough to prevent death, not to the point of satisfaction or fullness.
This illustrates the quantitative limit.
4
Relate to mufti's practice
When issuing a fatwa, the mufti must ensure the permission is strictly limited to the necessity's measure and not exceeded.
This is crucial for fatwa integrity.

33/81-C: 'Necessities are estimated by their measure' (Majalla article 22). This rule is a restriction on the previous one. Thus, a prohibition that is greater in harm than enduring the necessity is not permitted by necessity. Likewise, necessity only permits from prohibitions the amount that wards off [the harm].
Source pages p. 939

Commentary
This passage presents the third maxim 'Al-darurat tuqaddaru bi qadariha' (Necessities are estimated by their measure). The author explains that this rule is a restriction (qayd) on the previous maxim about necessity permitting prohibitions. It means that necessity does not give a blanket permission; rather, one may only do the minimum required to remove the harm. Additionally, if the harm of the prohibited act is greater than the harm of enduring the necessity, it is not permitted at all. This maxim ensures that the principle of necessity is not abused.

Key Terms
قيد (qayd): A restriction or condition that limits the application of a rule
ضرورة (darura): Necessity that triggers a ruling permitting otherwise prohibited acts

Key Terms from Source Passage

The Maxim 'Necessities are Measured by Their Extent' and Its Applications
Explaining the maxim الضرورات تقدر بقدرها and its furuat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'الضرورات تقدر بقدرها' to new cases by identifying the extent of necessity and the exceptions.

Core Idea
Necessity permits the prohibited only to the extent needed; when danger ends, the prohibition returns.

This lesson examines one of the foundational maxims of Islamic jurisprudence: 'Necessities are measured by their extent.' It acts as a crucial limitation on the broader principle that necessity permits prohibitions. Through concrete examples from medical treatment, you will learn how the law balances between allowing life-saving measures and preventing overreach. You will also learn the three prohibitions that are never permitted, even under duress.

Guided Path
1
Understand the maxim's function
This maxim qualifies the previous maxim about necessity. It ensures that only the minimum prohibition is lifted.
2
Identify the illa (effective cause)
The illa is the extent of harm; only as much as needed to remove the danger is allowed.
3
Apply to medical furuat
Doctor uncovers only as much as needed; prefer same-gender practitioner.
4
Recognize the istithna' (exception)
The three prohibitions (kufr, qatl, zina) are not permitted even under necessity.
5
Determine when the prohibition returns
Once the danger ceases, the original prohibition is reinstated.

Fiqh: Chapter Ten: The Universal Maxims. - Whoever fears destruction from hunger, thirst, or choking in a place, and finds nothing but carrion, pig, wine, or the property of another person who is not in similar need, it is permissible for him—nay, obligatory—to partake of it to ward off destruction. And by analogy, other cases are judged. What I deem is that actual destruction from abstaining from the prohibited is not a condition; rather, it suffices that abstaining leads to unbearable weakness or a health affliction. The criterion is that the consequence of abstaining should be a greater evil than committing the prohibited, because safeguarding life is greater and more obligatory than safeguarding another's property and respecting his right, or than eating pig or carrion. However, among the prohibited matters are those that are never permissible under any circumstances, although duress may lighten their sin. According to our jurists, these are three: disbelief, murder, and fornication. Thus, threatening even with death to coerce a person into disbelief does not excuse him in disbelief, [provided his heart is firm in faith?]; but outward compliance with it while [inner faith remains?]... but killing without this outward compliance is better for him, because it is greater for the strength of Islam in the eyes of its enemies. - And threatening even with death to coerce a person to kill another does not permit him to kill him; but if he did, there is no retaliation upon him, but rather upon the one who coerced him, as previously mentioned (see footnote 5/34). - And coercing a man even with death to commit fornication does not permit him to proceed with it legally, although it may remove the prescribed punishment, because fornication has far-reaching corruptions in family and society greater than the death of a person. (al-Durr al-Mukhtar on coercion 5/85). [Article] 33/81 - c - 'Necessities are measured by their extent' (M/22). This rule is a restriction on its predecessor. So by necessity, a prohibition whose harm is greater than enduring it is not permitted; and duress only permits of prohibitions the amount needed to repel danger; and it is not permissible to exceed, and when the danger ceases, the prohibition returns. - So if there is need to treat the private parts, the doctor uncovers only as much as needed. - And regarding a woman, it is not permissible for a man to look at her private parts for treatment or childbirth if a woman who is skilled is available, because the sight of one gender on its own is less prohibited. [Article] 34/81 - d - 'Duress does not nullify the right of another' (M/33). Duress is considered an excuse that removes sin and exempts from punishment for transgression against another's right, but there is no necessity to nullify the right. - So one who is compelled to save himself from destruction by eating another's food must guarantee its value. - As for one who is coerced by irresistible duress to destroy another's property, the guarantee of the property's value is upon the one who directly coerced, because he is more deserving of bearing the consequence than the doer. [Article] 35/81 - e - 'Need takes the place of necessity, whether general or specific' (M/32). Necessity is more severe in degree and impetus than need; necessity is something whose violation leads to danger, as in irresistible duress and fear of death from hunger. As for need, it is something whose non-fulfillment leads to hardship and difficulty. The meaning of 'general' is that the need is widespread; the meaning of 'specific' is that the need is for a group among them, like the people of a country or a profession, similar to what preceded in the meaning of general and specific custom; and 'specific' here does not mean individual. The meaning of the rule is that exceptional legislative facilitations are not limited to
Source pages p. 939

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of several interrelated legal maxims from the Mecelle, centered on the principle that 'Necessities are measured by their extent.' The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains the practical application of these maxims in Hanafi fiqh. He begins by discussing the classic case of dire necessity: a person facing death from hunger, thirst, or choking may consume otherwise forbidden substances (carrion, pig, wine) or even another's property if the owner is not similarly desperate. This is not merely permitted but obligatory. Al-Zarqa adds his own view that actual death is not required; rather, if abstention leads to unbearable weakness or health harm, the necessity criterion is met. The key is that the harm of abstention must outweigh the harm of committing the prohibition. However, he emphasizes that three prohibitions are never permissible regardless of duress: disbelief (kufr), murder (qatl), and fornication (zina). Duress may reduce the sin or punishment but does not make these acts lawful. For example, a person threatened with death for refusing to utter words of disbelief is not excused if he complies, provided his heart remains firm in faith; in fact, it is better for him to endure death than to outwardly comply, as this strengthens Islam. Similarly, killing another under threat is not permitted; the coerced killer bears no retaliation, but the one who coerced is liable. Fornicating under threat is also not permitted, though the prescribed penalty (hadd) may be waived due to the duress, as the societal harm of fornication outweighs the individual's death. The passage then presents the maxim 'Necessities are measured by their extent' (Mecelle article 22), which restricts the previous maxim about necessity permitting prohibitions. This means that necessity only permits that which is necessary to remove the harm, no more; once the danger is gone, the prohibition returns. Examples include a doctor uncovering only the necessary area for treatment, and preferring a female practitioner over a male for women's private areas when possible. Next, the maxim 'Duress does not nullify the right of another' is explained: duress is an excuse that removes sin and punishment but does not eliminate the right. For instance, one who eats another's food under duress must still compensate (guarantee its value). If someone is irresistibly coerced to destroy another's property, the guarantor is the one who coerced, not the actual destroyer. Finally, the maxim 'Need takes the place of necessity, whether general or specific' distinguishes necessity from need: necessity involves danger, while need involves hardship. General need affects everyone or a large group (e.g., people of a region), while specific need affects a subgroup (e.g., a profession). This maxim allows for legislative facilitations (e.g., concessions) even in cases of need, not only dire necessity.

Key Terms
الضرورة (al-darura): A condition of dire need where violation leads to danger of death or severe harm
الاضطرار (al-idtirar): A state of being compelled or forced by necessity or duress

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When the danger that justified a necessity ceases, what happens to the prohibition?
A) The prohibition remains permanently lifted.
B) The prohibition returns.
C) It depends on the type of prohibition.
D) It is reassessed by a scholar to decide.

Key Takeaway
Necessity permits the prohibited only to the minimum extent needed; once danger ends, the original ruling resumes.

The Three Absolute Prohibitions Under Duress: Kufr, Qatl, Zina
Detailed Hanafi rulings on kufr, qatl, zina under duress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that disbelief, murder, and fornication are never permissible under duress, and identify the consequences when they occur.

Core Idea
Disbelief, murder, and fornication are never lawful, even under threat of death; duress may lighten sin or remove hadd but does not make the acts permissible.

While necessity can lift many prohibitions, Hanafi jurists identify three that remain absolutely prohibited: disbelief, murder, and fornication. This lesson delves into the rationale: these acts cause greater societal and spiritual harm than the death of an individual. You will learn the nuanced rulings on each and how they differ from other necessity cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three absolute prohibitions
List the three: kufr (disbelief), qatl (murder), zina (fornication/adultery).
These are mentioned in the source as exceptions to the general permissibility under duress.
2
Understand the Hanafi justification
The inherent harm (mafsada) of these acts is greater than the harm of death; protecting faith, life of another, and lineage overrides individual compulsion.
3
Analyze the ruling on kufr under duress
Outward compliance is not permitted if the heart remains firm; resisting martyrdom is preferable as it strengthens Islam.
The verse 'except him who is forced while his heart is secure in faith' is interpreted as applying to outward utterance without inner conviction.
4
Analyze the ruling on qatl under duress
Killing another under threat is not permitted; if done, liability falls on the coercer, not the coerced killer.
The coerced killer bears no qisas or diya; the coercer is liable.
5
Analyze the ruling on zina under duress
Fornication under duress is not permitted; however, the hadd punishment is removed due to the duress, but the act remains sinful.
The societal corruption from zina outweighs the individual's death.

Fiqh: Chapter Ten: The Universal Maxims. - Whoever fears destruction from hunger, thirst, or choking in a place, and finds nothing but carrion, pig, wine, or the property of another person who is not in similar need, it is permissible for him—nay, obligatory—to partake of it to ward off destruction. And by analogy, other cases are judged. What I deem is that actual destruction from abstaining from the prohibited is not a condition; rather, it suffices that abstaining leads to unbearable weakness or a health affliction. The criterion is that the consequence of abstaining should be a greater evil than committing the prohibited, because safeguarding life is greater and more obligatory than safeguarding another's property and respecting his right, or than eating pig or carrion. However, among the prohibited matters are those that are never permissible under any circumstances, although duress may lighten their sin. According to our jurists, these are three: disbelief, murder, and fornication. Thus, threatening even with death to coerce a person into disbelief does not excuse him in disbelief, [provided his heart is firm in faith?]; but outward compliance with it while [inner faith remains?]... but killing without this outward compliance is better for him, because it is greater for the strength of Islam in the eyes of its enemies. - And threatening even with death to coerce a person to kill another does not permit him to kill him; but if he did, there is no retaliation upon him, but rather upon the one who coerced him, as previously mentioned (see footnote 5/34). - And coercing a man even with death to commit fornication does not permit him to proceed with it legally, although it may remove the prescribed punishment, because fornication has far-reaching corruptions in family and society greater than the death of a person. (al-Durr al-Mukhtar on coercion 5/85). [Article] 33/81 - c - 'Necessities are measured by their extent' (M/22). This rule is a restriction on its predecessor. So by necessity, a prohibition whose harm is greater than enduring it is not permitted; and duress only permits of prohibitions the amount needed to repel danger; and it is not permissible to exceed, and when the danger ceases, the prohibition returns. - So if there is need to treat the private parts, the doctor uncovers only as much as needed. - And regarding a woman, it is not permissible for a man to look at her private parts for treatment or childbirth if a woman who is skilled is available, because the sight of one gender on its own is less prohibited. [Article] 34/81 - d - 'Duress does not nullify the right of another' (M/33). Duress is considered an excuse that removes sin and exempts from punishment for transgression against another's right, but there is no necessity to nullify the right. - So one who is compelled to save himself from destruction by eating another's food must guarantee its value. - As for one who is coerced by irresistible duress to destroy another's property, the guarantee of the property's value is upon the one who directly coerced, because he is more deserving of bearing the consequence than the doer. [Article] 35/81 - e - 'Need takes the place of necessity, whether general or specific' (M/32). Necessity is more severe in degree and impetus than need; necessity is something whose violation leads to danger, as in irresistible duress and fear of death from hunger. As for need, it is something whose non-fulfillment leads to hardship and difficulty. The meaning of 'general' is that the need is widespread; the meaning of 'specific' is that the need is for a group among them, like the people of a country or a profession, similar to what preceded in the meaning of general and specific custom; and 'specific' here does not mean individual. The meaning of the rule is that exceptional legislative facilitations are not limited to

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of several interrelated legal maxims from the Mecelle, centered on the principle that 'Necessities are measured by their extent.' The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains the practical application of these maxims in Hanafi fiqh. He begins by discussing the classic case of dire necessity: a person facing death from hunger, thirst, or choking may consume otherwise forbidden substances (carrion, pig, wine) or even another's property if the owner is not similarly desperate. This is not merely permitted but obligatory. Al-Zarqa adds his own view that actual death is not required; rather, if abstention leads to unbearable weakness or health harm, the necessity criterion is met. The key is that the harm of abstention must outweigh the harm of committing the prohibition. However, he emphasizes that three prohibitions are never permissible regardless of duress: disbelief (kufr), murder (qatl), and fornication (zina). Duress may reduce the sin or punishment but does not make these acts lawful. For example, a person threatened with death for refusing to utter words of disbelief is not excused if he complies, provided his heart remains firm in faith; in fact, it is better for him to endure death than to outwardly comply, as this strengthens Islam. Similarly, killing another under threat is not permitted; the coerced killer bears no retaliation, but the one who coerced is liable. Fornicating under threat is also not permitted, though the prescribed penalty (hadd) may be waived due to the duress, as the societal harm of fornication outweighs the individual's death. The passage then presents the maxim 'Necessities are measured by their extent' (Mecelle article 22), which restricts the previous maxim about necessity permitting prohibitions. This means that necessity only permits that which is necessary to remove the harm, no more; once the danger is gone, the prohibition returns. Examples include a doctor uncovering only the necessary area for treatment, and preferring a female practitioner over a male for women's private areas when possible. Next, the maxim 'Duress does not nullify the right of another' is explained: duress is an excuse that removes sin and punishment but does not eliminate the right. For instance, one who eats another's food under duress must still compensate (guarantee its value). If someone is irresistibly coerced to destroy another's property, the guarantor is the one who coerced, not the actual destroyer. Finally, the maxim 'Need takes the place of necessity, whether general or specific' distinguishes necessity from need: necessity involves danger, while need involves hardship. General need affects everyone or a large group (e.g., people of a region), while specific need affects a subgroup (e.g., a profession). This maxim allows for legislative facilitations (e.g., concessions) even in cases of need, not only dire necessity.

Key Terms
الإكراه الملجىء (al-ikrah al-mulji'): Irresistible duress that threatens life or limb, leaving no choice
الحد (al-hadd): Prescribed fixed punishment for certain offenses

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person is forced to kill another under threat of death, what is the ruling on the killer?
A) He is guilty of murder and liable for qisas.
B) He is not liable for qisas, but the act remains sinful and not permissible.
C) He is excused because he was forced, and no sin is recorded.
D) He must pay diya to the victim's family.

Key Takeaway
Disbelief, murder, and fornication are never permitted under duress; duress only lightens the worldly penalty, not the moral prohibition.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
8 min
Related Maxims: Duress Does Not Nullify Rights and Need Takes the Place of Necessity
Twin maxims: duress does not nullify rights and need takes place of necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxims 'الاضطرار لا يبطل حق الغير' and 'الحاجة تنزل منزلة الضرورة' to new cases by distinguishing between necessity and need and identifying the liability for property under duress.

Core Idea
Duress is an excuse that removes sin and punishment, but it does not nullify the rights of others; need, when general or specific, can be treated like necessity for legislative facilitations.

This lesson explores two complementary maxims that refine the application of necessity. The first, 'Duress does not nullify the right of another,' clarifies that while duress may excuse the agent, the rights of third parties remain intact—compensation must still be paid by the coercer. The second, 'Need takes the place of necessity,' extends facilitations beyond dire emergencies to situations of general or specific hardship, providing flexibility in legislation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the maxim 'Duress does not nullify the right of another'
Duress is an excuse that removes sin and punishment, but it does not eliminate the right of the owner.
2
Apply to eating another's food under duress
The person must guarantee the value of the food consumed, even though he is excused from sin.
3
Apply to coerced destruction of property
If someone is irresistibly coerced to destroy another's property, the coercer is liable for the value, not the actual destroyer.
The coercer is considered the direct cause (mubashir) in Hanafi law.
4
Understand the distinction between necessity and need
Necessity (darura) involves danger; need (haja) involves hardship. Need can be general (affecting many) or specific (affecting a group).
5
Apply the maxim 'Need takes the place of necessity'
Legislative facilitations (rukhsas) can be extended to cases of general need, not just dire necessity.
Example: allowing a cash waqf due to widespread need.
