Commentary
This passage is an exposition of several interrelated legal maxims from the Mecelle, centered on the principle that 'Necessities are measured by their extent.' The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains the practical application of these maxims in Hanafi fiqh. He begins by discussing the classic case of dire necessity: a person facing death from hunger, thirst, or choking may consume otherwise forbidden substances (carrion, pig, wine) or even another's property if the owner is not similarly desperate. This is not merely permitted but obligatory. Al-Zarqa adds his own view that actual death is not required; rather, if abstention leads to unbearable weakness or health harm, the necessity criterion is met. The key is that the harm of abstention must outweigh the harm of committing the prohibition. However, he emphasizes that three prohibitions are never permissible regardless of duress: disbelief (kufr), murder (qatl), and fornication (zina). Duress may reduce the sin or punishment but does not make these acts lawful. For example, a person threatened with death for refusing to utter words of disbelief is not excused if he complies, provided his heart remains firm in faith; in fact, it is better for him to endure death than to outwardly comply, as this strengthens Islam. Similarly, killing another under threat is not permitted; the coerced killer bears no retaliation, but the one who coerced is liable. Fornicating under threat is also not permitted, though the prescribed penalty (hadd) may be waived due to the duress, as the societal harm of fornication outweighs the individual's death. The passage then presents the maxim 'Necessities are measured by their extent' (Mecelle article 22), which restricts the previous maxim about necessity permitting prohibitions. This means that necessity only permits that which is necessary to remove the harm, no more; once the danger is gone, the prohibition returns. Examples include a doctor uncovering only the necessary area for treatment, and preferring a female practitioner over a male for women's private areas when possible. Next, the maxim 'Duress does not nullify the right of another' is explained: duress is an excuse that removes sin and punishment but does not eliminate the right. For instance, one who eats another's food under duress must still compensate (guarantee its value). If someone is irresistibly coerced to destroy another's property, the guarantor is the one who coerced, not the actual destroyer. Finally, the maxim 'Need takes the place of necessity, whether general or specific' distinguishes necessity from need: necessity involves danger, while need involves hardship. General need affects everyone or a large group (e.g., people of a region), while specific need affects a subgroup (e.g., a profession). This maxim allows for legislative facilitations (e.g., concessions) even in cases of need, not only dire necessity.

Key Terms
الحاجة (al-haja): A condition where non-fulfillment leads to hardship but not danger
الضمان (al-daman): Liability or guarantee to compensate for loss or damage

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'الاضطرار لا يبطل حق الغير', what is the ruling for a person who eats another's food under duress?
A) He is not liable because he was forced.
B) He must guarantee the value of the food.
C) He must repent but no financial liability.
D) He shares liability with the coercer.

Key Takeaway
Duress excuses sin and punishment but does not nullify property rights; need can qualify for legal concessions when it is general or specific.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
6 min
Necessity Does Not Annul the Right of Another (Majalla 33)
Explains the maxim and its two applications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-iḍṭirār lā yubṭil ḥaqq al-ghayr' to determine liability when necessity forces a violation of another's right.

Core Idea
Necessity excuses sin but does not eliminate liability.

This lesson examines the first maxim from the Majalla: 'Necessity does not annul the right of another.' While necessity may exempt a person from sin and punishment when forced to transgress another's right, it does not void the right itself. The obligation to compensate remains. We explore two key scenarios: one who eats another's food to save his life, and one who is coerced into destroying property. Understanding this maxim is crucial for fatwa-giving in cases of duress and necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its legal effect
State the maxim in Arabic and English. Understand that necessity removes sin and punishment (liability in the Hereafter) but does not annul the civil liability (damān).
The maxim is a qā‘idah kulliyah governing compensation in cases of necessity.
2
Apply the maxim to the first scenario: eating another's food
A person compelled by hunger to eat another's food must guarantee its price. The necessity is an excuse for the act, but the owner's right to compensation remains intact.
The ḥājah here is individual and extreme (ḍarūrah).
3
Apply the maxim to the second scenario: coerced destruction of property
If a person is coerced (ikrāh mulji‘) to destroy another's property, the liability falls on the coercer, not the coerced actor. This is because the coerced person is merely an instrument.
This differs from the first scenario: in coercion, the direct perpetrator is not liable; the one who caused the coercion is.
4
Distinguish between the two scenarios
In the first, the actor acts voluntarily out of necessity; in the second, the actor acts involuntarily under duress. The liability shifts accordingly.
Both are covered by the same maxim: the right of the owner is not annulled.
5
Formulate the fatwa for a modern case
Consider a modern scenario (e.g., a starving person taking food; a person forced at gunpoint to break someone's phone). Apply the maxim to determine liability.
The muftā bihi is the clear ruling from the Hanafi school as expressed in the Majalla.

D – Necessity does not annul the right of another (Majalla 33). Rather, necessity is considered an excuse that removes sin and exempts from punishment for transgressing the right of another, but there is no necessity to annul the right. So one who is compelled to avert destruction by eating another's food must guarantee its price. E – Need takes the place of necessity, whether general or specific (Majalla 32). Necessity is a higher degree and more compelling than need: necessity is that whose non-compliance entails risk, as in coercive duress and fear of death by hunger. As for need, it is that whose non-compliance entails hardship and difficulty. By 'general' is meant that the need is comprehensive, and by 'specific' is meant that the need is for a group such as the people of a town or a profession, analogous to what was mentioned earlier regarding the meaning of general and specific custom; and by 'specific' is not meant that it is individual. The meaning of the maxim is that exceptional legislative facilitations are not limited to cases of compelling necessity, but rather the needs of the community, which are below necessity, also necessitate exceptional facilitations, as is clear from the following applications: 1 – On this basis, provisions of the type of exceptions from its general rules were legislated in the original shari‘ah texts due to need. For example, it is reported in the Sunnah that the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) prohibited selling what is not in one's possession, but permitted salam (advance payment sale) (see: Fath al-Qadir 3/72). So the shari‘ah’s permission of salam, even though it is a sale of a non-existent item that the general text prohibited, is only due to the need of many people to sell their produce and obtain its price in advance before production to aid in production; therefore, prompt payment of the price was stipulated. 2 – Also on this basis, the jurists permitted in ijtihadi rulings the sale of fruits that appear in succession when some have appeared and loss is not feared, even if most are non-existent and have not yet appeared, due to need. 3 – They also permitted renting a bathhouse for a fee despite ignorance of the duration the person will stay and ignorance of the amount of water he will consume, also due to need. 4 – They also permitted wafa’ (a type of sale) as an exception from the rules due to need (see: Fath al-Qadir 46, sales section). 5 – All the rulings that the jurists prescribed to change due to change of time or its corruption were only prescribed as new rulings and changed due to need. 6 – Consideration of custom, whether general or specific, and giving it authority in rulings is only a response to the call of need, as explained earlier in the theory of custom. Commentary
This passage presents two important maxims from the Majalla. First, maxim 33: 'Necessity does not annul the right of another' (al-iḍṭirār lā yubṭil ḥaqq al-ghayr). The author explains that necessity is an excuse that removes sin and punishment for violating another's right, but the right itself remains intact. Thus, a person forced to eat another's food to save his life must compensate the owner. In contrast, if someone is coerced (ikrāh mulji‘) into destroying another's property, the liability falls on the coercer, not the coerced actor. Second, maxim 32: 'Need takes the place of necessity, whether general or specific' (al-ḥājah tunazzilu manzilat al-ḍarūrah). The author distinguishes between necessity (ḍarūrah), which involves a risk of destruction or death, and need (ḥājah), which involves hardship but not risk. Need, whether general (affecting all) or specific (affecting a group), also justifies exceptional legislative facilitations. Six furū‘ (applications) are given: (1) salam sale, an exception to the prohibition of selling non-existent items, permitted due to widespread need; (2) selling fruits that appear sequentially before full maturity, allowed due to need despite non-existence of most; (3) renting a bathhouse despite ignorance of duration and water consumption; (4) the wafā‘ sale (buy-back arrangement) as an exception; (5) changes in rulings due to changes in time and corruption of society; (6) giving authority to custom as a response to need. Finally, the author notes that necessity and need differ in two ways: (a) necessity permits the prohibited outright, even for an individual, while need does not reach that level? (The text breaks off but likely continues with the second difference.)

Key Terms
الاضطرار (al-iḍṭirār): Compulsion or necessity that forces one to take a prohibited action to avoid harm.
الإكراه الملجئ (al-ikrāh al-mulji‘): Overwhelming coercion that leaves no choice, such as threat of death.
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Guarantee or compensation for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a person is coerced (ikrāh mulji‘) to destroy another's property, who is liable for the value?
A) The person who actually destroyed the property.
B) The one who coerced the person.
C) Both are jointly liable.
D) No one is liable because of necessity.

Key Takeaway
Necessity excuses sin but does not annul the right of another; compensation is always due.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate

Need Takes the Place of Necessity (Majalla 32)
Explains the maxim and lists six applications with the reason of need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-ḥājah tunazzilu manzilat al-ḍarūrah' to identify when need justifies exceptional rulings in commercial transactions and customs.

Core Idea
Need, whether general or specific, can justify legal exceptions similar to necessity.

This lesson explores the second maxim: 'Need takes the place of necessity, whether general or specific.' The author distinguishes necessity (risk of destruction) from need (hardship), and explains that need—especially when widespread—can justify exceptional rulings, as demonstrated by six applications from classical fiqh. Understanding this maxim helps a mufti extend facilitations to new situations where hardship would otherwise occur.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish necessity (ḍarūrah) from need (ḥājah)
Necessity involves risk of life or destruction; need involves hardship but not risk. This difference is crucial for applying the maxim: need justifies exceptions, but not to the same degree as necessity.
The passage states: 'الضرورة أشد درجة ودافعاً من الحاجة'.
2
Define general and specific need
General need affects all people; specific need affects a group (e.g., a town or profession). Both can justify exceptions, but individual need may not.
The author explicitly excludes individual need from the maxim.
3
Analyze the first furū‘: salam sale
Salam (advance payment sale) is an exception to the prohibition of selling non-existent items, permitted due to general need of farmers and traders.
The ruling is based on a prophetic permission despite the general prohibition.
4
Analyze the remaining furū‘
Each application (fruit sale, bathhouse rental, wafā‘, changing rulings over time, considering custom) is permitted due to need. Understand the specific need in each case.
These are ijtihādī exceptions, not original texts.
5
Formulate the fatwa for a modern case
Apply the maxim to a new situation (e.g., online transactions with deferred delivery) to determine if need justifies an exception.
The mufti must assess whether the need is general or specific, and whether it rises to the level of hardship.

الحاجة (al-ḥājah): A need that, if unmet, causes hardship but not destruction.

The Fifth Major Rule: Custom is Authoritative and Its Branches
Expansion of the fifth major qawida with branches

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fifth major qawida, state its three derivative sub-rules, and apply the condition of predominance to evaluate a custom.

Core Idea
Custom is binding in law, but only if consistent or predominant.

This lesson introduces one of the five major legal maxims of the Hanafi school: 'Custom is authoritative' (al-ʿādatu muḥakkamah). This qawida establishes that customary practices, both verbal and practical, carry legal weight in distributing rights and obligations. The author presents three derivative sub-rules that clarify the conditions under which custom is binding—primarily that it must be consistent or predominant. Understanding this maxim is essential for muftis dealing with transactional and contractual issues where custom determines key terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main qawida
Locate the rule 'Custom is authoritative' and understand its meaning: customary practice is a binding source of law in Hanafi fiqh, especially in muʿāmalāt.
The author equates 'ādah with 'urf, covering both verbal and practical customs.
2
Recognize the three derivative sub-rules
The passage lists three sub-rules: (a) usage of people is a proof to be acted upon, (b) custom is only considered if consistent or predominant, (c) consideration is for the predominant, not the rare.
These sub-rules expand the main maxim and provide operational criteria.
3
Understand the conditions for custom
The second and third sub-rules establish that a custom must be either consistently applied (muṭṭarid) or predominant (ghālib). Isolated or rare practices are not authoritative.
This prevents arbitrary or unrepresentative practices from being enforced.
4
Apply the conditions to a case
Test a hypothetical custom: e.g., a local market practice of pricing by weight. Determine if it is consistent and predominant in that community.
If it is sporadic or only a minority practice, it does not qualify as binding custom.
5
Note exceptions
The qawida does not override explicit textual evidence (naṣṣ) or harmful customs. These exceptions are governed by other maxims like 'lā ijtihāda maʿa al-naṣṣ' and 'al-ḍararu yuzāl'.
A custom contradicting the Quran or Sunnah is invalid.

۱۰۰۸
81/36 - The Fifth Rule: Custom is authoritative (Art. 36).

What is meant by custom in this rule is 'urf, both verbal and practical. It expresses the status and consideration of custom in Islamic Sharia, and its governing authority among people in distributing rights and obligations between them in transactions.

This rule and the following rules branching from it have already been cited and relied upon on several occasions in the theory of custom, as they are sufficient as an explanation and exemplification of its application; so there is no need to repeat that here. (See: vol. 9, pp. 67 and 69.1).

What branches from it among the rules:

81/37 - a) The usage of people is a proof that must be acted upon (Art. 37).
This rule, like its origin "Custom is authoritative," includes both verbal and practical custom, because 'usage' here means custom and 'urf absolutely.

81/38 - b) Custom is only considered if it is consistent or predominant (Art. 41).
81/39 - c) Consideration is for the predominant and widespread, not the rare (Art. 42).
These two rules express some of the conditions for considering custom that have been presented in the discussion of its theory, namely the condition of consistency or predominance, and they have been clarified there (see: vol. 2, p. 70).
(1) 'Muhakkamah' with a shaddah on the open kaf, passive participle from 'tahkim' (arbitration).
1008
Source pages p. 943

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth major legal maxim of the Majalla: 'Custom is authoritative' (al-'adatu muhakkamah). The author defines 'adah as synonymous with 'urf in both its verbal and practical forms. This rule establishes custom as a binding source in distributing rights and obligations in transactions. The author notes that this rule and its branches have already been discussed in the theory of custom, so only a summary is given here. Three derivative sub-rules are mentioned: (a) The usage of people is a proof that must be acted upon (isti'mal al-nas hujja), which mirrors the main rule and covers both types of custom. (b) Custom is only considered if it is consistent (mutarid) or predominant (ghalib). (c) Consideration is for the predominant and widespread, not the rare. These last two sub-rules are conditions for the validity of custom: it must be either consistently applied or predominant in practice. The author points to earlier sections for detailed explanation.

Key Terms
عادة (ʿāda): A recurring practice; synonymous with 'urf, including both verbal and practical forms.
عرف (ʿurf): Custom (urf) which is either verbal (what people say) or practical (what people do), and is authoritative in law.
استعمال (istiʿmāl): The common usage of people; synonymous with custom in this context.
اطراد (iṭṭirād): Consistency or regularity of a custom (it is consistently observed).
غلبة (ghalaba): Predominance of a custom (it occurs more often than not).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition must a custom fulfill to be considered legally authoritative?
A) It must be written down
B) It must be consistent or predominant (mutarid or ghalib)
C) It must be agreed upon by all scholars
D) It must be approved by a judge

Key Takeaway
Custom is a binding authority in Hanafi law, but only when it is consistent or predominant and does not contradict explicit texts or cause harm.

The Maxim: Giving Effect to Speech is Preferable to Neglect
Explaining a major maxim with applications and a derivative rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'إعمال الكلام أولى من إهماله' to determine the legal effect of multiple utterances in contracts and declarations, distinguishing between judicial (qaḍā') and religious (diyānah) contexts.

Core Idea
Every utterance is presumed to have legal effect unless proven otherwise — give speech its full weight, not neglect.

This lesson introduces a fundamental maxim of Islamic legal interpretation: when speech can be given a meaningful effect, that interpretation must be chosen over one that renders it meaningless. Rooted in the rationality of legislative and transactional language, this maxim governs how judges and muftis handle repeated declarations, multiple acknowledgments, and similar scenarios. We will explore its definition, illustrative cases, and the crucial distinction between judicial ruling and religious duty.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the maxim's core principle
Define 'إعمال' (giving effect) and 'إهمال' (neglect). Understand why giving effect is preferred: rational speech is presumed purposeful.
The maxim is from the Majalla (article 1009).
2
Identify the default interpretive rule
The default is 'تأسيس' (establishing new meaning) over 'تأكيد' (affirmation). This rule drives the maxim's application.
Contrast with the sub-rule 'الأصل في الكلام الحقيقة' (default is literal meaning).
3
Apply the maxim to examples
Work through the three given furu': multiple acknowledgments, multiple bequests, repeated divorce pronouncements. In each case, each utterance is given independent effect in court.
These examples illustrate the maxim's broad scope.
4
Distinguish between qaḍā' and diyānah
In judicial setting (qaḍā'), external evidence of intention is not accepted without clear proof. In religious duty (diyānah), the speaker's true intention matters.
This distinction is crucial for muftis advising individuals.
5
Connect to derivative rules
The first derivative rule 'الأصل في الكلام الحقيقة' dictates that words are interpreted according to their literal meanings unless evidence indicates otherwise.
This guides the initial step in giving effect to speech.

47/81 - The sixth maxim: Giving effect to speech is preferable to neglecting it (M/1009).

Giving effect to speech: assigning a ruling to it. Neglecting it: not deriving a practical consequence from it. The meaning of the maxim is that if a word issued in a legislative or transactional context, when interpreted according to one possible meaning, yields no ruling, and when interpreted according to another meaning yields a ruling, then it must be interpreted in the meaning that produces a new ruling, because the opposite is neglect and nullification, and the speech of rational people is protected from nullification as much as possible (see: al-Qawāʿid al-Fiqhiyyah by Shaykh Maḥmūd Ḥamza p. 155).
- Accordingly: if a person acknowledges by a document that he owes another a thousand without specifying its origin, then later acknowledges in another document another thousand similarly, he is held liable for both thousands and his claim that they are the same is not accepted.
- Similarly, if someone bequeaths one hundred for charitable purposes, then bequeaths another hundred similarly, the bequest is considered two hundred, and the heirs' claim that he meant the second to be the same as the first is not accepted.
- Likewise, if a man divorces his wife by saying: 'You are divorced, you are divorced,' in the view of the court it is considered two divorces, and the second is not interpreted as an affirmation of the first, unless there is evidence indicating the intention of affirmation, because the default in speech is to establish something new, not to affirm.
However, in the realm of religious duty (diyānah) if he intended affirmation, they are one.
The meaning of 'establishing something new' (ta'sīs) is that the speech conveys a new benefit not conveyed by the previous one.
From this maxim branch several rules that delineate how to give effect to speech as required by this maxim and explain the preferred rational methods therein. They are the following rules:
What branches from it:
48/11 - a - The default in speech is the literal meaning (M/12).
The literal meaning is the original meaning for which the word was coined.
Its opposite is the metaphorical meaning, which is any other meaning for which the word was not coined, but between
Source pages p. 944

Commentary
This passage explains the sixth major legal maxim from the Majalla: 'Giving effect to speech is preferable to neglecting it.' It defines key terms: إعمال (giving effect) means assigning a legal ruling to the utterance, and إهمال (neglect) means not deriving any practical consequence. The core principle is that when a statement can be interpreted in multiple ways, the interpretation that yields a legal effect must be chosen over one that renders the statement meaningless. This is because rational speech is presumed to have purpose. Three illustrative examples are given: (1) multiple acknowledgments of debt are treated as separate unless proven otherwise; (2) multiple bequests are added together; (3) repeated pronouncements of divorce are counted as multiple divorces in judicial ruling (qaḍā'), even if the speaker intended affirmation—though in religious duty (diyānah) the intention matters. The principle of ta'sīs (establishing new content) is preferred over ta'kīd (affirmation) when no evidence indicates the latter. The passage then introduces the first derivative rule: 'The default in speech is the literal meaning' (al-aṣl fī al-kalām al-ḥaqīqah), defining literal and metaphorical meanings. This sub-rule guides how to determine the primary meaning that should be given effect.

Key Terms
إعمال الكلام (iʿmāl al-kalām): Assigning a legal ruling to an utterance such that it produces a practical effect.
إهمال الكلام (ihmāl al-kalām): Not deriving any legal consequence from an utterance.
التأسيس (al-ta'sīs): When speech establishes a new meaning not already conveyed.
حقيقة (ḥaqīqah): The original meaning for which a word was coined.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his wife 'You are divorced, you are divorced' without any evidence of intent. According to the maxim 'إعمال الكلام أولى من إهماله', how does the court treat these pronouncements?
A) As one divorce, because the second is likely affirmation.
B) As two divorces, because the default is to give each utterance independent effect.
C) As invalid, because divorce must be pronounced only once.
D) As one divorce only if the man says he meant affirmation; otherwise two.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'إعمال الكلام أولى من إهماله' ensures that every utterance in legal contexts carries independent effect unless proven otherwise, with the important distinction between judicial outcome and religious obligation.

Giving Effect to Speech and the Rule of Literal Meaning
Explains maxim and derived rule of literal meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'إعمال الكلام أولى من إهماله' and the rule 'الأصل في الكلام الحقيقة' to interpret contractual and testamentary expressions, distinguishing between ta'sis and ta'kid, and identifying when a contextual clue (qarina) shifts to majaz.

Core Idea
Give every word its legal effect: assume literal meaning unless a clear clue shows otherwise.

This lesson introduces two foundational principles that govern how we interpret legal speech — from confessions to contracts to divorce. Understanding these maxims is essential for any mufti analyzing ambiguous expressions. They ensure that the speech of rational people is given its full legal consequence, and that literal meanings are applied unless a clear contextual clue indicates metaphorical intent.

Guided Path
1
State the governing maxim and its rationale
The maxim 'إعمال الكلام أولى من إهماله' means that when a statement can be interpreted in a way that produces a legal effect versus a way that produces none, the effective interpretation must be chosen. This is because rational speech is presumed to have purpose.
This is a universal (kulliya) maxim applying across all fiqh chapters.
2
Define haqiqa and majaz with examples
Haqiqa is the original linguistic meaning; majaz is any derived meaning based on a relationship and a contextual clue. For example, 'court' (mahkama) literally means place, but can metaphorically mean the judges.
Hanafi usul holds that the default is haqiqa; majaz requires a clear qarina.
3
Apply the maxim to multiple utterances: ta'sis vs ta'kid
When a person repeats a statement (e.g., 'you are divorced, you are divorced'), the preferred interpretation is ta'sis (new effect) unless there is evidence of ta'kid (emphasis). Thus, two confessions of debt are treated as separate obligations.
In court (qada), both divorces count; in divine judgment (diyaana), inner intent may differ.
4
Identify when a qarina shifts interpretation
A contextual clue (qarina) can override the literal meaning. For example, saying 'I gift this to you for two dinars' introduces a price, which is a qarina showing that the word 'gift' is used metaphorically for sale.
The sub-maxim: 'If literal meaning is impossible, resort to metaphorical meaning'.
5
Practice with furuat from the text
Apply the maxims to cases like waqf 'for my children' (includes sons and daughters literally), waqf 'for the children of my children' (includes grandchildren of both sides), and bequest to 'awlad falan' (limited to direct offspring).
These furuat show the consistent application of the literal meaning rule.

- وكذا لو وقف على أولاد أولاده دخل في الوقف أولاد البنات . ولو أوصى لأولاد فلان، وكان لفلان أولاد صلبيون وحفدة، انصرفت الوصية إلى الأولاد الصلبيين - أي الطبقة الأولى من ذريته - لأنه المعنى الحقيقي للفظ «الأولاد».
Chapter Ten: Universal Legal Maxims. Giving effect to speech: assigning a ruling to it. Neglecting it: not deriving a practical consequence from it. The meaning of the maxim is that when a statement issued in the context of legislation or transaction can be interpreted in one of possible meanings without legal effect, and interpreted in another meaning with legal effect, it must be interpreted in the meaning that produces a new ruling, because to do otherwise is neglect and nullification, and the speech of rational people is protected from nullification as much as possible (ref: al-Qawa'id al-Fiqhiyya by Shaykh Mahmud Hamza, p. 155). Based on this: If a person confesses to owing one thousand to another by virtue of a document without stating its origin, and then confesses again in another document to another thousand likewise, he is demanded for two thousand and his claim that they are the same is not accepted. Similarly, if he bequeaths one hundred for charitable purposes, then bequeaths another hundred likewise, the bequest is considered two hundred, and the heirs' claim that he intended the second to be the same as the first is not accepted. Likewise, if he divorces his wife by saying: 'You are divorced, you are divorced,' it is considered in court as two divorces, and the second is not considered an emphasis of the first, unless there is evidence indicating the intent to emphasize, because the principle in speech is establishment (ta'sis) not emphasis (ta'kid). However, in the matter of religion (divine judgment), if he intended emphasis, they are one. The meaning of ta'sis is that the speech conveys a new benefit not conveyed by its predecessor. Several rules have branched from this maxim, outlining how to give effect to speech as required by this maxim, and explaining the preferred and reasonable methods for it. They are the following rules: 48/11 - A: 'The principle in speech is literal meaning' (M/12). 'Literal meaning' is the original meaning for which the word was coined. Its opposite is 'metaphorical meaning', which is any other meaning not coined for the word, but between it and the literal meaning there is a relationship that permits the speaker to intend it by that word, relying on a contextual clue indicating that he intended it instead of the literal meaning. For example, the word 'court' (al-mahkama) literally means the place of the judge. If it is used to mean the judge himself, as in 'the court ruled', it is metaphorical. The word 'killing' (al-qatl) literally means taking the soul. If it is used to mean painful beating, it is metaphorical. The meaning of the rule is that in giving effect to the speech of a speaker, whether a legislator, contractor, oath-taker, or others, it is obligatory to interpret his words according to their literal meanings when there is an absence of contextual clues that favor intended metaphorical meaning. So if one says to another: 'I gift this to you,' and the addressee takes it, then the speaker claims he intended the gift to be a sale metaphorically and demands payment, his claim is not accepted, because the principle in speech is literal meaning and the literal meaning of gift is ownership without compensation. Unlike if he says: 'I gift it to you for two dinars,' because mentioning two dinars as compensation is a contextual clue that he metaphorically intended the gift as a sale, so it is interpreted accordingly. Likewise, if a waqf founder stipulates 'for my children' (awlad), sons and daughters are included because the word 'walad' literally includes both. Similarly, if he stipulates 'for the children of my children' (awlad awladi), the children of daughters are included in the waqf. If one makes a bequest to the children of so-and-so, and so-and-so has direct offspring and grandchildren, the bequest is directed to the direct offspring—i.e., the first generation of his descendants—because that is the literal meaning of the word 'awlad'.
Source pages p. 945

Commentary
This passage presents two foundational rules of the Hanafi legal methodology: the general maxim 'Giving effect to speech is preferred over neglecting it' (إعمال الكلام أولى من إهماله) and the more specific rule 'The principle in speech is literal meaning' (الأصل في الكلام الحقيقة). The author first defines the general maxim: when a statement can be interpreted in different ways, the interpretation that produces a legal effect must be chosen, because rational speech is not uttered in vain. Several examples illustrate this: multiple confessions or bequests are treated as separate unless clear evidence shows they are the same; divorce pronouncements are counted as separate unless the intent to emphasize is proven by a contextual clue (though in matters of religion, the inner intent may differ). The discussion then moves to the derived rule 'الأصل في الكلام الحقيقة', which mandates that words should be understood in their literal, original meaning unless there is a clear contextual clue (قرينة) indicating metaphorical intent. The author defines 'haqiqa' (literal meaning) and 'majaz' (metaphorical meaning) with examples: 'court' for the building (literal) vs. the judge (metaphorical); 'killing' for taking life vs. painful beating. This rule applies to speech of the Lawgiver, contractors, and oath-takers. The examples given—gift interpreted as gift unless price is mentioned indicating sale metaphor; 'children' including both sons and daughters in waqf; 'children of my children' including grandchildren of both sons and daughters; and bequest to 'children of so-and-so' being limited to direct offspring—demonstrate how the literal meaning is applied unless a clear contextual clue shifts the interpretation. This is a key principle in Hanafi uṣūl al-fiqh, used throughout fiqh to ensure consistency in interpreting legal texts and transactions.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqa): The original meaning for which the word was coined (المعنى الأصلي الذي وضع له اللفظ).
المجاز (al-majaz): A meaning other than the original, used due to a relationship with the literal meaning and a contextual clue.
التأسيس (al-ta'sis): The speech conveys a new benefit not conveyed by previous speech.
التأكيد (al-ta'kid): Emphasis; repeating a statement to confirm the previous one without new meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the rule 'الأصل في الكلام الحقيقة', what is the default interpretation of a person's statement if there is no contextual clue to suggest otherwise?
A) It is interpreted metaphorically.
B) It is interpreted literally.
C) It is considered invalid.
D) It is interpreted based on the speaker's inner intent.

Key Takeaway
Every legal utterance must be given effect, and the starting point for interpretation is its literal meaning unless a clear contextual clue indicates otherwise.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

The Default in Speech is Literal Meaning
Explaining the default literal meaning maxim with furuat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-aṣl fī al-kalām al-ḥaqīqah' to interpret ambiguous statements in contracts, oaths, and waqf, identifying when a qarīnah shifts to metaphorical meaning.

Core Idea
The literal meaning is presumed unless a clear contextual indicator points to metaphor.

Imagine someone says 'I gift you this' and later demands payment—does the law take his claim seriously? The Hanafi school says no, because the literal meaning of 'gift' is free transfer. This lesson unpacks the first great maxim of legal interpretation: words are taken at face value unless there is a clear reason to do otherwise. You will learn to identify when a qarīnah (contextual indicator) changes the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal meaning
Determine the primary, customary meaning of the term as understood in Arabic linguistic custom. For example, 'gift' (hibah) is transfer without compensation; 'child' (walad) includes both sons and daughters.
2
Check for a qarīnah
Look for any verbal, situational, or customary indicator that suggests the speaker intended a metaphorical meaning. For instance, mentioning a price alongside 'gift' indicates sale.
The qarīnah must be clear and specific.
3
Apply the maxim
If no qarīnah exists, enforce the literal meaning. If a qarīnah exists, interpret metaphorically. If the literal meaning is impossible or extremely difficult, resort to metaphor to avoid nullifying the speech.
This sub-maxim is 'إذا تعذرت الحقيقة يُصار إلى المجاز'
4
Test with furū‘
Apply the steps to real cases: gift with price (sale), waqf on children when only grandchildren exist, oath to eat from a tree (fruit). Verify the ruling matches the maxim.

الفصل (81) شرح قواعد المجلة وتصنيفها

فلفظ «المحكمة» مثلاً حقيقة معناه هو مكان الحاكم. فإذا أريد به الحاكم نفسه كما في قولنا حكمت المحكمة، كان مجازاً.
ولفظ «القتل» حقيقة معناه إزهاق الروح. فإذا استعمل بمعنى الضرب الموجع كان مجازاً.

-
لأن المجاز عندئذ يتعيّن طريقاً لإعمال الكلام، واجتناب إهماله :

وكذا إذا وكله بشراء متاع ما فاشتراه له بثمن المثل، فقال الموكل إنما أردت أن تشتريه من السوق الفلانية، أو بثمن أقل. فلا عبرة لقوله وينفذ عليه الشراء، لأن أمره للوكيل مطلق عن القيود فيجري على إطلاقه .
وعقد الإعارة المطلق يسوع المستعير جميع وجوه الانتفاع بالحدود المعتادة، فلا يتقيّد بمكان أو زمان أو طريقة مما ليس عليه دليل .
1012
Chapter (81): Explanation of the maxims of the Majalla and their classification. Between the literal meaning and the speaker there is a relationship that allows the speaker to intend that meaning by that expression, relying on a contextual indicator (qarīnah) that shows that he intended it rather than the metaphorical meaning. For example, the word 'court' (al-mahkamah) literally means the place of the judge. If it is used to mean the judge himself, as in 'the court ruled', it is metaphorical (majāz). The word 'killing' (al-qatl) literally means taking life. If used to mean painful beating, it is metaphorical. The meaning of the maxim is that when interpreting the speech of a speaker—whether a legislator, a contractor, an oath-taker, etc.—his words must be taken according to their literal meanings when there are no contextual indicators that favor intending the metaphorical meaning. So if one says to another, 'I gifted you this thing,' and the recipient takes it, then the speaker says he intended the sale metaphorically by 'gift' and demands a price, his statement is not accepted, because the default in speech is literalism, and the literal meaning of gift is transfer without compensation. Contrast this with if he said, 'I gifted it to you for two dinars,' then mentioning the two dinars as consideration is a contextual indicator that he intended sale metaphorically by 'gift,' so it is interpreted accordingly. Likewise, if a waqf founder stipulates 'for his children' (awlāduh), both sons and daughters are included, because the word 'walad' literally applies to both. Likewise, if he stipulates 'for the children of his children,' the waqf includes the children of daughters. If one makes a bequest for 'the children of so-and-so,' and that person has direct children and grandchildren, the bequest applies only to the direct children—that is, the first generation of his offspring—because that is the literal meaning of the word 'children' (awlād). (49/81 - B) If the literal meaning is impossible, then one turns to the metaphorical (Majalla article 61). Because the metaphorical then becomes the only way to give effect to the speech and to avoid disregarding it: So if one makes a waqf 'for his children' and at the time of the waqf he has only grandchildren, the waqf applies to them metaphorically, because the literal meaning of the word 'children'—direct children—is impossible, and a grandchild is called a 'child' metaphorically, so the word is applied to it, and that is better than disregarding it. Like impossibility is extreme difficulty. For example, one who swears 'I shall eat from this tree.' The literal meaning of eating from it is eating its wood, and that is difficult, so it becomes a contextual indicator for intending the metaphorical meaning, which is eating its fruit, and the meaning is diverted to that. This is also the verbal custom (al-urf al-lafzī) in such oaths, as previously mentioned in the theory of custom. (50/81 - C) An absolute (muṭlaq) term runs on its absoluteness unless there is evidence for restriction (taqyīd), by text or by indication (Majalla article 64). Absoluteness and restriction are attributes of words. The absolute term is that which indicates a thing free from restrictions that impose some attributes or limits. The restricted term is that which is specified by some such restriction. So the word 'horse' (faras) is absolute; 'white horse' (faras bayḍāʾ) is restricted by the attribute of whiteness. The word 'place' (makān) is absolute; 'place of eating' (makān al-akl) is restricted. Cases of absoluteness: If one appoints another to buy a 'horse,' and he buys a red one, and the principal says 'I meant white only,' the purchase is binding on him because his speech is absolute and runs on its absoluteness. Likewise, if he appoints him to buy some goods, and he buys them at the market price, and the principal says 'I meant that you buy from such-and-such market or at a lower price,' his statement is disregarded, and the purchase is binding on him, because his command to the agent is absolute of restrictions and runs on its absoluteness. The absolute contract of loan (iʿārah) permits the borrower all normal modes of benefit, and it is not restricted to a place, time, or method for which there is no evidence.
Source pages p. 946

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm explains two foundational maxims of Islamic legal interpretation that originated from the Majalla. The first maxim (article 61) establishes that when interpreting speech—whether from the Lawgiver (Shar‘), contracting parties, oath-takers, or others—the literal (haqīqī) meaning is presumed unless there is a clear contextual indicator (qarīnah) that shows the speaker intended the metaphorical (majāzī) meaning. The author illustrates this with the examples of 'court' and 'killing' to show how a single word can shift from literal to metaphorical. In legal practice, this means that if someone says 'I gift you this' (hibah) and later claims he meant 'sale' and demands payment, his claim is rejected because the literal meaning of hibah is a transfer without compensation. However, if he says 'I gift you this for two dinars,' the mention of a price is a qarīnah indicating metaphorical sale. Similarly, in waqf and bequests, 'children' (awlād) literally includes both sons and daughters; 'children of children' includes grandchildren via sons and daughters. But if the testator has no direct children, the word is applied metaphorically to grandchildren. The rule also covers extreme difficulty: an oath to 'eat from a tree' cannot be taken literally (eating wood), so it is understood as eating its fruit—applying the sub-maxim 'if literal is impossible (or extremely difficult), resort to metaphorical.' The second maxim (article 64) states that an absolute (muṭlaq) term remains unrestricted unless there is evidence (text or indication) to restrict it. Examples: an agent told to buy a 'horse' (absolute) cannot later object that he meant a white one—any horse suffices. Similarly, an agent told to buy 'goods' (absolute) can buy at market price from any market. In an absolute loan (i‘ārah), the borrower may use the item in all customary ways, unrestricted by time, place, or method unless specified. These maxims reflect the Hanafi reliance on the literal meaning and the principle that words are given effect as far as possible.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqah): The literal, original meaning of a word as established by linguistic custom.
المجاز (al-majāz): The metaphorical meaning of a word, used when a qarīnah indicates that the literal meaning is not intended or is impossible/difficult.
القرينة (al-qarīnah): A contextual indicator (verbal, situational, or customary) that shows the intended meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a person makes a waqf 'for my children' but at the time he has only grandchildren, how is the waqf interpreted?
A) The waqf is invalid because 'children' literally means direct children only.
B) The waqf applies to the grandchildren metaphorically since the literal meaning is impossible.
C) The waqf is suspended until the founder has direct children.
D) The waqf applies only to the grandchildren if they are in need.

Key Takeaway
Default to literal meaning in all speech unless a clear contextual indicator (qarīnah) justifies a metaphorical interpretation; when literal is impossible or extremely difficult, metaphor based on custom is necessary.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
10 min
Absolute Terms Remain Unrestricted Unless Evidence of Restriction Exists
Explaining the absolute/restricted maxim with furuat from agency and loan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-muṭlaq yajrī ‘alā iṭlāqih' to agency, loan, and sales contracts, determining when a term is absolute and when restriction is proven.

Core Idea
An absolute term remains unrestricted unless evidence proves otherwise.

If you tell an agent to buy a 'horse' and he returns with a red one, can you refuse? The Hanafi maxim says no: 'horse' is absolute and covers any color. This lesson explains the second great maxim of interpretation: absoluteness is the default, and restrictions require proof. You will learn to distinguish absolute from restricted terms and apply the rule to practical cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term as mutlaq or muqayyad
Determine if the term has any qualifying attribute, condition, or limit. For example, 'horse' is absolute; 'white horse' is restricted.
The text defines mutlaq as free from restrictions.
2
Apply the maxim
If the term is mutlaq, it runs on its absoluteness. The speaker or principal cannot later impose a restriction unless there is evidence.
Evidence can be text (nass) or indication (dalalah).
3
Check for evidence of restriction
Search for any explicit condition, customary practice, or other qarīnah that would restrict the absolute term. If none, the absolute meaning governs.
4
Test with furū‘
Apply to agency (horse purchase, goods purchase) and loan (i‘ārah). Confirm that the agent's or borrower's actions are binding within the absolute scope.

Chapter (81): Explanation of the maxims of the Majalla and their classification. Likewise, if he stipulates 'for the children of his children,' the waqf includes the children of daughters. If one makes a bequest for 'the children of so-and-so,' and that person has direct children and grandchildren, the bequest applies only to the direct children—that is, the first generation of his offspring—because that is the literal meaning of the word 'children' (awlād). (49/81 - B) If the literal meaning is impossible, then one turns to the metaphorical (Majalla article 61). Because the metaphorical then becomes the only way to give effect to the speech and to avoid disregarding it: So if one makes a waqf 'for his children' and at the time of the waqf he has only grandchildren, the waqf applies to them metaphorically, because the literal meaning of the word 'children'—direct children—is impossible, and a grandchild is called a 'child' metaphorically, so the word is applied to it, and that is better than disregarding it. Like impossibility is extreme difficulty. For example, one who swears 'I shall eat from this tree.' The literal meaning of eating from it is eating its wood, and that is difficult, so it becomes a contextual indicator for intending the metaphorical meaning, which is eating its fruit, and the meaning is diverted to that. This is also the verbal custom (al-urf al-lafzī) in such oaths, as previously mentioned in the theory of custom. (50/81 - C) An absolute (muṭlaq) term runs on its absoluteness unless there is evidence for restriction (taqyīd), by text or by indication (Majalla article 64). Absoluteness and restriction are attributes of words. The absolute term is that which indicates a thing free from restrictions that impose some attributes or limits. The restricted term is that which is specified by some such restriction. So the word 'horse' (faras) is absolute; 'white horse' (faras bayḍāʾ) is restricted by the attribute of whiteness. The word 'place' (makān) is absolute; 'place of eating' (makān al-akl) is restricted. Cases of absoluteness: If one appoints another to buy a 'horse,' and he buys a red one, and the principal says 'I meant white only,' the purchase is binding on him because his speech is absolute and runs on its absoluteness. Likewise, if he appoints him to buy some goods, and he buys them at the market price, and the principal says 'I meant that you buy from such-and-such market or at a lower price,' his statement is disregarded, and the purchase is binding on him, because his command to the agent is absolute of restrictions and runs on its absoluteness. The absolute contract of loan (iʿārah) permits the borrower all normal modes of benefit, and it is not restricted to a place, time, or method for which there is no evidence.
Source pages p. 946

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm explains two foundational maxims of Islamic legal interpretation that originated from the Majalla. The first maxim (article 61) establishes that when interpreting speech—whether from the Lawgiver (Shar‘), contracting parties, oath-takers, or others—the literal (haqīqī) meaning is presumed unless there is a clear contextual indicator (qarīnah) that shows the speaker intended the metaphorical (majāzī) meaning. The author illustrates this with the examples of 'court' and 'killing' to show how a single word can shift from literal to metaphorical. In legal practice, this means that if someone says 'I gift you this' (hibah) and later claims he meant 'sale' and demands payment, his claim is rejected because the literal meaning of hibah is a transfer without compensation. However, if he says 'I gift you this for two dinars,' the mention of a price is a qarīnah indicating metaphorical sale. Similarly, in waqf and bequests, 'children' (awlād) literally includes both sons and daughters; 'children of children' includes grandchildren via sons and daughters. But if the testator has no direct children, the word is applied metaphorically to grandchildren. The rule also covers extreme difficulty: an oath to 'eat from a tree' cannot be taken literally (eating wood), so it is understood as eating its fruit—applying the sub-maxim 'if literal is impossible (or extremely difficult), resort to metaphorical.' The second maxim (article 64) states that an absolute (muṭlaq) term remains unrestricted unless there is evidence (text or indication) to restrict it. Examples: an agent told to buy a 'horse' (absolute) cannot later object that he meant a white one—any horse suffices. Similarly, an agent told to buy 'goods' (absolute) can buy at market price from any market. In an absolute loan (i‘ārah), the borrower may use the item in all customary ways, unrestricted by time, place, or method unless specified. These maxims reflect the Hanafi reliance on the literal meaning and the principle that words are given effect as far as possible.

Key Terms
المطلق (al-muṭlaq): A term that is unrestricted by any attribute, condition, or limit.
المقيد (al-muqayyad): A term that is restricted by an attribute, condition, or limit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a principal tells an agent to buy 'goods' (absolute), and the agent buys at the market price, but the principal claims he meant to buy from a specific market, what is the ruling?
A) The principal's claim is accepted, and the agent must return the goods.
B) The purchase is binding on the principal regardless of his unstated intention.
C) The court will investigate the principal's true intention.
D) The agent is liable if he bought from a different market.

Key Takeaway
An absolute term (mutlaq) is binding in its full scope until evidence (text or indication) proves a restriction; this principle ensures stability in agency, loans, and all contracts.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
10 min
Maxim: No Ijtihad Where There is a Text
Defining a major usul maxim with types of ijtihad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim, define 'nass' in the Hanafi context, distinguish the two types of ijtihad allowed, and apply the maxim to determine when ijtihad is valid or invalid.

Core Idea
The clear text binds the mujtahid; ijtihad is only in understanding the text or extending it by analogy.

This lesson introduces one of the great maxims of Hanafi usul: where a definitive text exists, independent reasoning that contradicts it has no scope. The text defines 'nass' as the Quran, authentic hadith, or established consensus, and clarifies that ijtihad remains necessary for interpreting the text and for deriving rulings on new cases through analogy. This maxim ensures that revelation remains the primary source of law.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the maxim
Recite the maxim in Arabic and English until internalized. It is the core of the lesson.
2
Define 'nass' as used here
Understand that nass includes Quran, authentic hadith, and consensus. In Hanafi usul, nass is qat'i (definitive) in both transmission and meaning.
What is the effect of nass on ijtihad?
3
Distinguish the two types of ijtihad
Identify that ijtihad in understanding the text is always required, while ijtihad via qiyas is only for cases not covered by a text.
Which type of ijtihad is forbidden by the maxim?
4
Apply the maxim to real scenarios
Consider cases where a clear text exists (e.g., prohibition of riba) and test if any ijtihad can change the ruling.
The maxim prevents ijtihad that contradicts the text, not ijtihad that clarifies it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٥٥/٨١ - القاعدة السابعة : لا مسَاغَ للاجتهاد في مورد النص» (م/ ١٤). النص: خطاب الشارع، وهو آيات القرآن والأحاديث النبوية الصحيحة، أي الثابتة . ولكن المراد بالنص هنا ما يشمل الإجماع الثابت بالنقل الصحيح. والاجتهاد بذل الجهد العلمي في استنباط الأحكام الشرعية من أدلتها، وهو نوعان: - اجتهاد الفقهاء في فهم النص التشريعي. واجتهادهم في قياس حكم لا نصَّ فيه على حكم منصوص عليه. أ - فأما الاجتهاد في فهم النص فلا بد منه مع وجود النص لمعرفة
55/81 - The seventh maxim: There is no room for ijtihad where there is a text (M/14). The text: the address of the Lawgiver, which is the verses of the Quran and authentic prophetic hadiths, i.e., established ones. But what is meant by text here includes consensus established by authentic transmission. And ijtihad is the exertion of scholarly effort in deriving legal rulings from their evidences, and it is of two types: - The ijtihad of jurists in understanding the legislative text. And their ijtihad in analogizing a ruling for which there is no text to a textual ruling. A) As for ijtihad in understanding the text, it is indispensable in the presence of the text to know...
Source pages p. 950

Commentary
This passage introduces a foundational maxim in usul al-fiqh: where there is a clear, authoritative text (nass) from the Quran, authentic hadith, or consensus, there is no scope for independent legal reasoning (ijtihad) that contradicts it. Ijtihad is permissible only in two areas: (1) interpreting and understanding the text within its possible meanings, and (2) applying analogy (qiyas) to cases not explicitly covered. The text begins to explain the first type but is cut off. The maxim ensures that rulings are grounded in revelation and that ijtihad does not override clear injunctions.

Key Terms
النص (al-nass): Clear, definitive revelation (Quran/hadith) or established consensus.
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): Exertion of scholarly effort to derive legal rulings from evidence.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning to extend a ruling from an original case to a new case sharing the same cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this maxim, when is ijtihad in deriving a ruling permissible?
A) Only when there is no text at all.
B) Always, even with a clear text.
C) Only in understanding the text or in cases not covered by text.
D) Only for mujtahids of the highest rank.

Key Takeaway
A clear, authentic text prohibits ijtihad that contradicts it, but ijtihad in understanding the text and extending it via analogy remains essential.
